THE RED RAGE.

That “cry of Sappho’s”'—" the. moon has. s t, nud the
Pleiades; it is vi'ilniht, the lime is j/oiiiy l/!, • ml I sleep
alone”— has haunted me ever sit ce you printed it. Tell
us some more about Sappho
and the figure works obediently.
* * » *
‘"■Sappho, the one great woman poet of the
world, who called herself Psap| ha in her own
iEolic dialect, is said to have been at the zenith of
her fame about the year <>lo li.o. Bpt, balancing
all the evi l •nee, Fynes-Clinion, in his I'm i Mel-
Icnici, tak ;s (vie years 011-51)2 B.c. to be tin* period
in which Sappho flourished. < uat she was a
native of Lesbos, an island in the ditgean Sea, is
universally adm -..t • 1.” “Nowhere, writes J.
Addington Spm. >u s, “inanyag •of lareek history,
or in an v art of i Leilas, did toe love of physical
beauty, 'ikn sensibility to radian t scenes of nature,
the consuming fervour of personal feeling, assume
such gram! proportions and receive so illustrious
an expression as they cud in Lesbos. There seems
to have been something pas donate and intense in
the AColian temperament, which made the emo-
tions of the Dorian and the lonian feeble by com-
parison. At first, this passion blossomed into the
most exquisite lyrical poetry that the world has
known : this was the [lower-time of the yEolians,
their brief and brillia t spring. But the fruit it
bore was bitter and rotten. Lesbos, the centre of
Aeolian culture, became a byword for corruption.
The passions which for a moment had flame 1 into
the gorgeousness of Art, burnt their envelope of
words and images, remained a mere furnace of
sen nra ity. The voluptuousness of Aeolian poetry
is not like that of Persian or Arabian art. It is
Greek in its self-restraint, proportion, tact. We
fill’d nothing burdensome in its sweetness. All is
so rhythmically and Sublimely ordered in the
poems of Bapplio that supreme art lends solemnity
and grandeur-to the expression of unmitigated
pas i mi.
* • * *
Beyond the fact that Saw ho lived and loved
and wrote, -nothing is certainly known of her.
There is a tradition that she “ took the Leueadian
leap” and so perished in love of lovely Dhaon.
This Leueadian leap was a leap from a cliff over-
hanging the sea, and the virtue of ending hopeless
love was ascribed to it—if the leaner survived :
frequently se was killed on the rocks below, and
end 'd love and life together. _ Addison gives an
imaginative account of jSapplio’s leap in the Spec-
tator, and adds that “Alceeus, the famous lyric
poet, who had for some time been passionately, in
love with Sappho, arrived at the promontory of
Leucate that very evening in order to take the
leap on her account ; but hearing that Sappho had
been there before him, and that hjjr body could
nowhere be found, lie very generously lamented
her fall, and is said to have written his hundred
and twenty-fifth ode upon that occasion. ’’_ Part
of the cliff is called “Sappho’s Leap” to this day.
★ * * *
Henry Wharton, from whose monograph on
Sappho these facts and quotations are taken,
thinks it necessary to try and whitewash his sub-
ject in the interests of respectability. For Sappho
is traditionally credited with having loved women
rather than men, and sapphism is still a current
term for the habit. But the adage connecting
smoke with fire has some validity ; and, a priori,
the predominance of male elements in Sappho
would, according to local dogma, account for her
preference. And though such a preference be ab-
normal, and rightly to be deprecated in the in-
terests of society, it is wrongly called “ un-
natural ”' from the individual point of view. As
Kra it-Ebing says in Moll’s study of the subject
(still the leading clinical manual), “ tout amide la
verite et de l’humanite apprendra avec satisfaction
que le perverti sexuel est un malheureux, et non
un criminel; qu’il n’est pas un prdfanateur de la
dignite humaine, mais un veritable desherite de
la Nature mariitre, et qu’il ne merite pas plus le
mepris qu’un individu venu au monde avec une
malformation physique. ” So that Respectability
need make no wry face at the worst stories of
Sappho, if only it be scientific enough not to be
stupid.
• * * *
Sappho, says Maximus Tyrius, was “small and
dark ” —une petite brunette —“ est etiam fusco grata
colore venus.”
The small, dark body’s Lesbian loveliness
That held the fire eternal.
-%WINBURNE.
This is likely enough. Sappho was transcendency
vital; and the evidence goes to show that, on the
whole, dark races and individuals possess the
stronger vitality. And. as for littleness: every
distinguished woman-writer has been little. Even
in the small Australian way, six times in ten the
woman who can write a story or a poem rather
more intense than the ruck turns out to be a little
blaekavised creature with a bright eye and her
clothes bundled on anyhow. Four times in ten
she isn’t blaeka vised ; but she never fails to be
little. Her brains have stolen the energy that
makes other women’s bodies grow. Sappho’s
bright eyes are mentioned also ; and somebody
calls her sweet-voiced.
* * * *
The poetry of Sappho was characterised by a
peculiar poignancy of emotion and expression.
Addison says her soul seems to have been made
up of love and poetry. And Addington Symonds
writes- -
The world has suffered no greater literary loss than the
loss of Sappho's poems. So perfect are the smallest
fragments preserved that we muse in a sad rapture of
astonishment to think what, (he cnnijOete poems most
have been. Of all the poets of l.'i • wmJ I. of all the illus-
trious artists of all lit s a tores. Sn ; ho is the one w hose
every word has a peculiar perfume, a sen I of absolute
perfection and illimitable grace, in her nit she was un-
errin<r. Even Archilochus so ms connnon| liH-e when com-
pared with her exquisite rarity of phrase. Whether
addressing the maidens, whom even in Elysium, as
Horace says, .Sappho could not forget; or embodying the
prolotmder yearnings of an intense soul after beauty
which has never on earth existed, but which inflames the
hearts of noblest poets, robbing their eyes of sleep, and
giving them the bitterness of tears to drink—these
dazzling fragments
Which still, like snorkles of Greek fire
Burn on through Time, and ne’er expire,
are the ultimate and finished forms of passionate utter-
ance diamonds, topazes, and blazing rubies, in which
the fire of t he soul is crystallised for ever.
» » * *
And practically all of the works of this incom-
parable poet have been destroyed. There remain
one complete ode—“ To Aphrodite ” ; a consider-
able fragment of an ode to Anactoria, a pupil
and lover of Sappho’s ; and 1(53 smaller frag-
ments —sometimes a verse, sometimes, only a
line or a word—to show how bitter is the loss.
“Sea’iter says that the works of Sappho and
other lyric poets were burnt at Constantinople
and at Rome in the year 1073, in the popedom of
Gregory VII. Cardan says the burning took place
under Gregory Nazianzen, about 380 a.d. And
Petrus Alcyonius relates that he heard when a boy
that- very many of the works of the Greek poets
were burnt by order of the Byzantine emperors,
and the poems of Gregory Nazianzen circulated in
their stead. ‘ Few indeed, but those roses,’ as the
poet Meleager said, are the precious verses the
zeal of anti-paganism has spared to us.” The
fragments now extant occur mostly as quotations
in the writings of ancient grammarians and critics
of. style.
* • » •
The unique thrill of Sappho’s phrase cannot be
preserved in translation ; and the English versions
of the ode to “ Aphrodite” are not inspiring. It is
notable that although the poem refers to a female
lover, the translators (with the exception of Swin-
burne) decorously change the she to fee—-thus mis-
representing the author. The fragment of the ode
to Anactoria is more intense; and Swinburne’s
“ dilution and dilation ” of the spirit of it in his
‘‘ Anactoria ” does not yield as strong an impres-
sion as the brief original. For the rest, Wharton’s
prose versions are generally superior to. the poeti-
cal renderings which he quotes, and the justifica-
tion of the Encyclopaedia Britannica critic’s cavil
at his scholarship is not apparent to the ordinary
reader. ‘ ‘ Sappho’s poems or mele were undoubtedly
written for recitation with the aid of music ;
‘they were, in fact,’ to quote Prof. Mai taffy, ‘the
earliest specimens of what is called in modern days
the Song or Ballad, in which the repetition
of short rhythms produces a certain pleasant
monotony, easy to remember and easy to under-
stand. ’ What Melic poetry like Sappho’s actually
was is best comprehended in the light of Plato’s
definition of melos —that it is ‘compounded out of
three things, speech, music, and rhythm.’ In this
practice and definition there seems to be some-
thing kin with Sidney Lanier’s idea that rhythm
in verse is the same as rhythm in music—an idea
to which he gave such fine shape in ‘ The Marshes
of Glynn, ’ and which he might have succeeded in
firmly establishing had he lived a few years longer.
* * * •
An American poet of some capacity, Maurice
Thompson, has woven several of the notable
fragments of Sappho into verses of his own ; and
though the shape of one or two of the fragments
has been altered, and the body of Thompson’s
piece is generally undistinguished, his result re-
mains ingenious and interesting. He prints the
Sapphic fragments in italics.
TO SAPPHO.
Up from the Caribbean
The wind comes like a paean
As on my fragrant orange-bough I swing,
Dreaming, and wondering,
And piping Sapphic fragments o’er and o’er.
Along the shore
The surf foams madly and the breakers roar
Strange odors from afar,
Spice, amber, nard, and tar.
And Lesbian roses grown in Mitylene,
And violet breath, and waft of myrtle green,
Steep me in visions passionate and wild
Of love, all undefiled,
Whereby was Sappho’s bright
Rose garden of delight,
Flooded with starry splendors of old night.
A bower of J tine,
With morning freshness lingering after noon
Upon the petals of the passion-rose ;
All round through apple boughs the cool air blows,
Shaking soft slumber doivn the dusky leaves
Where still the subtle violet-weaver weaves !
Eden of Love’s own choice.
Haunted of Love’s own voice.
Thy ways I could not take—
Nay, not for Sappho’s sake,
And leave my orange grove
And the fresh promise of the land I lov® I
Liberty
Wild liberty,
Not licence, give to me !
Give me the glory of what is to come—
Fill me with prophecy—-the mighty hum
Of swarming yeah; and wonders yet to be 1
And still, 0 Sa >p,io ! still I turn and sigh
That all but tins' stray notes of all thy songs
should die :
That these chance' fragments old _ _ <
Of things men- golden-than the virgin gold,
To music set bevon 1 all melody,
1 i.ave eu i i to me
Of all thy treasure blown across the sea I
Mad maid of Mitylene,
Song-slave, son ' mistress, an 1 s.»ig-queen:
Blessed the n pitiiigale
Whom thou didst sing
And hail
As tender messenger of Spring !
Sappho, 0 thou
Sweet-apple on the bouuh !
Yen, hi unking on the top spraji of the tree,
Whom nil the. apple-gatherers fa Uni to nee,
Nay , nine, but could not reach, and cannot now ;
Thy son ; perforce will fill my throat
And burn it with each golden, molten note.
Who knows the best
Kn >ws never peace or rest,
And yet his every thought
Is a joy from Heaven caught:
And his heart flower open blows
Like a dark-red L ■ 'ran rose !
God-like he thrones him* , who si ’s by thee,
And feels the dear delirium of thv word*,
And of thy*lau ihter hears the tender chords t
The vision thrills my heart; for if 1 see
In merest glimpse thy form, my voice fails me,
My tongue is numb, with,lire my blood is shot,
My ears hum, and my straining eyes see not;
My frame, bedewed with madness, quivereth
And paler than pale grass, I look like death !
Oh, not in wanton whirls
Thy singing bevy sweet
Danced round thee on charmed feet,
O blessed girds !
Who felt thy song-breath on their shining curls,
And thy fierce flame of longing in their veins !
What delicate, deep, delicious bitterness,
What stress _ _
Of overmastering, sad ecstasy within
Each quivering heart! What glorious din
Of girlish voices and love-smitten lyres !
What incense from what all-consuming fires l
O Sappho ! didst thou sing
This haunting, saddening thing?—
Ever shalt thou lie dead,
No more remembered,
Since no Pierian roses blew for thee ;
Unnoticed’mid the shadow; dim and dread —
Alone in the hereafter shalt thou be.
Didst thou,mean me?
But what care I?
I shall not die,
For Liberty lends to me her deathless wing;
My land is the eternal home of Spring,
Beyond all clouds my purple dome of sky,
The nations hunger for the songs I sing,
Making the New World’s groves with Freedom’s
transports ring!
Naught have I for the tomb,
Blooming, as centuries bloom,
Fairer with each new day.
No petal of my song-flower shall decay!
Not so with thine,
Veined like the lily’s, as the rose’s fine ;
Plucked by the vulgar hand of comedy,
They scent the desert (lust and sweeten all the sea!
Pressed thin between the pages
Of the torn book of ages,
Suggestive of what fragrant thoughts it is
To find a bloom like this :
Last night I spoke with Venus in my dreamt
Ah, in what bower of bliss,
By wliat Pan-haunted stream,
Didst thou the lips of Aphrodite kiss ?
What bitter-sweet
Wild love shook thee from head to feet,
And tore thy breast,
Like a wind that rends the oak-tree on the moun-
tain’s crest?
What garland didst thou wear?
And who was Atthis fair ?
Loved once, loved madly, loved so long ago ?
And oh !
for whom didst thou lie lonesome when the moon
had set,
And the Pleiades were down ?
What purple gown
Didst thou quit wearing when thy love came not ?
And (0 delicate thought!)
For thy lost girlhood crying all in vain,
What strange, wild joys were caught
And tangled in thy pain?
Far in the night,
When the moon sails high and white
In the rich, dark firmament,
And the sea-wind is spent,
And the magnolia’s heavy flowers are hung
Like ghastly death-bells waiting to be rung,
I yearn
In a low, many-toned, deep, sweet nocturne,
To guess
And so express,
The lost notes that, once stricken from thy lyre,
Touched all the world with fiery tenderness,
And filled the air with veins of tender fire ;
But of my longing cometh unto ml
Never the bubbling honey, nor the honey-beet
Nay,
Never tongue may say
What founts of inspiration and delight
Forsook the day, what stars shrank from the night,
When all thy songs,
Torn into shreds and smirched with foulest wrongs,
\.Vere scattered,
Bettered,
Bruised, and so left to lie
In places lone an 1 high,
Dark petals of a hyaoin 'li, odorous still,
Crushed by rude fee '; on m my a windy hill t

WALLACE.
Failing Manhood.
When your manhood begins
to go you will twitch and show
signs of premature weakness.
Following are some of the symp-
toms :
Do your facil nerves twitch ?
Are you all run down ?
Spots before the eyes?
Do the muscles twitch ?
Have you pains in small of back?
Have you pains in head ?
Have you shooting pains?
Do you feel fatigued ?
Are you weary ?
Does your head swim ?
Do your knees knock?
Does your hand tremble?
Nervous Twitchings.
A nervous man easily gets all
of a tremble, gets fits of weak-

Various Versea.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A curious case, in which a plaintiff, aged
12, sued for injuries before birth, was decided by
the Illinois (U.S.A.) Supreme Court in June. His
mother had been taken to a hospital, and was in-
jured in a lift between two of its floors. One side
of her hip and body was injured, and, according
to the documents in the case, the unborn child
was bruised in his left side and left arm. His left
limbs and the left side of his body have been para-
lysed since birth, and there is a serious shortening
of the left leg. He claimed $50,000, and was shot
out of court, but not unanimously, on the ground
that he had no cause of action.
Dream.-Echoes.
I.
I love the Morning Star—
O love pure and tender I
In Dreamland very far
She dwelleth in splendor.
Her bosom hath a spell
For cooling, for healing;
Her beauty is a well
Of mystic revealing.
The flowers on a bier
Have some of her sweetness,
And the leaves that grow sere
In autumn’s winged fleetness.
11.
The sirens are singing
Sweeter than music of lute,
Or harp of mortal stringing,
And the angels are mute.
0 desolate sadness !
O notes elusive and vain!
Ecstacy touched to madness I
Deepness of yearning pain 1
111.
Love that brings the laughing tears
Of an April day
Comes but once in all our years,
Comes but once for aye.
Roses shadow forth fair hue,
Violets sweet breath,
Lilies hint of form so true,
Marigolds of death.
JAMES HEBBLETHWAITH.
The Deep Sea Sings Her Dead.
A band there sleeps in the Sea-King’s hall
Whose couch is a blend of blue,
Of sea-weed pale is the twining pall
That laps them in tendrils true;
0, wondrous deep is that last long sleep,
And wondrous soft the bed,
While to list’ning star and to beach and bar
The Deep Sea sings her Dead.
With Joy, deep Joy, in the Days of Birth,
The young hills rent and rang,
With Joy, deep Joy, in the Loves of Earth
The Sons of Morning sang;
But sad and long is the low, soft song
That lulls each once-loved head,
Where to list’ning star and to beach and bar
The Deep Sea sings her Dead.
From North and South and East and West
She draws her children home,
And well they sleep on the heaving breast
So soft in its billowy foam ;
And none may wrest from her halls her guest,
Or part whom she once hath wed,
And her every surge is a noble dirge
When the Deep Sea sings her Dead.
She sang her song when the Heav’ns were young,
So sweet, so grand, so drear,
Till the Deeps of Space with the music rung
And the Angels bent to hear;
She will sing her song thro’ the Ages long,
When the last salt tears are shed,
From the moaning bar to the fading star
Will the Deep Sea sing her Dead !
Q.
P. LUFTIC.
A Song for the Sea.
lam the Sea ! I hold the land
As one may hold a pebble in hand ;
Hold it fast, with sleepless eyes
Watching the continents sink and rise :
Out of my bosom the mountains grow :
Back to its depth they crumble slow ;
The Barth is a helpless child to me,
I am the Sea l
lam the Sea! the land I sway t
Granite to me is as potter’s clay.
Under the touch of my ceaseless waves
It rises in towers and sinks in caves ;
The iron cliffs that edge the land
I grind to pebbles and sift to sand ;
It is all but a moment of change to me—
Time with me is Eternity.
lam the Sea ! In my bosom deep
Wealth and Wonder and Beauty sleep ;
Wonder and Wealth and Beauty rise
To changing splendor of varied skies ;
I comfort the land with rains and snows
Till waves the harvest and blooms the rose;
Flower and forest and man have breath
With me have life—without me death.
What if the ships go down in me ?
I am the Sea !
J.H.

Special Notice.
The whole contents of this newspaper are copyright by
the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
'Correspondence" column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not extending two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return- all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

SATURDAY, OCTOBER 1, 1898.
The 41 Corporations of Sydney.
The antics of Sydney’s unpaid aldermen are
pleasant diversions for a semi-insolvent city
to witness. Sydney has a debenture debt of
£1,410,000 and a bank overdraft of £123,000.
The total debt equals over £l5 10s. per in-
habitant (the population of the city of
Sydney is estimated at 97,000). The bulk
of this was borrowed in London for loner
terms at high rates—4, 5 and 6 per
The only per cent, loan of the lot was
raised in Sydney. The city has a fine,
healthy deficit of about £36,000 a year, and its
unpaid aldermen are in the main deaf to all
serious proposals of retrenchment. Adustyre-
port which is filed away somewhere amongthe
rats shows ways and means of saving £24)000
a year, and a small increase of rates, and a
more vigorous collection of the rates already
existing would make up the balance, ]but
the dust gathers on the report, and the
aldermen still slumber. When Sydney got
in a very tight place before, it simply per-
suaded the country to take over about
£700,000 of its liabilities and put them
into the general debt, and but for
that relief the city would probably have
filed its municipal schedule long ago.
Apparently a similar clean-up is getting
nearly due again. The other 40 metropoli-
tan municipalities are in yarious stages of
solvency and insolvency, but,- taking°them
altogether, they average about the same
1 1 men table prospect as Sydney itself. Some
of them are better ; some worse ;-bjit the
general result when they are ail added'up is
failure.
* * • •
The only visible remedy worth calling such
is to strike the whole institution fatally on
the head with a club, and build something
completely new in its stead. The first essen-
tial is to put the entire metropolitan district
under one central authority. It may be cut
up into small' constituencies, each returning
its own alderman, and there may be cast-iron
rules about the expenditure within each dis-
trict of its own road-repair money, and all
manner of other precautions, but the need
for one central authority is urgent. The
420,000 people who constitute the metro-
politan population can’t afford to maintain
41 mayors, town-clerks, complete official
staffs, sets of offices, town-halls, and all the
other pomp and majesty that appertains to
the grocery alderman. The 39 different
municipal debts are a grievous waste of
interest. If they were all consolidated with
the security of the whole metropolis behind
them a saving of per cent, interest would be
easily effected, and that alone would mean
£33,000 a year, for the credit of the small
baby municipalities is so low that they mostly
pay between 5 and 7 per cent, for their
accommodation. The auditing of 41 different
sets of accounts, and the publication of the
same, and the publication separately of 41
different sets of municipal notices —notices
of aldermanic meetings, notices about arrears
of rates, about goats and dogs, about elec-
tions, about all manner of things—are all
items of waste. The attempt to run 41 little
Parliaments for 420,000 people is too much
joy in every way. The Sydney Water and
Sewerage Board contrives to do the work, in
its line, of practically the whole county
of Cumberland. In Britain, such small
casual places as Birmingham (501,000 popu-
lation), Bristol (221,000), Leeds (400,000),
Liverpool (629,000)), Manchester (529,000),
and any number more are able to run their
municipal show with one Mayor and corpor-
tion, and, seemingly, do it well enough.
Ihe pocket-handkerchief municipality is
mainly an Australian invention, and a very
bad one.
* * * »
Even supposing that municipal federation
left the alderman as stupid and incapable as
he is now, the saving by the consolidation of
offices atid debts should not be far short of
£BO,OOO per annum. But it need not leave
him as stupid and incapable. It is quite
possible to find one good Mayor where 41
are unobtainable ; New South Wales can
raise a decent Premier now and then, but 41
decent Premiers would be very hard to find
at any time. Moreover, to be Mayor —the
popularly elected Mayor—of Greater Sydney
would be to hold an office almost equal to
the Premiership. The Foreign Secretary
of Great Britain has sat as Chairman of the
London County Council and been proud of
the office, and many of the ablest poli-
ticians in the country have been private mem-
bersof the samebody. TheCoimcil of Greater
Sydney would attract the best financiers and
business men of the city instead of being
filled with small, unbusiness-like potato-
merchants and the like. If to be a member
of it was such a billet that it would be
worth an able and ambitious man’s while to
struggle after it—a billet that might be a
short cut to the Premiership or the Agent-
Generalship—then able men would come
into the Council. And if the Council
managed the business concerns of 500,000
people—nearly half the population of the
province—it would be just that kind of a
billet. Moreover, if the whole Council
went out of office say every three years, and
there was a large, stormy general election,
the matter would probably attract sufficient
attention to make the public take an intelli-
gent and useful interest in its municipal
government and the policy thereof. The
present quiet little annual bye-election
attracts no notice except when Joins Nor-
ton is a candidate, and no one has ever yet
heard of a municipal policy in New South
Wales.
* * * *
The pocket-handkerchief municipality sys-
tem is full of disadvantages, in addition to
the needless salaries and expenses, and debt
interest ; ; d the contempt into which it
brings Mayors and aldermen when there
are 41 sets of them all within eye-range of
Sydney Post Office tower ; and the needless
incapacity of these same individuals arising
from the fact that no city can produce 41 sets
of good administrators at once. There is
not one decently straight thoroughfare of
any length in all the 41 crooked municipali-
ties. There are plenty of places where the
making of one would vastly increase the
value of property, but the consent of seven
or eight corporations is generally necessary
before it is made, and seven or eight corpor-
ations seldom agree about anything. The
private gas monopoly sits tight because 41
corporations can’t arrive at any comprehen-
sive municipal electric-lighting scheme. It
isn’t worth while troubling much about
slums in many cases ; if two or three muni-
cipalities divide a really foul slum among
them it. isn t worth the while of one to start
the purification process by itself. A muni-
cipality-of Greater Sydney could build its
own North Shore bridge and make it pay by
levying moderate tolls ; the straggling 41
can’t for want of union, and so the work
waits for the Central Government, and the
Central Government waits for private enter-
prise, and private enterprise waits to find
out how much the scheme can he floated for
in London. There would never have been
any popularly-owned tramway service if the
Central Government had not taken the
matter in hand and.also annexed the profits,
foi 41 independent corporations are incap-
able of organising any Ihi ng. There ought
to be a cheap municipal ferry-service to all
the waterside suburbs, but it isn’t the busi-
ness of any one Mayor to get the other 40
together and shout for them (40 sixpenny
drinks—£l) while the matter is being dis-
cussed.. All the good work in the way of
municipal socialism that is being done by
such corporations as those of London, Glas-
gow, and Birmingham is impossible under
the regime of the 41 Mayors of Sydney.
• • * •
There is no prospect of any improvement
if Parliament waits for all these corporations
to take the initiative, or even to give a pas-
sive consent. No one could ever persuade
41 Mayors and corporations even to decide
which public-house they would meet in to
discuss so momentous a question. Moreover,
the other 40 Mayors don’t want to abdicate,’
and the other 40 town-clerks are attached to
their billets, and the people who have lent the
small corporation money at 7 per shent. don’t
want the debts consolidated at 3 per cent.,
and the small suburban paper has no desire
to lose the job of printing the dog-and-goat
notices and the municipal balance-sheet. The
short way is for Parliament to strike the
present system on the head with a hard,
business-like shock. And The Bulletin
waits with confidence to hear how Parlia-
ment doesn’t do it.
We—and Land Monopoly.
The Bulletin doesn’t often hear from the
angry Single-Taxer now, therefore “ G.L. W.,”
who writes under the reproachful heading
“You—and Land Monopoly,” is rather an
agreeable change. His plaint—a few non-
essential parts omitted to save space —is as
follows :
Your political views are unsound economically, and
Tory to the last degree.. You advocate Protection. Pro-
tection is undemocratic, and fur several hundred years
was associated in England with Tory landlordism. Every
now and then Conservatives of the Howard Vincent type
or that of the Duke of Itutland, try to get a rehearing for
the poor old played-out .game. In America it is associated
with gruesome and unspeakable monopolists of the Car-
Neoie sort-. In Victoria a good many persons flourished
by Protection at first who were smart enough to grab
what they could before the principle became general—
and imperative. The price paid by the country is that
business is rotten to the core in Melbourne and elsewhere,
as I know to my cost. Nine tenths of the business people
in that depleted province are hopelessly entangled with
those beautiful institutions, the banks, who are enabled
to batten on the people’s industry through the action of
the fundamental error of our social system—land
monopoly. Profiting not by universal experience else-
where, New South Wales may yet give Protection a tem-
porary footing, but it will be by the retrogres-
sion of a single wave—the tide will continue to
rise all the same. The ignorant may be per-
suaded that by paying the taxes levied on indispens-
able commodities (and so protecting the fat fellow’s
pocket) the industries will be localised; and all the
other chestnutty old crammers that have donedutysolong.
But, as surely as you have a bank-account, the country
will get to work before long, with or without your assist-
ance, to put the land-laws in satisfactory order. Federa-
tion may come or stay away, it matters little, so that the
infinitely greater question of land-reform is settled. Fede-
ration will do little towards making Australia a sweet and
wholesome home for whoso is willing to work. And that
is just what land-nationalisation will undoubtedly do.
You have given up all pretence of caring- for restoring the
land to the people. You saw clearly enough that land is
the agent through which all monopolies, at the last
resort, exist. You preached that the owner of land can
impose arty terms or conditions on labor. You traced
villainies and corruptions of all sorts to the baleful system
of allowing private property in land. If these things were
true then, are they not true now ? But you have turned
your coat. You found that the A.M.P. Society would
suffer. If, for instance, a 6d. tax on land-values were im-
posed, the mortgages of that excellent institution would
deteriorate, and the bonuses, and the other fat pickings
would lose some of their rotundity. Also, Protection
would die finally, for want of anybody out of a lunatic-
asylum to take the least notice of it. Come back, before
it is too late.
* * * *
It sets The Bulletin thinking of the bygone
days when the Single-tax crowd sent in letters
like that 18 times a week or so. Those good
old days ! And the letter is just the same old
splash—same whoop—same utter inaccuracy—
same cheerful assumption ! England never had
“ several hundred years of Protection ; ” the
British Protectionist policy lasted about 150
years, and it left Britain the boss manufactur-
ing and commercial nation of the world. In
the dark, semi-barbarous six centuries or so
which followed the Conquest, England went in
mostly for Ereetrade, or something nearly
approaching it, and direct taxation—mostly
land-tax. And there is something unspeakably
lovely in the loud assumption about the “ uni-
versal ” failure of the policy which is followed
by Germany, France, the United States,
Russia, Austria, Queensland, Maoriland, Vic-
toria, South Australia, and any number of other
communities ; and the universal success of the
policy which is backed by England, Ireland,
N.S.W., Turkey, China, Persia, a few bar-
barous nigger States in the African interior,
&c. Also, it is so beautifully easy to say that
the bloated Yankee millionaire is the horrible
product of Protection, and that the bloated
English millionaire isn’t the product of Free-
trade ; and that the policy which is followed
by Maoriland and S.A.—the most distinctly
Democratic provinces in Australasia and well
up among the half-dozen most Democratic com-
munities in the world—is barbaric Toryism,
and that the policy of Turkey and China and
of England in the Dark Ages is enlightened
Democratic sentiment.
* * * *
Curious as it may seem, The Bulletin backs
Protection because it is the democratic policy—
in Australia, anyhow. The fact that the big
landlord in England or in Jupiter may be, or
has been, a Protectionist is nothing to the
point. The big English landlord let out his
land for wheat-growing in a wheat-importing
country, and he wanted Protection to keep
wheat out. The big N.S.W. landlord mostly
grows wool in a wool-exporting country, and
he is mostly Freetrade, or else utterly indiffer-
ent—because there is no foreign wool in his
case to keep out. And the N.S. W. Freetrader
yells with the big landlord in his own country
for the sake of being against the big landlord
at the other end of the world. He ha,s im-
ported his fiscal ideas from a country where
the conditions are upside-down. Metaphoric-
ally-speaking, he keeps a big fire at Xmas
because they need big Xmas fires in England,
and he tries to dig up his potato-harvest in
August because in England potatoes ripen in
August.
*- * *
And The Bulletin, it seems, is not solid on
the Single-tax idea. It doesn’t yell loud enough
and curse with sufficient fury. Perhaps that is
because there are two kinds of democratic
organ, and this is the other kind. The easiest.
way to be a Democrat is to say with a burst of
great flame and blanky that something ought
not to be and that something else ought to be,
and that somebody ought to do it somehow •
to form societies for alleging that something
has got to come about, and to shout generally—
without once showing the way to bring "the
change to pass. It is so much easier to leave
the finding of the way to the other fellow, and
clo the loose pounding of the big drum oneself.
The more honest way is to take the responsi-
bility of showing how the thing is to be done.
The Bulletin tries to do this, and that is why
it can’t blast quite so loud as the people who
accept no such responsibilitjL Taking N.S.W.
for an example, the Single-tax situation is this*
so far as The Bulletin can see. N.S.W. is
an expensive country to govern, because there
is little population, and vast area, and
stupendous debt. Land values are low, because
the country is barren and droughty and thinly-
peopled. To get enough revenue to run the
country on the Single-tax basis it would appar-
ently be necessary to tax private lands to their
full rental value—in other words, to confiscate
all the private land of N.S.W. For if the
State sells a man land worth £5O a year, and
takes ,£l5OO from him in payment, and’then
charges him just the same .£5O as if it had
never got anything from him at all, and yet
keeps the £l5OO, it confiscates his land or his
money (it can be put either way), and no
amount of explanation will get away from the
fact. Yet. the State can’t give hack the money ;
if it borrowed the requisite sum, then the in-
terest on the loan would swallow up the rent
and the Single-Tax ideal would be as far off as
ever.
* * * *
For years past, whenever a Single-taxer who
seemed worthy of discussion has sent along his
anathema about how the State should own all
lands and run the country on the proceeds, this
paper has asked him whether he is prepared to
advocate this huge confiscation in order to
bring the thing about. Or if he proposes any
other way to bring it about. And not one of
them has sent along a definite reply. Not one.
they went right along and blasted in gener-
alities just the same. The Bulletin doesn’t feel
equal to saying, straight out, that the 30,000 or
so small landholders who eke out a hungry sub-
sistence from their little farms should have
almost all their very small wealth taken from
them. The above correspondent’s loose plaint
about the big, rich landholders of Britain
(which is a very long way off) sound eloquent,
but half the N.S.W. landholders are not rich
they are smq.ll and poor and less able to stands
bad shock than almost any other section of the
community. The wrecking of the A.M.T.
Society and others of its kind by the confisca-
tion of their mortgages, doesn’t seem much
when one speaks of these in the aggregate as
great, bloated corporations; but the A.M.P.
really represents the savings of about 130,000
people ; most of them are poor people, and
their interest in the concern is worth, on an
average, about £l2O per head. The partial
smashing of the Savings Bank by the confisca-
tion of its mortgages w'ould also strike a class
which isn t at all wealthy, and the wreckage
of the private banks by the same cause would
swamp the small depositor who keeps a little
hungry shop as well as the rich one and the
shareholder. The rich landholder and insurer
and depositor don’t arouse The Bulletin’s
sympathy much. But it is a cheap and easy
hypocrisy to get over the difficulty by assum-
ing that all the people of these classes are rich.
And as regards the Victorian banks that “bat-
ten ”on the people—they don’t “batten.” They
wanted to batten, but they didn’t manage it.
In the last eight years they have lost among
them just about all they ever made since the
first Victorian bank started. Out of five Mel-
bourne-owned banks, only one now pays its
original owners even a microscopic dividend.
These banks have been a curse to the people—
and to themselves.
* * « *
Of course, there would be a per contra. If
the Single-tax idea prevailed, and the land as
the alleged “sole source of wealth”—which
it isn’t—became also, by this process of
chucking - out, the sole source of revenue,
the big, rich importer (often an agent of a
Britishfirm) who doesn’t own land, would escape
income-tax on his gilded pile. So would the
lawyer, and the pawnbroker, and bill-dis-
counter, and manufacturer, and the publican,
and the jeweller, and the ship-owner, and the
gas-monopolist, and the man whose cash is in
State securities, and the leasehold squatter.
Drink and tobacco would be freed from duty,
and though the small agricultural freeholder
might go down wallop, the man who owned a
seven storey city building erected on leasehold
ground would escape. If it is desirable to
relieve these people from taxation, then let the
Single-taxers say so. But people who write
blasting letters to this office don’t explain.
They simply keep on blasting in general terms.
* * * *
The Bulletin advocates everything which
seems like a definite move in the direction
of repressing the big private landholder
and the big monopolist of other kinds. It
preaches the abolition of State land-sales,
and their replacement by a leasehold sys-
tem ; heavy absentee-tax, to discourage the
worst variety of landlord; and heavy
graduated land-tax so as to break up big estates
and prevent the formation or continuation of a
landholding oligarchy. It also advocates the
steady and compulsory repurchase of large
properties by the State, and the leasing of
them to small holders; the experience of be-
nighted Maoriland, where the undemocratic
policy of Protection is on top, has proved this
possible and profitable. It calls for a State
Land Bank so that, among other ad van i ages,
the foreclosed property will fall to Ihe State
instead of to the private money-lender. And
it advocates building a new Federal capital on
a large area of State land which shall remain
Federal property for ever, so that there shall
be one city to begin with which, with all the
agricultural district that is bound to grow up
around it, will be national property. In most
of these matters—the last especially—the
Single-tax crowd has given little assistance,
yet they are all movements, more or less, in the
direction of Land Nationalisation, and each


one of them is a perfectly practicable move-
ment with experience behind it.
But now and then this paper receives a long
sheet of yell and blither and denunciation
asking why it doesn’t reverence Henry George
more than it does, and be a slavish copy of
him ; and why it doesn’t go the whole instan-
taneous theoretical hog on Land-Nationalisation
and Single-tax. And the sender always wants
to point out that Henry George, who was
brought up among a wholly different set of
conditions, believed in Land-Nationalisation
and Freetr.ule, and wore a big overcoat at
Xmas. So lie holds that nobody can be a true
Democrat unless he also wears a big overcoat
at Xmas just like George. And though it is
in such strongly protective provinces as Maori-
land and S. A. that the most has been done to
repress the private landlord, he says (without
a shadow of proof) that Land Nationalisation
will be the end of Protection. Which is chiefly
lunacy, and the rest ignorance and servile
copying of his idol’s weak points as well as his
strong ones. Land Nationalisation is more
likely to be the end of that abject subjection
to the importer, and that doctrine of the sur-
vival of the cheapest, ■which is called Freetrade.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Grab for the Capital.
The proposal of the Reid Ministry to demand
that the Federal capital shall be in N.S.W.,
and the unofficial demand that it shall. be
located in Sydney, arouses Melbourne Age to
state Victoria’s views on the matter. Victoria
gets so many of its political opinions ready-
made at the Age office— David Syme keeps a
tank-full of opinions on the premises, and the
suitable opinion is handed out to David’s
bleeding country as the occasion arises—that
the above is a short and yet reasonably accurate
way of putting it. The opinion in the present
case is something like this : If N.S.W. demands
that the clause providing that the capital shall
be Federal territory is to be abrogated, and that
Sydney shall be the capital, Victoria will putin
a similar demand for Melbourne and fight the
matter to the bitter end. Then, doubtless,
S.A. will put in its claim for Adelaide and
there will be chaos. If it is demanded that
the capital shall be in any other N.S.W.
town, but that the said capital shall remain
N.S.W. territory, Victoria will kick against
any province having absolute possession of the
Federal city. If it is demanded thafthe capital
shall be anywhere in N.S.W., on territory that
is the property of the Federal Government,
Victoria —or the D. Syme who runs Victoria—
is perfectly agreeable, provided said capital is
as accessible from Melbourne as from Sydney.
This implies that the distances must be about
equal ; that N.S.W. will, if necessary, connect
the nearest Victorian border railway with the
Federal capital, so as to give Melbourne and
Sydney an equal chance at the trade of the
new city; and that N.S.W. will so adjust
freights as to give Melbourne the same chance
as Sydney. Of course, if this sordid bargaining
once begins, S.A. and Queensland —if she is in
it—will put in similar claims, and all the bene-
fit of the wise compromise arrived at by the Con-
vention will be lost. And the Federal railway
problem will raise its head again in the confusion
—in fact, it is bound to do so, anyhow. For, if
N.S.W- got the capital, and located it half-way
between Sydney and Melbourne, either on the
existing line or somewhere else, and it supplied
the Federal city with cheap freights and abun-
dance of railway communication with Sydney,
and no railways or heavy freights on the exist-
ing railways, to Melbourne, undoubtedly it
could seize all the trade of the growing Federal
city as its own exclusive property. If the
railways are not placed under Federal control
the province which gets the capital is almost
certain to do that unless the Federal district
is placed where three provinces meet, and even
that would be only a partial remedy. If the
railways were placed under Federal control it
would make the way quite easy, but so far un-
federal Australia refuses to dream of such a
thing.

Rats.
“ T.” offers his contribution to the eternal sex
problem :
Dear Bulletin.—l wish to say a few words in reply to
“ the Baron ” and Creeve Roe and your other sex-
problem writers, and the burden thereof is mainly
“ Rats ! ” Very large rats and many of them. My own
individual theory about the sex-problem is that there
ain’t any problem. Or, rather, there is no special and
separate sex-problem. There is one big composite ques-
tion which overshadows everything—the question where
everything came from, and where everything is going to,
and what everything is, and why and how it is. But the
alleged sex-problem by itself is no more remarkable than
the water-problem, or the tree-problem, or the problem
why we are hungry just before dinner and thirsty just
before beer. No one ever worries you with long ietters
about these other problems. Why? Well, my theory is
that convention (another problem) has decided that the
alleged s-x-problem isn’t Respectable, therefore the dirty-
minded section of the community likes to write about It
and get paid for its copy at so much a column. The
alleged sex-problem offers facilities for dreary maunder-
ing about the human race with its clothes off. If the
human race didn’t wear clothes (and personally I would
just as soon it didn’t) then it couldn’t be considered with
them taken off, for you can’t take off, even in your
mind, what has never been on. Then I believe that 99
per cent, of the sex-problem maunderers would maunder
no more. Remember, I don’t object to them because of
their mental frowsiness ; their frowsiness is solely their
own frowsiness and doesn’t affect me. But they are
Bores. Not one of the sex-probletn essayists ever starts
by defining his problem or proving that there is a pro-
blem. He merely starts off about “ the sex-problem,” or
sometimes he calls it “ the problem of the relation of the
sexes.” And he never offers any solution, because all of
the problem that comes within his reach solved itself
many thousands or millions of years ago, and has been
solving itself every day since, and will go on solving it-
self just the same even if he drivels in 24 big volumes.
The moist and shapeless infant is the outward symbol of
that solution. No one can tell why there is such a thing
as sex unless he gets back to the Creation (if there was
one), and then he would have also to find out
why there is anything. No one can tell why
there is what is called an affinity between the
sexes unless he can find out what life is and what death
is made of, and many other impossibilities. But, for all
practical purposes, there is no sex-problem, and notone
of the Bores who wail about it in print has ever attempted
to define it —because be can’t. Simply calling it “the
problem of the relation of the sexes” is merely labelling
it with a long name —it is no more an attempt at defining
it than if it were labelled the bunyip, or the bandersnatch,
or the whangdoodle, or the Bagdad-Nebuchadnezzar. If
these people proposed to tackle the mystery of sex and
sexual affinity and procreation in its scientific aspect, and
If they had the million years or so which would probably
be required to arrive at any knowledge worth mentioning
on so tremendous a question—then there is no reason why
they shouldn’t go ahead to some useful purpose. But the
footless worms who crawl poetically to no purpose over
the surface of their alleged subject are a Bore and an
aggravation. They are a fraudulent conspiracy, whose
object is to get paid by some paper for dreaming
about _ somebody mid nodings on. I don’t object
to their dreams, or to the ideal mid nodings on, or to
anything except the stupid, purposeless monotony of the
whole soul-blasting outfit. And I rise up to ask these
people : “ What is the sex-problem ? What wants doing
to it? And what do they propose to do to it?” This
world is pretty old and grey by now, and for goodness
knows how many thousand ages it went right along and
solved its sex-problem every day of its life, apparently
without knowing that there was one. The legal status of
the husband and the wife and the shapeless, moist infant
varied, and will keep on varying, but the problem settled
itself every time. All the morbid little mites that chatter
and canter round it, and get paid for their hazy remarks
about it, make not the slightest difference in the result.
Some of them are half-brother to the indecent Ger-
man photograph and the seduction-case ; personally I
neither care for nor object much to either of these things,
but if I were the indecent German photograph I would
like to kick a lot of these relations of mine for their fee-
bleness and haziness and utter incompetence. About
the most enthusiastic sex-problem writer that I am
personally acquainted with is a bottomless mine of blue
stories, and has twice run away with another fellow’s
wife. I don’t object to that, either, but both the wives
were ugly and commonplace, and one of them was a very
tall, thick woman, who perspired dreadfully in the sum-
mer, and that is unpardonable. Most of the other pro-
blem-writers of my acquaintance are bards or alleged
artists, or people with a carefullj’-aequired wildness in
the hair, who have got into a dreadful habit of talking
about the “wood” in Greek art. I don’t in the least
object to the “ nood ’’ in art—in fact, I object to very
few things, except the dull things that send me to sleep,
and the loud things that wake me again before I am ready
—but, then, these people, so far as I know them, mostly
don’t know art from Gomorrah. “ The Baron,” who holds
forth in a recent Bulletin, is a very fine example of the
sex-maunderer. He says, among other tangled moans,
that “ we have lost the balance and dignity of the
ancients in our conception of the sex-relationship.” How
is that for high ? The ancients—well, it is probable that
the mere catalogue of the names of their old. nations
would fill the Encyclopedia Britannica with small print.
And we have fragmentary records of some dozen or two
who brought up the tail of the procession—the last dozen
or so of sand-grains on a sea-shore whose other end is out
of sight. And by reason of these scraps “ the Baron ”
goes on as above. Hats 1
In the Lean Man’s Interests.
Brisbane (Q.) dailies are beautifully unanimous
in agreeing that the proposed abolition of the
attachment of wages for debt was a wrong
thing, as it would “ injure deserving people
who would otherwise obtain credit.” What a
good old wlieeze it is, and how the Fat Man is
worried—not for fear he may lose one of his
means of dragging his pound of flesh out of the
Lean Man, but lest the abolition of the dragging
process will injure the Lean Man ! There was
a time when, if the Lean Man didn’t pay up,
his wife and. children and, if necessary, himself,
were realised upon in the slave-market, and
when that was abolished no doubt the Fat Man
was much worried lest traders, no longer having
this security, would stop the Lean Man’s credit.
When life-imprisonment for debt was abolished
he was also deeply grieved and concerned lest
the debtor’s interests should suffer thereby. In
the old English days the man who didn’t pay
could be gaoled until he did (for his natural
life if need be), and as he didn’t like being
gaoled for life, he naturally did his best to pay
(if he didn’t bolt or cut his throat), and
traders, because they knew that he had this
inducement to pay, gave him credit. That was
the theory, anyhow. Crave Fat Men were
quite worried to death lest the abolition of the
life-imprisonment of the Lean Man should pre-
vent him getting credit and thus do him irre-
parable injury, and they reckoned that a
serious wrong would be done to their Lean
Brother if he lost the blessed privilege of being
gaoled for the rest of his days over a small debt.
Many Fat Gentlemen sacrificed their natural
rest through mourning over this matter, and
didn’t recover for quite a long while. Accord-
ing to their own arguments, the Brisbane dailies
should howl for a return to these good old
principles. The same logic which calls for the
attachment of wages for debt calls in quite as
loud a roar for the re-establishment of slavery,
or life-imprisonment for debt, or for the cruci-
fixion of the defaulting debtor. If “deserving
people” can get credit when they otherwise
wouldn’t because the creditor knows he lias a
remedy to fall hack on in the shape of attach-
ment of wages, then it is equally certain that
“deserving people” would get a lot more
credit if there was a bigger remedy to
fall back upon. If the remedy (say cruci-
fixion) were only made certain and horrible
enough to furnish absolute assurance that the
debtor would rather swim in blood than not
pay, a deserving person could get pretty near
all the credit he cared to ask for. And, if it is
a desirable thing that the deserving person
should get credit, which the Fat-Man argument
takes for granted, then the fact that Fat Man
should fix some such horror as this on the Lean
Man (strictly in the Lean Man’s interests, you
know), becomes as conspicuous as Cheops’
pyramid. The Bulletin’s idea is that the
less credit the deserving person gets, and the
less remedy his debtor has against him, the
better it is in the end for both parties.
Sydney D.T., in a recent leader entitled “Free
Education,” says : “A sentimental idea has got
abroad . . . that the practice ” (of payment of
school fees) “creates two classes in the public
schools of N.S. W.—the payers and the paupers.
Carried away by this sentimental idea, the
House decided by its resolution to put all the
children into the pauper class.” As a slab of
sheer imbecility, that ought to break all records.
The people will pay the cost of education
through the general taxation, and, because they
all get what they all pay for, they will all be
paupers. Free education (so-called) means that
there will be no pauper children in the public
schools ; when every child receives the benefit
of what his father pays for, pauperism can’t
come in anywhere. By the Telegraph’s pro-
cess of logic, the people are also pauperised by
being supplied with a gratis police force,
defence force, and judiciary ; by getting their
gaols and M’s.P. for nothing; by having the
interest on their public debt paid for
them by some beneficent outside source,
and by getting all their public works
absolutely free of payment. None of these
things are paid for by a direct levy of 3d.
a week per head, after the fashion of the school-
fees. And if the abolition of the direct levy of
3d. a week and the charging of the whole edu-
cation bill to the general revenue is pauperism,
then they are all pauperism. The miserable
3d., by the way, doesn’t nearly pay the cost of
State education in N.S.W., so by the Tele-
graph’s logic everybody is a pauper, anyhow.
But the difference between the humiliated
alleged pauper child who hasn’t the 3d., because
its father is out of work or for some other cause,
and the dignified partial pauper who has the
3d., involves an amount of public insult
and degradation that six threepennies wouldn’t
compensate for. And the pauperised newspaper
which is carried all over the country at the
expense of the taxpayer —the paper which is
spoon-fed by the State, and which lives on
charity all its days, and which defends the
pauper-newspaper system with all its might —
looks remarkably low and mean when it gets on
a racket like this. To educate the children of
all the people at the expense of all the people
isn’t pauperism—it is merely a matter of every-
body paying for himself. To subsidise the
business of a few newspaper owners at the
expense of all the people is pauperism—and a
pretty bad variety, too.
The Executive of Vic. Miners’ Association has
adopted a Bulletin suggestion re the Vic-
torian Accident Relief Fund, which was founded
to succor the sufferers by a Creswick mine dis-
aster, and has decided “ to urge the Minister of
Mines to deal with the fund so as to form the
nucleus of a national fund for the relief of suf-
ferers through accidents in connection with
mining in this province.” Unfortunately, the
Vic. Relief Fund, which is controlled by a
mixed body of trustees, consisting mostly of
Mayors “ for time being,” is itself in difficulties.
Years ago .£20,000 was advanced to a tobacco
firm on a Queen-st. property ; and the mort-
gagor now seeks to be relieved, offering the
property and £lOOO. It is estimated that the
loss would amount to about <£ll,ooo if this offer
were accepted and the property were at once
realised. Yet, even if this gloomy estimate
proves right, there remains something to
start with ; for the demands on the fund on
account of the Creswick accident have now
fallen away to very small proportions.
Up to last week, it was commonly supposed
that a Victorian Leg. Councillor coukl do no
legal wrong in his own country. The result of
the Wallace v. Wallace case proves that a
member of the Hupper ’Ouse can do wrong, but
only on the distinct understanding that he
thought he was doing right all the time.
Although John A. Wallace, M.L.C., was
ruled unfit to retain the trusteeship of the
Monahan estate, he left the court with the
most unstained character on record. Bench
and Bar asserted the defendant’s honesty ad
nauseam. The court adjourned for two or
three hours, in order that defendant’s counsel
might interview defendant as to whether he
would prefer to remove himself altogether from
the trusteeship, or partly remove himself, or
leave it to the Judge to decide. Under the
circumstances, nothing was more surprising
than the trustee’s compulsory retirement from
the billet. Chief-Justice Madden’s previous
utterances had led everybody to suppose
that arrogant old Wallace would be per-
mitted to keep a finger in the Monahan pie.
For a long time the learned Judge “saw nothing
to justify the removal of a trustee.” His
manner of addressing Wallace, M.L.C., was
almost obsequious. He allowed the witness to
carry on as though the Court were his private
property, to bullyrag the counsel who sought
information from him, and to make a holy show
of the Law’s partiality where legislative fatmen
are concerned. Judge Madden (there can be no
doubt about it) was ridiculously kind to old
man Wallace, and very slow to realise the
necessity for dealing with him as any Judge
would have dealt with any smaller trustee of
equally proved incompetence. Hence the belief
that Judge Madden took judicial advice on the
subject, and didn’t arrive at the determination
to remove the Monahan estate from Wallace’s
hands until he had been persuaded that the act
of sacrilege was inevitable.

Melbourne people— i.e., the few who try to
understand what’s the matter—are getting very
tired of Judge Williams’ “severe reflections”
on his brethren of the local County Court.
Judge Molesworth seems to be the principal
object for attack. There is an old-established
legal gag to the effect that Molesworth gives
wrong reasons for arriving at right conclusions.
According to Supreme Court jargon, the
estimable Molesworth is weak in legal expres-
sion but strong in judgment, and the fact that
he is therefore a Judge after the public’s own
heart probably accounts for his unpopularity
with two or three of his—haw !—superior
officers. As the late Arthur Orton would
have summed up the case: Some Judges has
plenty law, but no common sense, and others
has plenty common sense but no law.
Molesworth is one of the others. When his
County Court rulings have been overruled by
the Chief Justice (as happened in two or three
notorious instances), or by any other Supreme
Court luminary, the opinion of the public has
usually been unfavorable to the overruling. In
the instance which provided ground for the
latest attack on Molesworth’s legal character,
it happened that his decision was upheld. It
would be better, perhaps, for the level-headed
Molesworth to condense his wise judgments
into the smallest possible word-space, leaving
the argumentative Supreme Courters to show
orthodox cause and reason why their law is
generally regarded as a hass.

Labor member Thomas, having wearied of
waiting for that comprehensive Local Govern-
ment Bill which has been the bunch of carrots
before N.S.W. Parliamentary ass Tor 25 years
or more, has introduced a little amending
measure providing for one-man-one-vote at
municipal elections. Considering what very
poor results have been achieved under the old
principle of some-men-many-votes, this experi-
ment seems worth trying. But if the Bill is
pushed on it will open up a curious political
complication. Premier Reid’s last Local
Government Bill (the abortive thing that
pegged out wearily three years ago and was
never revived) allowed the Fat Man about 16
votes at a municipal election. On the motion
of Reid’s allies, the Labor-party, assisted by
some Opposition votes, the Fat Man’s
minimum was reduced to four votes. There-
upon Premier Reid decided rather to sacri-
fice the Local Government Bill than the
Fat Man, and withdrew the measure altogether.
Since then he has persistently alleged that it
was rejected (whereas it was never rejected at
all) by the Opposition (whereas the amendment
at which he kicked really came from his own
camp). And, after flatly refusing the Labor-
party’s demand for a four-vote minimum, Reid
now finds the same party asking for a minimum
of one vote. But now the Labor-party holds
the balance so securely that it could fire Reid
out on the sole of his head by a big majority
at any moment—while three years ago it
couldn’t. So what will happen ?
Of <£74,231 2s. 2d. set down as Tas. public*
works proposals for 1899, only ,£7190 is appor-
tioned to railway items. Roads, bridges,
buildings, and other non-productive works, take
the bulk of the money. Two-thirds of Tas.
territory—nearly all highly mineral-bearing—
is covered by impenetrable “ horizontal” scrub,
yet a mean little £B5OO is all the Government
of Go-Slow can spare for cutting tracks. But
while the said Government can only put up
>£7190 for State railways, it proposes to give to
the Great Midland and West Coast Railway
land-grants and other concessions, which the
Government’s own railway-manager reports
will bring in more than ,£200,000 a year, on
condition that it spends £200,000 in building 60
miles of railway, and keeps the railway, and
charges any freights and fares it darned well
pleases ! Obviously if the Braddon Fishy
Government built that line itself, and threw in
all these privileges along with it, it could make
that glorious 100 per cent, for the country.
Think of the awful incapacity or dishonesty of
a Government and a Parliament which throws
away a chance like that !
John Haynes, M.L.A. (N.S.W.), w'ants it pro-
vided, by way of his little amendment, that if
the Federal House of Representatives twice
passes a Bill which the Senate rejects, the
House of Reps, may then pass it “ on its own”
without any reference to the Senate. The
Federal Senate will be elected on the same
liberal suffrage as the other House, and paid
like it. And it will probably contain the best
statesmen, for the Representatives are to be
chosen on the constituency plan, and all the
mean little pumpkin-and-culvert men will be
there, while each province will elect its Senators
as a whole. The Senator will be the man who
can get the vote of all N.S.W. or Victoria; the
Rep. will be the man who can get the vote of his
own little hole and corner. But it is necessary
for the Democrat to be against such an Upper
House as N.S.W. or Vic. possesses; and some
of the bogus specimens of him, having learned
their creed parrot-wise, propose to be
against the Democratic Upper House of the
Commonwealth just the same. Haynes
actually wants also to deprive the paid
and manhood-suffrage-elected Senate of all
power of initiating new legislation, and re-
serve that authority solely for the Federal
House of Culverts. And he asks, on top of all,
that amendments of the Federal Constitution
should be treated just like any other form of
legislation—therefore, after twc rejections, the
House which represents the small constituencies
only could, by its own sole act, abolish the
Democratic House which represents whole
States. The aldermen to have power to abolish
the statesmen ! The representative of Boggabri
to have power to abolish the representative of
N.S.W. !! The irony of it 1 !

“Homeo” dogmatises on an interesting sub-
ject :
“ Old children ” and their characteristics are wrongly
conceived by “ Vilain,” in his criticism of Sept. 3rd. That
the children of aged parents “should never by any
chance be of sound mind,” is contrary to all experience.
Some of our best men and women have been the children
of aged parents. A score of instances may be cited to
encumber the columns of The Bulletin. “Vilain” says
that “ the old child is often clever, and sometimes
healthy, but never by any chance mens sana.” A little
reading and some personal observation might tell him
more. He would then learn that the child of young
parents, “the young child,” so to call it, is endowed with
more muscular and less nervous power than the child of
old parents. The latter is a nervous and timid being com-
pared with the former. But, none the less, the child of old
parents has a “ vitality” not always possessed by the others.
“Vitality” does not mean muscular strength in this
case. It means that power which pulls the patient
through fevers and afflictions which kill the merelv-mus-
cular being. The child of aged parents lives to be aged
also. The child of young parents becomes an athlete, a
prizefighter, a soldier and sailor, and a navigator ; a
hewer of wood and worker in mines—an animal sort of
man in strength and way of using it, and one, also, who
dies off early, as too many do of our athletes. The ner-
vously-built child —the “ old child ” —goes in for occupa-
tions of less-arduous kind so far as muscular strength is
concerned—occupations of the head more than of the
hand. More human than animal is “ the old child,” but
for that reason all the more sound in mind. “ Vilain ”
will find that the tenants of our lunatic-asylums are
mostly those whose bodily strength has proved too power-
ful for their brains. They are rarely the children of old
parents.

“ Nauticus ” writes on an important Labor
question :
Dear Bulletin.— Some of the Australian steamship cos.
have been in the habit, during recent years when the
carrying trade in European waters was slack, of engaging
large cargo-carriers of the common tramp class at very
moderate rates, just enough to pay the owner better than
laying; up to rust and rot. These ships sign-on a
crew for, perhaps, three years in a British or Continental
port, latter for preference, as the low wages are still
lower on the Continent. On arrival on the Australian
coast the ship and crew is handed over holus-bolus to the
local co. and proceeds to work in opposition to the Aus-
tralian-owned boats whose crews are signed-on under the
local agreement at double the rate of wages. A local
man with coasting exemptions is put in charge, and the
vessel pays no pilotage dues.
Apparenrl.v there is no clause in the Local Navigation
Acts compelling vessels trading on the coast for a period
of six months and upwards to sign-on crews at local
wages, as there should be in justice to crews of both
classes of ships.
Boards are appointed to fix rates of wages and minimise
sweating as far as possible in other trades, but here,
through some legal flaw, what is practically sweating
(because to compel a man to do coast work at deep-water
scale of wages is nothing else) is allowed to go on not
only unchecked but under the sanction and approval of
the law.
Insert a clause in the Navigation Act of each province
that no vessel shall trade interpro.vincially unless her
agreement is a, local one and the wages current on the
coast are paid to the crew, and all such cases will drop
instanter. What M’s.P. will take up the case?

.Bland Holt’s newest drama, “ Sporting
Bif e i ” leaves behind it a pleasant recollection of
splendid scenery, careful finish, gas, glitter and
rich coloring, as all B. Holt’s dramas do. From
a literary point of view, however, it is below the
usual B. Holt standard. As in most sporting
dramas, the noble but ruined Baker has planked
the last cent of his own money, besides all he can
borrow from the benevolent Jew, on a horse, and
that horse carries lOst. of the bad man’s curses on
his back as he goes out, and wins the Derby in a
canter. If he didn’t win, the ruined character
couldn’t marry his best girl; as it is, he is able to
buy back the ancestral ’all, and be happy. The
story is complicated, as usual, by the fact that a
remorseless enemy has staked £50,000 against the
horse, and illegally sets fire to the animal’s tail the
night before the race. As a further complication
the enemy wants to marry Baker’s girl, and his
own wife (he is going to get rid of her in some way
not explained in order to marry Baker’s girl) was
formerly Baker’s other girl, and still wants to
marry Baker or rum him—she doesn’t care which
And Baker s girl’s young brother is madly in love
with Baker s other girl, and that siren gets him
to forge Baker s name to a £6OOO bill, which she
discounts with the benevolent Jew (one reason
why Baker’s last cent is on the horse is that he
has sold the ancestral ’all to take up the bill and
save the misguided youth). Also, Baker’s girl’s
sister has been seduced by the prize-fighter on
whom Baker has laid his shirt ; and her father,
nearing of it from the enemy, hocusses the de-
cmver; consequently Baker has to get into the
i mg in and personally plug the opponent.
On top of all these troubles the young
man af orem entioned, hearing at the keyhole that the
siren never loved him after all, steps in and
strangles that lady. Naturally the police get a
warrant for Baker’s arrest. The reason they can’t
find him is that he has gone off in haste to save
his horse from being set on fire the night before
the race, a policeman having warned him outside a
pubhc-house, and has been kidnapped by bad
characters. And next day everything clears up
with a bang, for the missing gentleman is found
sitting straight up in a van for no particular reason.
His horse wins. Nobody says anything more about
his intended arrest. Nobody says anything more
about the young man who did the murder. The
dead woman remains on the floor, and nobody
says anything more about her either. And by this
tune Baker’s girl and Baker’s other girl are so
mixed up that it is best to drop them altogether.
I hey are hereby dropped.
* . * *
The benevolent Jew of Bland Holt is about
the most finished character in a performance that
is excellent throughout, though the queenly-look-
ing Miss Frances Ross comes close up in popular
estimation. Miss. Watson’s siren, too, is above
that lady s usual level ; she is a deceiver whose
white teeth gleam like a danger-signal above a
sunken rock, and as a cleaner-out of the affluent
dude she is highlyimpressive. Arthur Styan, late of
the Cartwright co. takes Cosgrove’s old place as the
affable hawk, and does it well on the whole, though
he doesn t put much strength into the part. Seve-
ral or eight other people—Mrs. Holt, Miss Fitz-
maunce Bill, Baker, Albert Norman and Charles
Brown, but principally Brown, whose moist and
unctuous P°( lceman . is a cop of great tonnage—are
hereby dismissed with three well-deserved cheers
A willing go between two real pugilists, M’Gowan
and Iweedie, who biff each other in a manner
worthy of the old Sydney nose-blood days, is an
episode which will probably fill the Royal for
many weeks to come. And, barring the curious
break-down of the tale towards the end, there isn’t
much to complain of after all.
** * *
The “ Southern Cross,” partly by the Rev.
Geo. Walters, of “Joseph of Canaan,” was pro-
duced at Sydney Lyceum on Saturday. Australia
bears up reasonably well beneath the shock, and
hopes to recover under careful treatment, provided
it stays in bed and has a mustard-plaster put on
its back. It is a pretty bad shock all the same.
Ihe drama is called the “Southern Cross ” for no
particular reason, except that the crude Austra-
lian author always wants to arrest that scrappy
constellation and bring it into his tale under strict
custody. It would be just as appropriate for
J lnero to call his next comedy the “ Great Bear ”
or the Pole Star,” for the poor old Cross has no
more relation to the show than the egg, or the
Lmperor of Germany, or the elephant. It is a
cheap horse and bushranger Deadwood Dick, and
the scene is laid somewhere out west or north or
south, on the mountainous plains where the wild
billy and the kangaroo-tail soup have their lair
1 he exact address is in the Northern Fijerritory
of New South Queen victoria or thereabouts. Par-
son Walters’ hero is a rich squatter with a
gorgeous city home beside Sydney Harbor, and
Ins gruff and brutal manager arrives there in
evening-dress and insultingly presents the lower
part of his coat-tail to his boss’s friends—having
come all the way from Out Back to ask if he will
sell a horse. Obviously it must have cost him six
times the horse in travelling: expenses. The
squatter, through the machinations of a hidden
enemy, presumably the manager, is constantly
being arrested on bogus charges of horse-steal-
ing. Rev. Walters possibly thinks that a rich
squatter, instead of being a great howling deity
who sits on the bench and gives the population six
months in a perfectly off-hand manner, is only a
small wreck with one cow whom the police run in
pretty much as they please.
* * • *
The squatter, being arrested as usual oil
suspicion of a horse, gets so mad that he breaks
away and turns bushranger, and three ridiculous
’rangers (who can’t ride to any great extent) stick
up a ridiculous little bank in a town that is built
of logs in the American fashion, and steal one
small bag of money without resistance. The
manager takes the squatter’s Wife on as house-
keeper (what has become of the station and the
Darling Point mansion doesn’t appear) and has
designs on her virtue. The girl that the manager
seduced disappears, and the manager explains, on
his own undressed assertion, that she has been led
astray by the late boss, now bushranger. In the
end, the girl comes back and explains, and the
late boss comes back and explains also, and
gives up ’ranging. The original mystery about a
wealthy pastoralist being driven to crime by sus-
picions regarding a horse is left just where it
originally was. There are 14 short scenes and 13
of them end ineffectively. The dialogue is pain-
fully ordinary. The manager (Diver) is beastly
uncivil to his boss, and hauls his boss’s wife
about m a fashion which should get him the per-
emptory sack. Among a long list of characters
Miss Julie Woodville, who looks very pretty and
dresses gracefully, and plays the thin part of the
girl who went astray admirably throughout, leaves
a solitary footprint on this paper’s memory. And
the rest is a shadowy impression of a persecuted
squatter being yanked across endless plains of
dust and saltbush on baseless suspicions about a
certain or uncertain animal —which the present
writer doesn’t believe there ever was any such
animal at all.
** * *
A further intelligent consideration of “La
Poup4e ” at Her Majesty’s half-inclines this paper
to the idea that that little opera is getting better
as it goes along. Regarding the Doll there was
never any possible doubt, but some of the other
characters seem to have settled down better in
their places, and to hustle the little streak of
humor along to better advantage. At all events,
the Doll waves her wooden joints seven times a
week to profitable business.
s ** * *
Miss Ada, Reeve is due to-morrow (Friday)
at Sydney Tivoli, and as Juleene’s Electric Organ
has also arrived there and takes up a good deal of
space on the programme, and Biondi has got a
new sketch, which is quite as remarkable as the
old one, someone has to be sacrificed to make
room. Therefore, conjuror Winton’s last week in
Sydney is announced, and as Winton has been
there a long tune, and has probably used up most
of his wonders, it is just possible that his loss will
not be irreparable. The G-ovell sisters also depart
this week.

Mr. John Cosgrove, for a very long time
of Bland Holt’s co., opens at Sydney Criterion on
Saturday with Scudamore’s drama, the 1 ‘ Dangers
of London.” Said literary effort is new to Aus-
tralia, and is a capacious spectacle of great possi-
binties. Drama at the Criterion is rather an un-
usual departure, but that pretty little house has
taken _ turns at opera, comedy, mesmerism and
trained horse, so there seems no reason why the
spectacular play should not boom there also. Pro-
fessor Kennedy, whose mesmeric show was lately
m possession, has moved on. The Bulletin’s Syd-
ney representative hasn’t taken the trouble to in-
quire where he has moved to, but if he has moved
to the only place that his kind of show ought to
move to, the_ said representative trusts, by leading
a spotless existence for the rest of his days, not to
meet him there.
