Sydney Palace Theatre, the most beautiful
house of amusement in Australia, is to re-open
after a very long closure, on Saturday, October 8!
Dante, a conjuror who is alleged by experts to “lay
over even the renowned Carl Hertz, is to be the
attraction, and the able and popular Louis Lohr
will be m charge.
** * *
The almost-forgotten Alhambra, down on
Brickfield Hill, Sydney, re-opens on Saturday
under the management of W. J. Wilson and St.
John. Also, the Mammoth Minstrels have con-
trived to keep the luckless Opera House open for
several weeks; so that the show business is rasing*
with unusual violence in Sydney just now.
** # *
‘Under the Red Robe,” introduced to
Australia, on Saturday, at Melb. Princess’s, is an-
other adaptation from a novel. The first-night
reception of such plays as “Red Robe,” “Little
Minister, and ‘Zenda” throws little light on
their prospects for a first-night audience mostly
knows the novel, whereas the people coming after
being less acquainted with the original yarn will
be less prone to comparative criticism, and also
less easily interested. To those who have already
met Stanley Weyman’s characters in print
Edward Roses drama explains itself. Thev
know what sort of person the hero, Gil
de Berault, is meant to be a dare-devil
gambler, no longer young, who feels, for the
first time in his loose career, the influence of a
noble, gilt-edged woman, and is carried by roman-
tic circumstances from one chivalrous deed to
another. Circumstances press him into the posi-
tion of a spy in a house where truth and hohor
reign, and feminine graces soothe the savage
breast. The spy goes to capture the rebel brother
of an adorable girl, but remains to befriend him.
Love transforms the “hard-case” into a self-
sacrificer, and the maid and the worldling, s 6
strangely brought together, are left to live happily
ever afterwards as in the novel.
* * * *
Stanley Weymail’s plot, laid in France at
the time When Cardinal Richelieu ruled the king,
is mainly an equestrian plot. It covers a lot of
open ground. In the play there are no horseback
journeyihgs, and things get pulled together rather
violently and clumsily between the four walls of
the Chateau de Cocheforet. Gil de Berault (he
has character points in common with our old friend
Don Caesar de Bazan) cuts a meaner figure than
the hero of the novel, although he is not subjected
to so many humiliations. For instance, he steals
from a cupboard the sachet containing diamonds
(which in the novel, he casually picks up from the
street), and hayingburgled the sachet he restores it
almost immediately in an unsatisfactory way. He is
not given sufficient time for mental struggles. He
loves the girl in too much of a hurry, all things
considered; likewise, the girl’s fondness for him
is unduly sudden. The plot of the play will seem
forced to most people, and, but for the spectacular
dignity of Gordon’s scenery and the general
fixings, there would be little hope for “Under the
Red Robe.” In London it appealed to a wide
circle of Stanley Weyman’s readers. The Melb.
circle is small. But Melb. is always kindly dis-
posed towards a handsome show, and the dresses
will count as an attraction. None so rich and
genuine-looking have been seen in any previous
drama hereabouts. If purple and fine linen can
make a new and gloomy entertainment go, this
will go for some weeks.
• * * •
In the present writer’s opinion, “Under
the Red Robe ”is very faultily acted. There are
fine parts, and fine clothes, but the performance
is never fine. Julius Knight, after a spirited
duel with Richard Stewart in the first scene,
declines into a heavy, monotonous hero of bow-
legged appearance. Velvet “smalls,” big boots,
and a hanging wig don’t suit the ex-Roman
Prefect; he wants more inches as well as a larger
assortment of moods and tones. Miss Ferrar
carries her beauteous gowns effectively, but she by
no means rises to the full height of her opportu-
nities, and sometimes her indignant bend of the
back verges on the ludicrous. Mrs. Maesmore
Morris is valuable as a lovely, shrinking wife in
the background, but when she is put into the fore-
ground of a tragic situation the play loses some
natural force. As for Mrs. M. ’s husband, whose
concealment, capture, and release are matters of
chief importance, he turns out to be Harry Hill, a
wooden, _ _ mechanical young actor. George
Majeroni is a commendably lean and weird dumb
servant; but Cecil Ward, as the weak-minded
Captain of guards, is out of the picture. He
might be the inane fop of modern comedy mas-
querading as a Cavalier, and it seems absurd for
the vengeful dumb man to drag so flimsy a joke
over a precipice. Neither is Gaston Mervale an
artistic Cardinal Richelieu, for he merely wears a
red gown and sham white hair, and intones his
lines in short lengths after the manner of the
Black Elphberg in “Zenda.” “Under the Red
Robe ” is to have Wednesday matinees.
* * *
“Jane,” at popular prices, came back to
Melb. Bijou on Saturday after a long lapse of
years, and Pattie Brown’s reappearance in the
pink cotton garb of the name character was joy-
fully received. “Jane” is a three-act farce that
hasn’t outlived its usefulness. The authors—there
were two of ’em —managed to break away from
the beaten _ track where the penitent husband
expiates his harmless razzle-dazzle under the
baleful eye of his mother-in-law. The male
sinner in this case is a gigantic bachelor, with a
tame best girl, and a long-lost uncle, and a couple
of domestic servants who have just been made
one. In a troubled moment he borrows parlor-
maid Jane for mock-matrimonial purposes, and
clothes her in white silk and trimmings. Also,
he endows her with a borrowed baby, and omits
to explain things to William, the man-servant.
Wherefore William finds himself waiting on his
plump young bride in a menial capacity, and falls
a prey to horrible suspicions, whilst Jane sits
threatening him with instant dismissal and a
week’s wages. “ Jane ”is a roaring complication
all the time. She handles her real husband and
her borrowed belongings with great discretion,
and displays Pattie Browne at Pattie’s best. The
company otherwise differs from the co. that ap-
appeared m Sydney last month. Long Mac Vicars
is the deceitful bachelor, and Sweeney gives a
rather heavy sketch of William. Miss Eloise
Juno, Oily Deering, and Fred Cambourne fill
their respective places; likewise Miss Noni
Rmkards is a bright boy in buttons. At the Bijou,
as m Sydney, they raise the curtain with “Her
Good Name,” which 20-minutes tragedy brings
Miss Beatrice Day and Mac Vicars into favorable
notice, and leaves Walner Gregory dead in the
adjoining room.

** W W
At the Royal, a sanguine management
decided to withdraw “ Round the World, ” and to
set up “Sons of the Sea ”on Saturday. The dif-
ference between these two harmless experiments is
scarcely worth mentioning, for they are both like
pantomimes which have erred and strayed from
the ways of Xmas. Perhaps “Sons of the Sea”
errs more unforgivmgly than the other entertain-
ment. It has a solemn side to it, and sometimes
the feast of lunacy and flow of soul suspend opera-
tions, whilst a castaway mariner tears his tangled
hair on the Island of Balker. The mariner and
his female son get lost on the Island soon after 8
o clock, and they appear again at 10.15 or there-
abouts m a frost-bitten state, waiting to be rescued
by the other members of the co., who have
been enjoying sundry small jokes and volcanic
eruptions and ballets meanwhile. The ballet is
their best enjoyment from the audience’s point of
view, although the volcano provides considerable
fun and fireworks. The jokes, not less than the
frozen residents of Balker, are lacking in human
interest, and the drama as a whole will not do.
Ihe Bulletin is sorry to feel quite positive on the
point. All sorts of unexpected things can be made
to do m this world, but there isn’t money in an
ice-bound extravaganza that bears no author’s
name on the cover, and is said to have been built
in Germany. Charles Maurice. Kins* Hedley.
Miss Minna Phillips and other people, who ap
pear m “Round the World ” are doing their best
for the new show. Also, the scenery is good, and
the interpolated girls have a soothing effect.
At Poverty Point.
Australee Man ” : Heard Melba 15 months ago at
Oovent Harden. Glitter, icebergs, glaciers, showers of
pearls, electric light, flash, coruscation, dazzling perfec-
tion—but no love, tears or throat-swelling in ner song
One even got tired of its exquisite brilliancy. To hear
her cost me 10s. 6d. Eighty in orchestra and the two
De Rezskes thrown in. Also, there were dukes thrown
in. Didn’t go back on her nights; had enough. Went
to see Patti. Sunshine, love, laughter, fountains play-
ing, birds singing. There is a caressing, honeyed tone in
her voice that makes a man want to hug her. 1 sat in the
front row and saw' plainly the ravages of Time, but old
woman as she is, I feel, when I think of her voice, as
if I could travel all the way across the world to ki«s her
Then again, 1 think of old wheezy Nicolini down in the
kitchen cooking for her, and insisting that she should
eat a sufficient quantity of Fat. But I went to five of her
concerts at 10s. fid. and would have kept on ad infinitum
had they continued. I have heard the best of ’em all for
25 years, and none but she makes the music-tired man
want to go two nights in succession. No wonder Auber
cried when he “ heard her first.”
Surry Hills (Sydney) boasts a novel theatrical enter-
tainment. A bottle-dealer gives a performance in an
empty house (himself and two sons constitute the C o )
Admission—front seats, three bottles ; middle, two ordi-
nary or one pickle; back seats, one bottle of any sort.
Says he averaged 150 bottles for three performances
“ Where is’Olt alongside that?” he queried. An oppo-
sition “ bottle-admission” show is going to start in East-
street, Surry Hills. Twelve performers!
Of course, the new Sydney Markets concert-hall proves
a sad alderinamc mistake. It was first used for a Plevel
concert on Saturday night, when the doorway was
blocked by small boys and other curious folk, and women
in evening attire had to crush their w'ay throich the
crowd. Inside, the atmosphere was stifling, and many of
the.audience left before the programme was half through
Also, the hall has only one exit, and will he about the
most dangerous in Sydney in case of a fire-stampede.
Its acoustics are all right, comparatively, but the hall is
only of a size for “sassiety” functions, and “sassietv”
will fight shy of those dreadful Markets.
When Oscar Wilde wrote his play of “Salome” in
French, he gave a copy of it to Leslie Thompson, now of
Pattie Browne’s co. Wilde was then rehearsing one of his
plays with Beerbohm Tree’s co., of which Thompson was
a member. He wanted an English translation made of
the play. “Do it for me,” he said, “ and I shall ever be
your debtor.” Thompson, a good French scholar, com-
pleted the translation, and sent the MS. to Wilde. Wilde
at once sent him a 400-word telegram, in which he rhap-
sodised about the eternal beauties of this piece of trans-
Jating. It was a thing of beauty ;an imperishable jewel
of literature; a pearl beyond price. The next thing
Thompson heard about “Salome” was that a translation
of it by Lord Alfred Douglas had been published with the
“™ or ’ s sanction. And he subsequently learned that
Wilde had got a small army of people to do translations
for him, and had thanked most of them in 400-word tele-
grams expressive of his rapturous joy !
Harry Plimmer has gone to the western piovincesas
leading man of the Firm’s new dramatic co., and will
perpetrate Marcus Superbus, Wilfred Denver, Rudolf
Rassendyll, N. Bonaparte, &c. As Marcus Superbus
Plimmer ought to lay over Julius Knight, and it is a pity
that he can’t also do a spell of “ Claudian.” Both are
mainly leg parts, and Plimmer has got all the legs that
even the short-skirted Roman could ask for. Considering
that that end of Plimmer would be as great a novelty as
the same end of the imported star, for the public
never saw that end of Plimmer before, what was the good
of importing a scar in preference to the local mo-a, any-
how ? J
“Tromboner”: I remember Poussard as the musical
conductor of a disastrous opera season at Melb. Opera
House. His wife was billed as manageress and bright
particular star of -the co.,and poor old Horace P was
seemingly the one man in the theatre who found music
in her voice and joy in her presence. Every night the
lady would get a “ treble encore ” for the dullest of dit-
ties. Six people, more or less, clamored for the encore on
each occasion, whilst the rest of the audience smiled or
squirmed derisively at the precious “demonstration.”
But Poussard believed in the singer and the applause.
His eyes sparkled as he gave the signal for the orchestra
to repeat the music for the last verse—and again—and
again. The old man’s admiration for Madame’s tiresome
performance was quite pathetic. I marvelled that a
musician’s ears could be so deaf to the melodious truth.
Tweedie and M’Gowan give a first-class couple of rounds
in “ Sporting Life ”at Sydney Royal; and Walter Baker
does not make half a bad showing in knocking his man
out. Lord Woodstock’s get-up, however, does not show
Bland Holt’s usual minuteness of detail. Baker fights
for a fortune in a stiff-starched shirt, trousers braces,
and leather-soled boots.
The condition of Perth is illustrated by the tact that
several Saturday evenings have lately passed without a
theatre or other public place of entertainment being open.
“Haresfoot ” : The highest literary water-mark of the
purely Australian stage, I think, was reached in two
original blank-verse tragedies, written and acted in Mel-
bourne. First in order of time was “ The Gladiator,” by
Jaffray, a pressman, of the Agb ; and, second, “ Bernardo
del Carpio,” by John Finnamore, a lawyer’s clerk, whom
I had the pleasure of meeting* the other day. During the
long intervening years, Finnamore might have done some
good work, if encouraged. But what manager could
possibly afford encouragement to the blank-verse-tragedy
industry ?
Latest reports from the London ’alls speak of Charles
Godfrey as being more recovered than ever. He seems
to have found a new constitution stronger than the one
that fell to pieces in Westralia.
The subscription for poor old Sims Reeves gets along
very slowly. W. S. Gilbert set a good example to sassiety
by weighing in with a £lO-note.

A man lately sang “Paradise Alley” at a “sacred”
concert in M.L. The pious hall-proprietor arose and told
the audience he did not know they were going to sing
comic songs. “If I’d a knowed it,” he said, “ I wouldn’t
a let ’em ’ave the ’all. Even now, if the ’all was lubri-
cated with gas, I’d turn the lights out on ’em.”
“A.H.”; Why must the stage contralto insist on re-
minding us of the vasty deep from which she calls her
tones, by beginning each phrase with a “Yah-hurru-
roop,” as though she were calling for the steward?
“ How do they know they are men ? It’s only a name
they give themselves.”— Peggy Pryde, on Women’s Bights.
“ Theorbo ” : Poussard had died out of mind in Melb.,
which he hated. His last appearance there was as
entrepreneur of comic opera, with Lottie Montal as prima
donna, in “ Madame L’Archiduc,” but it was a Polar frost.
Many old-chum Australians who remember the inter-
esting marriage and untimely deaths of Miss Furtadoand
comedian John Clarke, may have wondered what became
of their two orphan infant children. The girl, Teresa
Furtado Clarke, a charming young actress, was married
in great style the other week to McLean Barradile, a
theatrical potentate. Barradile’s bride has said good-bye
to the stage.
Olga Nethersole was to re-appear in London (at Her
Majesty s) on Sept. Ist, as the heroine of Louis Parker’s
new play “ The Termagant.”
Bessie Doyle, the Sydney violinist, was very badlv
spoken of by Continental audiences. One excited
foreigner exclaimed after a concert—" I went to hear a
violin, played by a player. I only heard wood. I did not
go to hear wood.” Shortly after this Miss Doyle got
married in a most unassuming manner, and disappeared
to America.

When the improvement comes this way, gas
shares should get a good lift from the introduction of
the new Welsbach burner, which needs no chimney or
artificial draught, is not affected by variation in pressure,
and gives a better light with the same consumption.

Apropos “ half fine to the informer.” A Sydney
shopkeeper accidentally gave an unstamped receipt to a
man who had a “ down ” upon him. “ See,” said the
latter, “ I’ll lay an information against you for this,” and
departed. Shopkeeper hurried up to “Old Hemming,”
the Commissioner, laid information against self, and
secured half the fine. When the “informer" proper
arrived, blue was the div.

* * * *
When an tip-country storekeeper smashes, the
stock is usually sold in one lot to highest cash tenderer.
Cash stipulation bars the local traders, and a city man
generally picks up the crumb at a low figure, and—sup-
plementing it with cheap auction and warehouse lines
and discarded portions of his town stock—advertises a
“monster cash clearing sale,” at which prices (thanks to
his many advantages) are much under the local figure,
and thus scoops the cash which should have reached the
locals in payment of long-standing scores. The city mer-
chants are then surprised at renewal-requests from the
regular price-paying storekeepers, and have not sagacity
to see that their own methods are ruining their best cus-
tomers for the benefit of the insolvent-estate shark—
usually a direct importer of his requirements.
* • • *
Some of the Australian provinces export dried
apricots to England, and are quite surprised to find that
they are subject to 7s. per cwt. duty, while tinned apri-
cots from California are admitted free. The idea that
Britain is a Freetrade country receives some hard knocks
now and then.

From a letter written to Australia by the prin-
cipal of a very big English firm trading here :—“ General
Expenses : In consequence of what has transpired re-
cently we are compelled to scrutinise your account very
closely, and we now notice an item under date 4th April
for clerk’s tea, 2s. We should certainly think that 6d. or,
at the outside, Is. should be sufficient, 2s. being ample for
lunch—food being much cheaper in the colony than in
London.”
* • * •
The new air compressor at the Long Tunnel
mine, Walhalla, Victoria, is giving great satisfaction, and
has made a continuous run, night and day, of over
18 months, without once stopping. Manager Thomson
speaks in the highest terms of it. The plant is a 350
I.H.P. cross compound double stage air compressor, with
Corliss type air and steam valves, and reflects great credit
on the Austral Otis Engineering Company, of South Mel-
bourne, who manufactured and erected it. This firm has
lately purchased the plant and stock of the old-established
Langlands Foundry co., now in liquidation, and, as it
has all the patterns, can supply duplicate parts of any
machines made by Langlands.*
* • * *
Libel-actions by the dozen having had no other
effect on Henry Hess than to fatten his paper, the Critic,
bis enemies (the London boomsters and boodlers) are now
threatening him with horsewhips and pistols. One
Hurnby having been described in the Critic as the asso-
ciate of a certain shark in nine out of fifteen of the latter’s
swindle#, he wrote Hess that he did not care to spend
money in law, hut as he understood that Hess was about
to spend the summer holidays at Flushing, he would meet
him there with any weapon he choose, and have it out
together. Also, that he would prefer the old English
method of fists, but, was doubtful if Hess would care for
that style ; if so, however, he promised “ the damnedest
thrashing you have ever received.” If Hess declined any
method of extermination, Humby says he will treat him
as a coward whenever and wherever he meets him, as he
is not going to permit “ a German with a tainted record
to elect himself a censor over his actions. If he had done
anything wrong he was ready to face a jury and answer
for the consequences.” Hess declines, remarking that he
may be twice over a coward, but that does not make Mr.
Humby less a swindler.

The protean Dreyfus-case breaks out in a
fresh place every day. Esterhazy (admittedly a
German spy) now puts responsibility for his ad-
mitted forgeries upon Colonel Sandherr, president
of the court-martial which condemned Dreyfus.
And Sandherr is “the Man who’s Dead,” once
more !
Grein, alias Rougemont, to an English
interviewer: “ It is, as you may suppose, a very
grave mistake to think that I am spouting out the
whole story glibly. Indeed, the connected inci-
dents come to me only very slowly, and I cannot
tax my unfortunate head for more than a couple
of hours at a time.” Naturally a man can’t lie about
cannibal kings, and ash-buckets of virgin gold,
and dogs with tails for rescuing drowning sailors,
&c., &c., all day long without stopping. You
must give inventors a leetle • time to let their
imagination hitch its pants up and get its boots on
the right feet, in a manner of speaking.

The Victorian revenue for year ended 30th
June, 1898, was 6,887,463 ; ordinary expenditure,
£6,682,704 ; surplus, which has been applied in reduction
of the old deficit, £204,759.
* * » *
Item from a manifesto lately issued by the
Perpetual Executors and Trustees Association (Mel-
bourne) :—“ At the present time, owing to the demand
for Government debentures, the very high premium at
which they are quoted and the great dearth in the supply
of these securities, even at abnormal rates—money has to
remain in the banks on current account, earning no
interest whatever. This is a most unfortunate position
for beneficiaries, and there seems to be no immediate
prospect of relief.” Victorian 3 per cent, stock is selling
at £lO3 or a little more in Melbourne. There is no English
quotation to comp ire with, but 3 per cent. N.SAV. stock
is only worth £llll 10s. in London. In the flotation of
new loans £lO3 in Melbourne is really equal to £lO5 or £lO6,
or even more, in London, because tV"’ latter involves (1)
payment of heavy stamp-duty to Bull Government; (2)
payment of heavy expenses to the bank which does the flo-
tation (in Melbourne it is done by the Treasury at practi-
cally no extra cost) ; (3) payment twice a year for 30 or 40
or 50 years or perhaps for five centuries of exchange in re-
mitting interest to London ; (4) payment for a few cen-
turies of a considerable sum annually for management of
the loan in London (in Melbourne it only costs the salary of
an extra Treasury clerk); (5) permanent loss of income-tax
on the interest (the London bondholders being exempt from
income-tax, while the Victorian ones pay that impost) ;
(6) loss of probate duty when the holder dies, and
the probate duty runs as high as 10 per cent. Yet, with
all these advantages in favor of local flotation, and the
fact that Victoria offers a bigger price than Bull and is
simply howling for Government securities, Premier
Turner only proposes vaguely to float £500,000 of his new
loan in Melbourne, and the other £1,000,000 and all the
conversion loans are apparently to be floated in London !
If Turner would offer 3 per cent, interminable securities
(repayable solely at the State’s option after, say, 20 years)
in Melbourne at 99 (and 99 in Melbourne would be worth
a great deal more than he is ever likely to get in London),
he could almost certainly get seven or eight millions.
That is provided he kept the stock on sale at the Trea-
sury, and let the money drift in gently, instead of asking
for a huge lump at once.
* 6 » *
Westralian Premier and Treasurer Forrest
cheerfully estimates that the revenue for the year to June,
1899, will be £2,905,350, or £150,603 more than it was last
year. He made this estimate late in August, when the
revenue was already falling about his ears like a paper
city struck by an earthquake. For July and August, the
revenue is £42,372 less than it was for July and August
last year, and, instead of £150,000 increase, the year will
probably show a drop of £250,000. Then Big John esti-
mated the year’s expenditure at £2,714,134, or £542,778
less than it was last year, and talks glibly of paying off
last year’s deficit and having a balance to the good. In
the roaring days, when the boom revenue rolled in beyond
the dreams of avarice, Big John was a good enough Trea-
surer—it was hardly possible to be otherwise then. In
the bad days that are looming, he promises to be the
worst financier these provinces ever saw or heard of.
» * * »
Westralia has managed to place the balance of
its £1,000,000 3 per cent, loan (the one which failed so
miserably a few weeks ago) in London privately at £94 4s.
Stamp-duty and expenses will probably bring this down
below £93. Allowing for the loss on repayment, Westralia
is paring 34 per cent, for its money, And it lately raised a
loan in Australia with much less trouble at the same
price.
* * • *
South Australia for the year to 30th June, 1898
—revenue, £2,566,611 ; expenditure, £2,598,939 ; deficit,
£32,328. For the year to June, 1899, estimates—revenue,
£2,649,899 ; expenditure, £2,619,220 ; surplus, £30,679.
Like Forrest, the hired optimist of Westralia, Treasurer
Holder doesn’t provide any new sources of revenue ; he
simply squares the accounts on paper by assuming that
the old sources will grow. And that is” how S.A usually
gets left.. In the last 23 financial years S.A. has had 11
surpluses and 12 deficits, but the four largest deficits were
£361,000, £295,000, £272,000, and £201,000 ; while the
four largest surpluses were £133,000, £129,000, £109,000,
£109,000 ; therefore the accumulated shortage is close on
a million. And, in addition to this, the Northern Terri-
tory has a deficit of about £560,000, which isn’t taken into
the S.A. accounts, though if S.A. doesn’t pay it no one
else can, unless the Federal Government takes it over.
* * • *
S.A. railway revenue for year ending June
shows a loss of £80,768, or nearly £30,000 more than in
previous year. Net revenue only provides 2'98 per cent,
on capital. Earnings, £984,228; expenses (including in-
terest, £461,523), £1,064,996.

“ Robert J. C.”: What The Bulletin said about
the Victoria Insurance Co. was true. And you care-
fully dodge every issue it raised (that the dividends
have been sometimes beyond earnings ; that the co. ex-
hausted its profits in immense dividends while there were
heavy losses, through depreciation of assets, unprovided
for ; and that it is consequently much poorer than it was
a few years ago). You talk vaguely about the age of the
co. and the wealth of the shareholders, but quite shirk the
real question.
* * * •
The Commercial Bank of Tasmania’s half-yearly
report to 31st August is at least a trifle more cheering
than that of the previous half—the previous half repre-
sented the Commercial’s worst on record for very many
years, therefore even a slight improvement is acceptable.
The net profits for the half-year are £3823, equal to
£2 6s. 2d. per cent, per annum on the shareholders’ funds
(capital £141,492 ; reserve £190,000). Considering that
the Commercial of Tasmania is a very old and deep-rooted
institution (it is now 66 years of age), that it never re-
constructed, and that it makes no mention of having any
jpsses to provide for, the earnings are certainly very
small and they vaguely suggest the idea that an overhaul
of the securities might not be amiss. The recent profits
have been :
June, 1892 ..£13,393 December, 1895 .. £3700
December, 1892.. 8352 June, 1896 .. .. 4199
June, 1893 .. 6589 December, 1896 .. 3833
December, 1893.. 5586 August, 1897
June, 1894, .. 4345 (8 months) .. 4969
December, 1894.. 4378 February, 1898 .. 3463
June, 1895 .. 4610 August, 1898 .. 3823
The usual 5 per cent, dividend and dividend-tax thereon
absorb £3714, and the remaining £lO9, with previous
balance, leaves £747 at credit of profit and loss.
The Commercial of Tasmania shows, as com-
pared with last half-year, a decrease of £94,000 in de-
posits, and the cash (£334,000) shows a decrease of £34,000.
Nine years ago the Commercial paid 20 per cent, dividends,
while the National of Tasmania, having a vastly smaller
reserve to earn profits with, paid a humble 7£. Also,
the Commercial showed a year’s profit of over £30,000,
while the National earned less than £lO,OOO. Now
they both pay 5 per cent., but the small National has
rather the larger profits of the two. This doesn’t neces-
sarily mean anything; still it shows that the fading of
Tasmania’s biggest bank, from a profit-earning point of
view, has been somewhat remarkable. The reserve of
cash and State securities now equals 32 percent, of all
liabilities to the public, and the fact that the Commercial
keeps a much larger cash-balance than in the old 20 per
cenc. days accounts for part of the drop. It also keeps a
much smaller reserve of State securities, which isn’t espe-
cially good policy. If it put 1100,000 of its cash (which,
should be easily spared) into State securities it would
make the half-year’s profits about £5400 instead of the
depressing i'3823.
» * • *
The British Bank of Australia —probably the
most decomposed financial carcase left over from the
boom—is near the end of its liquidation, and is expected
to yield the creditors 2d. in the £ all round. This is pro-
vided the debenture-holders don’t succeed in establishing
a prior claim ; if they do, the depositors will get abso-
lutely nothing. The rema ning assets appear in the
books as worth £469,476, and the liquidator has sanguine
hopes that they will pan out for £2016. Calls in arrear
(£47,925) are expected to yield £2OO ; most of the share-
holders have already been crushed and panned off, and
the survivors are only good for a speck or two. The
British Bank obtained £153,000 from depositors, and
invested it mostly in buying three times as much property
as it could pay' for. When the slump came, the mort-
gagees took the property, and the 8.8. was left. It didn’t
live long enough even to make a decent rise for the boom-
sters who van the show —but that was nothing unusual.
Even the brightest of the booms'ers was 45 per cent,
knave and 55 per cent, fool, and, while he left the public
badly, he invariably ended by getting left himself? He
didn’t deserve to be hanged any the less, forr /he
generally intended to be 100 per cent, knave and no fool
at all, but he hadn’t the needful brain-furniture.
The Premier Permanent Building Society, that
huge concern whose smash, seven or eight years ago, was
the first sign that the bottom was falling out of the Mel-
bourne boom, seems likely to pay its first and last divi-
dend shortly. An English absentee co. has offered to buy
the old stupendous wreck in one lot, and give the depos-
tors 2s. lOd. in the £ payable in five years with 3
per cent, interest, or 2s. in the £ cash. The offer
is being seriously considered. Seven years ago all
the dreadfully smart men in Melbourne were buying
these deposits at 16s. in the '£, and cackling a fruity
cackle at the thought of how they had taken the deposi-
tor in. And six years ago the depositor had begun to
feel glad he had sold and rescued at least the bulk of his
money out of the fire ; and he went and put that money
carefully in Colin Longmuir’s bank so that it would be
quite, quite safe. And most of the years since then both
parties have wished they were dead.
* * * *
The latest report of the South Australian Co.,
the big absentee land-owning concern which has become
so notorious in S. A., shows a profit of £28,861 for ’97, and
its Fat Men shareholders, most of whom have never seen
S.A., received a div. of 10 per cent, for the year. In the
last 13 years the lowest div. has been 9 and the highest 15
percent. Premier Kingston promised to enquire into
this rack-renting co.’s dealings awhile ago, but nothing
more has been heard of the matter. The great S.A. Co.
trouble doesn’t lie so much in the dividends, handsome as
they are, as in the accumulated surplus. The co. pro-
fesses to have £284,000 paid-up capital and a Very' modest,
reserve of about £35,000. But if it wrote up its assets to
their present value—which would be very unwise policy—
it could show a real reserve of about £750,000, and if it
sold out now it could, after paying excellent dividends for
a generation and a half, give its shareholders their money
back three or four times over. So the S.A. Co. has been
an eminently prosperous absentee, and has ground the
bones of the Crow-eater to some purpose.
* * * *
The N.S. Wales Assurance Corporation has
about finished its liquidation. It was killed mainly by
its London business, which landed it in a dead loss of
£15,397 in addition to expenses. The Pacific, which 15
years ago was the biggest N.S.W. fire-insurance concern,
died from the same cause ; and the Standard of Maoriland
lost almost all its belongings through its foreign business
about 18years ago, though it hassince retrieved its position.
European losses have swallowed up an enormous amount
of good Australian money in the insurance line, and the
most prosperous co.’s now keep pretty closely to the local
business—the most prosperous one of the lot even keeps
inside a single province. , The interested wail of the
“ expert,” that State fire-insurance is impossible because
it will necessarily be restricted to one province is a very
tattered lie, in the light of past experience.
• • * *
Every man who has a life-assurance policy to
leave to his descendants should carefully strike out of his
will that old familiar phrase, “after payment of all my
just debts.” In fact, he should leave out all mention of
his just debts, which will probably take mighty good care
of themselves. In several of the provinces a life-policy is
more or less protected against creditors, but the clause
above mentioned destroys that protection, and the “ just
debts” swallow it along with the other assets.
* • * *
The Excelsior Land Investment and Building
Co. and Bank (Sydney), shows for the year ended 31st
July, ’9B, a profit of £448 —about Bs. sd. per cent, per
annum on paid-up capital. Present prospects look not
especially bright. The Excelsior got a year’s exemption
from interest on its deposits, and now pays only 24 per
cent., yet even on these easy terms it just about
makes ends meet. About £lBO,OOO of alleged visible
assets produce a gross income of £2 18s. per cent.,
and the property and mortgages which yield only
£2 18s. per cent, are generally worth on the
whole a good deal less than their paper value.
The Excelsior’s third biggest “asset” is accumulated loss
£32,483. Its fourth biggest is “calls unpaid £8020,” and
the fifth largest asset is a painfully vague item called
“ suspense account, £1372.” Among them, therefore,
£41,875 of the Excelsior’s assets, or about one-fifth of the
whole, are mere book entries. The deposits should be
very respectable value for the 7s. 6d. in the £ they have
been lately selling at, or even for more than that, but the
present prospects of the shareholders look like not much
in the £.

Melbourne Age reckons that the disgraceful
Standard Bank will yield 3s. in the £to depositors. The
Bulletin estimates that, though the paper assets are
equal to more than the liabilities, the Standard won’t
yield over 2s. in the £ altogether. It wouldn’t be sur-
prised to see the old wreck pan out for only Is. 6d.

The Central Mill business, like the guarantee-
railway system, is going to land Q, Govt, in a pretty
pickle. Up to 30th June, ’9B, there had been advanced
£430,000 on Central sugar-mills. A total of £4BOO had
been paid to the Treasury in interest thereupon, and
nothing in redemption. There is owing in interest
£33,000, and in redemption-money £25,000 —a total of
£55,500. Of course, sugar-advocates will tell you, “Oh,
Govt, is amply secured by its mortgage on the "properties,”
but every man knows in his heart that the State won’t
foreclose. Queensland sugar-kings have had more pam-
pering and coddling than any other class in Australia.
Nigger, Jap, and Cingalese labor ; nice little branch lines
here and there ; and last, but not least, mills erected at
their doors by the dear paternal Govt. Talk about a soft
thing 1 „
During the Zeehan boom of ’9O, a prospector,
out for a Hobart syndicate, struck a lode somewhere, and,
selecting several specimens, sewed them up neatly in a
bag, and took them round to the capital. A special meet-
ing was called—for everyone was redhot in those days
and the p. attended with his pack of stuff. “ Now, gen-
tlemen,” he said, “ you’ve seen a few speciments, but I
venture to say that these will surprise you. Then he up-
ended the bag on the table, and out rolled about 151bs.
weight of Circular Head spuds. The originals had been
stolen at Strahan, brought round to Launceston, and
materially helped to float a big co., whose property was
never pegged, surveyed, or known : in fact, never existed.
Which recalls the box of silver sent by a Hobart
bank to its Zeehan branch. When it reached its destina-
tion, it was found to contain bricks. The mystery re-
mains.

Some Mt. Lyell figures : Ore smelted per month,
11,000 tons, mined by 280 men. Firewood consumed,
4600 tons per month. Sandstone and limestone for flux-
ing, 12,000 cubic yards per month ; coke, 750 tons. Tim-
ber output by the sawmills, 160,000 ft. Men employed on
or about the smelters, 1100. Eight locomotives are in
use. Expenditure in wages is now at the rate of a quarter
of a million a year, and will probably reach half a million
inside two years.

The Tasmania mine, Beaconsfield, Tas., was

11 million pounds worth of gold ; divs., £667,161.

New Pinafore (Tas.) deep-sinkers haven’t struck
it rich yet, but the manager “confidently anticipates
success at any moment.”
* * * *
Concerning the deadliness of Mt. Lyell fumes,
writer knows of several ex-furnace men, formerly fine,
strapping fellows, who, after six months at the smelters,
are reduced to walking skeletons of their former selves.
They sorrowfully admit that they are “ cooked.” Strong
men working in brickyards some distance away from the
smelting works have lately been knocked out by the
fumes. A vegetable garden, planted to windward and
over a mile from the works, throve amazingly until one
day ohe wind blew in a direction it had never been known
to blow before, and every cabbage plant was killed in less
than six hours ! Wages for slaving in such an atmosphere,
7s. 6d. a day. Despite precautionary regulations, which,
by the way, are not too strictly enforced, Tasmania’s
legacy of woe is certain to be materially increased from
this source.

Crawcours, of course.

Dear Bulletin.—How’s this for mean ? Since
return-h.alves of Vic. railway tickets have been made valid
for 7 days, a public servant (over £5OO a year) works this
neat game. On Monday morning he buys a Ist return-
ticket—his suburb-to-Melb., and uses one half. In after-
noon he buys ditto—Melb.-to-suburb, and uses one half.
Then for rest of week buys each day a second-class return,
and travels first. If train is raided he produces the
proper Ist class half. —O.K.
• * * *
“Melb.”: The broken-down land-agent is a
familiar study. How we used to envy this class in the
boom ! They mopped up the seats on the municipal
councils, enjoyed almost a monopoly of mavorhood, be-
sought guests to lip the champagne bucket, drove the
nattiest curricles, with spouses arrayed like Hadassah for
king Ahasuerus, lived in such places as Sir Davies’s
Bracknell, had their names gilt on the bluestone rock of
church memorial stones. Now, if you see a greeny-coated
Montague Tigg on his uppers, leaning against a tree-guard
in the street, or depriving the perambulating nursegirl of
the full length of a bench in the park with a midday
siesta, bet vour boots it’s a house-and-land agent.
* * » •
Sixteen Eehuea (Vic.) hotelkeepers (out of 20),
whose pubs, were closed under local option, “ fell in ”
beautifully by deposing (with an eye to compensation-
figure) to larger profits than had appeared in their income-
tax returns. The little discrepancy cost them a fiver and
costs a-piece for “ evading the income-tax.”
* • • *
Green vale Station (N.Q.) was sold the other day
for £l5OO. The station consisted of 640 sq. miles of
country and carried 10.000 head of cattle and 140 head of
horses. The sale price works out to about 3s. a head and
is looked upon as the record sacrifice of the North.
Messrs. Jillett Brothers, old pioneers, were the buyers.
* • * •
S.A. Gas co. holds what is practically a mono-
poly of Adelaide lighting until 1902, but Adelaide City
Corporation recently made a provisional agreement with
a local electric-light co. The matter went before the citi-
zens, and there was much acrimony' between the rival
cos., but electric-light carried the day by'two to one, and
experimental lines will now be laid.
• » * * •
Several of the English mining syndicates have
withdrawn from Maoriland, and others are preparing to
follow their example. Rumored that even the Anglo-
Continental Syndicate, of which Premier Seddon is a
shining light, thinks of “ clearing.” It is an open secret
that certain of its leading officials are at loggerheads.
One English manager declares that Govt, charges have
swallowed up 20 per cent, of the capital he brought into
the country. On the other hand, to show the little effect
that booms have on the production of gold, last month’s
yield was the record for Auckland province. And the
Auckland gold-boom is as dead as the pterodactyl’s
mother-in-law.
* * * *
In Maoriland, in sending commercial papers
to Australia, if they weigh under £oz. it is cheaper
to send them as letters. If you send them in open en-
velopes they are 2£d. Seal the envelope and they go for
2d. Again, Joz. commercial papers or Joz. printed papers
would cost Jd., but }oz. each together costs Id. Govern-
ment stroke isn’t paid for thinking.
* • • •
In Maoriland recently, a provincial smarty,
owing a bill in town, placed a half-sovereign in an
envelope. Then he put the envelope into a press and
succeeded in obtaining a good impression of the coin on
the wrapper. Abstracting the haif-sov. he forwarded the
impressed envelope to his town friend with a note, saying
that he was forwarding enclosed 10s. in settlement of his
debt. There being no coin enclosed, but an excellent
imprint of a “ half-sovereign ” on the envelope, the town
man immediately blamed the postal officials, and after a
big inquiry the matter ended by “towny” letting the
debt slide.
* • • *
“ Malle ”: It’s fashionable to adulate the
“ poor honest miner,” and to condemn the villainy of the
mining shark and promoter; but the horny-handed pick-
agitator usually knows enough to elbow the other fellow
into the wet if he gets a show. A party came on rich
specimens in a gully, and spent weeks unsuccessfully
trying to trace them. A couple of new-chum prospectors
seeing the party at work, thought—“ good enough for
them, good enough for me,” and began trenching a spur
near by. At knock-off time the new-comers strolled into
the old crowd’s camp and asked “What luck?” Being
told “ not much ” they produced some A 1 specimens and
said, “ We hadn’t done an hour’s graft before we tumbled
over this—and she’s 18in. wide.” One of the originals
was in his tent, heard this, slipped out at the back, and
half-an-hour later walked into camp with a “ Good even-
in’, mates,” to the new-chums. The specimens were again
produced, and, after listening to the description, he said:
“ Why, that’s on my lease. I found that a week ago. I
could go to it blindfold." They went together to inspect,
and sure enough the new reef was in the centre of a 10*
acre block applied for by Hardface and party. The
finders were magnanimously allowed to peg out next
block on line, and some months after, when a big sale of
the amalgamated blocks had come off, they were told how
they’d been bluffed.

WATSON’S WHISKY.
SOCIETY
S. A. Governor Buxton leaves with his
family for England this Thursday, and Adelaide
society joyously anticipates a lively time under the
regime of Way and his charming wife. Gov.
Buxton leaves behind him a mixed record of
frigidity and piety, leavened, however, by much
private generosity to deserving charities. He
aspires to sit in the Commons (which is bad news
tor the Commons), and may not return to S.A. at
the end of his six months’ leave.
* * * ♦
Gov. Buxton had a fatal habit of blundering
into discussions about controversial politics, and
a)most his last speech prior to leaving for Eng-
tand was distinguished by a veiled attack on
alleged Godless ” State education. S.A. parsons
and other Bible-reading advocates had their refer-
endum on the question awhile ago and were badly
beaten, but the Governor, with sad want of
discretion, openly advocated fresh agitation. The
interference of the stupid old blunderer was much
resented, and it was only the prospect of his early
departure that prevented a shindy
*** J »
When it was announced at a recent Ade-
laide Wesleyan Home Mission demonstration that
oaii OC V, y ’ n P° or circumstances had donated
201 b. of almonds, Chief Justice Way offered £1
tor lib. ; and, as others followed suit, the
whole donation was disposed of then and there
“ >r a of £2l- Hardly to be believed, hew-
ever that thereafter, amongst the goody-goody
crowd, there wasn t a single “ Christian ” to sug-
gest that a pound or two of the money should in
common decency be applied to the relief of the
old lady’s “poor circumstances.’’ And a Home
Mission, too-not even the excuse of the “pore
heathen.” Truly, mechanical Christianity is a
wondrous thing!
Ihe Maitland (N.S.W.) Chinese mission
(Maitland has got the Chinese mission very bad
indeed) is still struggling in the vineyard. Quite
a lot of Chows rolled up at the last series of
special services. As the Chinese minister didn't
happen to be there,” a European rev. had to
spread the light to those present by means of the
almighty middle man—Mr. Kem Yee, the inter-
preter. This he did in simple but earnest lan-
guage, suited to the comprehension of his hearers.
He showed the analogy existing* between
natural and spiritual seed—one of the listeners
thought it all depended on where you bought the
seed. All such technical points having been care-
fully explained, the light was beginning to shine
very brightly until Kem, the interpreter, related
an incident which blew it out very suddenly. A
little while ago a Chow had become gradually im-
pressed with the truth of Christianity and had
become a candidate for baptism. Before his wish
could be realised, however, an illness fell upon
him and he died. At this all the Chows present
turned pale and cast eager glances towards the
°°° r ; A »e impression was that the anger of Joss
had fallen on the convert, and there was a general
desire to escape before Joss boiled over again.
Kem Yee tried to retrieve his error by stating
that the death of the unlucky Chinaman was a
believing and a happy one. It was too late, how-
ever. Ihe collection which followed was not at
all satisfactory, and the Chinese took up their
baskets and went forth to hawk the vegetable in a
depressed spirit. No converts are expected from
the present generation of Maitland Chows, on
account of Kern’s unfortunate illustration.

That London journalist’s stirring picture
of girl-missionary life in China has provoked some
quaint replies from the girls in question. Here
are some of a Miss Voak’s counterblasts, copied
into a Dunedin paper : “ That the Chinese are in-
variably courteous to women ; that her person is
always sacred ; that even a slave-girl is treated
with respect; that nothing was too much to be
done for them, i.e. , the girl-missionaries ; and that
the further they were from British influence the
safer they were, as the people are more simple-
minded.” And now, being convinced that the
Chinese are a nation of Galahads, one wants to
know what the deuce they are being converted
for ?

“S.S.”: People snigger at Hobart Par-
liamentary Debating Society’s message of con-
dolence to Albert Edward in his crippled condi-
tion, but the Australian Agents-General went,
half-a-dozen better, and “on behalf of their
Governments and the Australian people” ex-
pressed unbounded admiration for “ the fortitude
with which H.R.H. bore his severe injury.” It
remains now for the Royal Humane Societies and
similar bodies throughout the Empiah to present
Albert Edward with medals and belts and things
in hearty appreciation of the German bull-dog
heroism he has displayed in not howling from the
house-tops and execrating heaven for permitting a
Royal Highness to hurt his leg. These grovel-
some Imperialists themselves might get along very
well without legs. They are always on theii
bellies.

Queenscliffe (Vic.) last week decided to
pursue Sirdar Kitchener up the Nile with a con-
gratulatory “resolution.” Queenscliffe is the little
tin-pot township on one of Port Phillip Heads
with three or four big hotels where tired Mel-
bournians grow tireder in summer. In the off
season, Q. consists of a few artillerymen, town
councillors and goats, and is about as cheerful a
spot as Henry Lawson’s Hungerford.
* * *' *
In a moment of slobber North Sydney
municipal council decided last June to erect a
“ jubilee fountain ” in honor of Her Gracious.
The foundation stone was duly laid and <£9o voted
for its completion ; yet within 50 yards of the site
selected is a marble fountain erected to the memory
of a late Mayor who held office for 18 years—which
memorial fountain, by the way, has never had any
water in it.

“Melb.”: Deakin’s oratory flowered like
the Victoria Regia at the exhibition, the other
Sunday afternoon, for the Wesleyan Mission,
which, I hear, is .£2OOO in debt. Between the
lines, this is what he said : “You Methodists are
a lot of cranks, with your twopenny-halfpenny
ideas about the blue-flames of hell and Heaven like
the transformation scene of a Xmas pantomine.”
I bev applauded furiously, even as they did when
Rabbi Abrahams told them the same thing. I
suppose John Wesley has gone to smash.
* * * *
According to a well-informed authority,
Sydney barmaids are paid by the spirit-merchants
as well as by the publicans—this to induce them
to push the whisky brands. One firm, it is said,
besides paying a commission on cases sold and
corks drawn, gave every Hebe in whose bar their
brand was successful a cheque for 15 guineas on a
big local rag-shop at Xmas-time. So that a bar-
maid may be perfectly straight and yet wear fine
clothes. A moral in this for the W.C.T.U.

At Carlton (Vic.) the other day the beaks
fined a man 40s. for breaking another man’s leg,
under circumstances of great brutality. Next
day Melbourne Bench fined a woman 20s. for
breaking a woman’s jaw. For the public con-
venience it will soon be necessary to publish a
Gazette notice, specifying the exact penal equiva-
lents of all degrees of assault and battery.
Now that the obnoxi-
ous word “pauper”is ban-
ished from most hospital
admission-cards and Govt,
relief ordinances, it is time
to wipe it off the legal
forms of the Insolvency
and Divorce Courts. “In
forma pauperis ” in the
law courts is neither more
or less than “Pauper’s
ticket ” in the invidious
old vernacular.

Although the school-
master has been abroad
in Maoriland for 50 years,
and education is free and
compulsory, there is still
a supply of illiterates.
Among recent discoveries
arc a Jaypee who can
neither read nor write, and

same blissful condition—
the latter having allegedly
passed his “exam.” by
getting a friend to hold
his hand while he filled in
the necessary papers. The
Premier has promised an

the jolly skipper, because
it’s a matter affecting hu-
man life; the Jaypee, it
would seem, may go on

“ Agricola ” : People
used to wonder what be-
came of the mutton-birds’ eggs, collected in
thousands at Western Port (Vic.). They were
sold to Melbourne pastrycooks, who used to dis-
guise the intolerable smell with vanilla and such
things.
» * * *
Smellbourne has its Lourdes. On Mount
Eagle, Ivan hoe, six miles from the city, dwells a
noted herbalist, and to him of late have been flock
ing crowds of fanatics to be healed of ailments,
real or imagined. Such is the rush that the man
of herbs has found it necessary to issue advance
tickets, and happy is she who can get hold of one
dated only a fortnight ahead. The results, accord-
ing to report, are phenomenal. Cases that are the
despair of Collins-street find, it is said easy cure
on Mount Eagle. The sage diagnoses the trouble
from examination of the finger-nails, then hands
over a bunch of herbs, takes a shilling, and—the
thing is done ! Up till recently the old gentle-
man ran the business on philanthropic lines, but,
in self-defence, has been obliged to institute the
modest charge aforesaid. Probably if he made
his fee <£s he would, by this time, find plenty
willing to pay it. Even as it is, the ancient is
laying up for himself treasure upon earth at a rate
that would make many a hungry “ regular” dance
f >r joy.

“J.A.K.”: What’s the matter with call-
ing the Federal capital Australina? This name
has a significance unknown to most Australians.
About 50 years ago, when Westralia was a howling
wilderness, somebody in London acquired a por-
tion of it, and prepared maps and plans showing,
among other things, a beautiful city with splendid
public buildings, which was to be called Ans-
tralina, and was one day to be not only the metrop.
of. W.A., but the Federal capital of Australasia.
Among the many people induced thus to emigrate
to W.A. was the present Premier’s father.

Three cases of diphtheria have been re-
ported at Maryborough, Vic., all due to kissing:—
Johnny stole a sweet kiss ’ne.ith the bough,
Anri returned it again, to her grief ;
For he had the diphtheria. Now
The receiver’s as bad as the thief !

At Wodonga, Vic., the other Sunday, an
“egg offering” was held at the local Church of
England. Each worshipper took an egg with a
verse of scripture written upon it, and deposited
it in a basket provided. The egg offering totted
up to 18 dozen. By the way, what did Premier
Reid’s last “ egg offering ” realise ?

Somebody has satirised Joe Chamberlain’s
apostaey by compiling a Chamberlain Birthday-
book. Under each date appears a quotation from
Joe’s ancient Radical utterances, contrasted with
the “ Tory wisdom ”of his latter days. In 1894
he voted against a clause in the Employers’
Liability Bill which prohibited “contracting out,”
and the birthday-book records this vote as evi-
dence that Joe had changed for the worse since
Aug. 6, 1885, when he said: “I propose in my
Merchant Shipping Bill to make it impossible for
any man to contract himself out of the liability to
the owners of property, and to the persons whose
lives were at stake from the negligence of himself
or the persons whom he employed.” This novelty
deserves to be adopted in Australia. It is an easy
means of bringing political humbug under the
notice of careless people. The Bulletin would
like to introduce a Yes-No Reid Birthday-book
into every home where such things are handed
round to visitors by the dear girls.
There are-many objections to the suggested
transfer of N.S.W. Lending Library to Sydney
City Council. The library is intended for ail
N.S.W., and thousands of books are annually
sent to country members, so that its control is
quite beyond the scope of any municipal body.

Australian Methodism, divided into five
sects, has been struggling hard after federation
for seven years; but, just when it looked like
reaching the end, it discovered its own particular
George Reid. The trouble is that the Primitives,
about the meanest of the sects, have kept their
ministers attached to an English old-age fund ;
and the bosses of the latter stand in the way. So
South Australian Methodists, a tremendously
strong force locally, «ho had resolved on union
next year, have now decided that they must hold
off for at least 12 months more. Meantime, of.
■course, the hat goes round as the inevitable way
■out of this as out of all clerical difficulties.

Instructive news item from Brisbane Tele-
graph, 17/9/’97
At South Brisbane Police-court, Mr. Augustus F.
Oatnerer, a schoolmaster, appeared on remand from Sep.
2 to answer a charge of assaulting Charlotte VVoodhead,
aged 11, pupil at defendant’s school. Mr. P. J. O’Shea
appeared for the defence. The girl told a very circum-
stantial story, hut under cross-examination she admitted
that her story was false, and that she had told her elder
sister a lie, because she wished to leave the school. After
hearing- further evid -nee, Mr. Yaldwyn, P.M., unhesitat-
ingly discharged accused.
And yet she was under eleven ! At what age do
women start lying in such matters?
Victoria and N.S.W.
being the only Australian
provinces in which divorce
may be obtained on proof of
desertion only, Melbourne
and Sydney are regarded as
hospices for matrimonially
distraught brides. But the
Courts keep a sharp eye on
them and oblige them to
prove actual domicile and
not a mere temporary resi-
dence, so that knot-slicers
need to plan their visits with
judgment.

Kelly’s wife was good-
looking—Kelly was jealous.
Kelly's wife was walking up
the main thorough fare of
Crow Plains township, ac-
companied by a beautiful
setter. Biggs, the new sport-
ing landlord of the “Hare
and Hounds,’’ standing at
his door, got quite excited
as she passed. He rushed
indoors for his hat, and fol-
lowed her closely up the
street. Mrs. Kelly stopped
at several shop windows;
Biggs did the same, taking
observations the whi 1 e.
Kelly, chatting at one of the
shop doors, keenly watched
the performance. Then, as
the lady resumed her stroll
with Biggs in pursuit, Kelly
rushed out. “ How dare you
follow my wife ! ” “ Non-
sense ! ” stammered Biggs,
“ I was admiring- ’’
“Well, how dare you admire
my wife ? ” hotly interrupted
Kelly. “Your wife! That
be d d ! I was struck on the dog,” said Biggs.

“ According to investigations made by the
authorities,” says a recent issue of the Japan
Gazette, “there are said to be over (5000 Japanese
women abroad pursuing immoral callings ; 500
are in the interior of Siberia, 350 in Vladivostok,
800 in Australia, 500 in the Straits Settlements,
300 in North and South America, 150 in India,
and 250 in Hongkong and Chinese ports. As
they are still on the increase the authorities are
keeping strict watch to prevent the departure of
such women to foreign countries,”
* * m *
“Fluffy”: Chalmers Church was in-
tensely anti-green while it lasted (some 40 years).
About two years ago a man wanted to graze a cow
on the church-lands. The price was approved,
and permission almost granted- when it turned
out that the man was a “ Cahtholic.” This caused
a reaction. A young man (Australian) suggested
that it surely didn’t matter as long as the cow
wasn’t Catholic ; whereupon a leading elder was
put up to admonish the youth for the levity of his
remark. Which he did in a 15-minute speech.
This is a fact, but you can print it as an Aborigi-
nality, if you like.
* * * *
Lord Kilmorey, who is trying to pilot an
Outer Harbor scheme through S. A. Parliament, is
a good advertiser. Last week, on a very stormy
day, he took a party of prominent men out to the
mail steamer, moored two miles off Largs Bay,
St. Vincent’s Gulf, and every one came back a
convert to Kilmorey’s harbor scheme. Several of
them were sea-sick, all were pitched about like
ninepins on the deck of the little steam-tug, and
Kilmorey himself nearly broke his neck in trans-
ferring from the mail steamer. But it was a
good “ad.”

There had been an amateur performance
of “ Hamlet ” and a Pepper’s ghost-show, both in
one week, in a Vic. up-country town. A local
magnate’s missis had been to the former, and was
afterwards asked by the doctor’s wife whether she
had been to see Pepper’s ghost. “ No,” she said,
“ but I went to see Hamilton’s ghost.”

“ Bargo ” : Noticeable that the religious
trend to the suburbs. Have just removed after
two years’ residence from the Illawarra line. The
weight and thickness of the Sabbath atmosphere
was overpowering. I attribute the suburban

The Hagey Institute at Auck-
land (M.L.) has closed up, and the
props are, or were, for sale by auction.
As for the cured drunks who paid
<£2s each to be Hageyed—well, most
of their names are, presumably, to be
found as usual on the list of persons
prohibited from obtaining drink. No
doubt a few people were cured by
Hagey because they believed in it
just as people are really cured by the
Holy Coat or the miraculous shrine
at Lourdes for the same reason. Al-
most every nervous disease can be
cured by almost anything, if the
patient only believes hard enough ;
and the big Murray cod or the lone
tree on the Old Man Plain could
work miracles under the right con-
ditions. But for the drunk who
doesn’t believe there is no pati nb
sudden remedy. And the Australian
is a casual cuss with few active
beliefs.

ment, therefore, unless a man has first shown
cause. We cannot send a man to prison if he has
no means. A man can only be committed to
prison if he has means and refuses to pay.” Which
almost reconciles one to everything else Wes-

THE CHRISTIAN ENDEAVOURERS.
A DAY OF PLAY AND PRAYER.
ON THE BEACH AT SAMDRINGHAM AND BLACK ROCK
A DAMP ENDING.
“From Sandringham to Black Kook along the sea
shore, and in the scrub, the ‘Endeavour’ Christians
gathered on Saturday for their great festival of play and
prayer. It was above everything a day of feasting and
recreation, but all the same prayer formed no insignifi-
cant part of the programme'. There was sunrise prayer
at district cent res long before the holiday makers sel out,
forenoon prayer, noonday prayer and afternoon prayer J
then there was sundown prayer and thanksgiving prayer,
prayer before tea and after tea, prayer of thanksgiving
for safe arrival and prayer for safe return, evening prayer
and a parting prayer. The day had passed gleefully . .
when a dust tornado swept over the scene. Hats and
umbrellas flew about, pots and pans were tossed into the
air and vanished iri the sea, and every piece of shelter
was promptly rushed. The ti-tree afforded no shelter
after the first few seconds of the downpour.”— Argus,
19 9/ ’98.
The “ Endeavour” Christians gathered at the Black Bock
on the Bay
For a day of close communion interspersed with prayer
and play ; ■
And they camped beneath the ti-tree scrub that whispers
in the breeze :
“ Come you, love you one another in the shade beneath
the trees! ”
You can do just as you please,
Love or pray, for no one sees
Underneath the ti-tree blossoms that are bobbing in the
breeze.
O “ Deliver us from evil ” was the prayer that woke the
sun
On that joyful picnio morning, and they prayed it every-
one ;
Yes, they prayed for strength of spirit, for the flesh has
shaky knees
When John embraces Mary in the shade beneath the
trees:
O stop it, will you, please,
Let us play at twos-and-threes
On the sandy beach and open where there ain’t no shade
nor trees.
Arm-in-arm they waded bootless, or built houses in the
sand,
And they talked “ Endeavour” sweetly as each squeezed
the other’s hand ;
But the elders kept their eyes upon the baskets at the
camp,
For they feared that worst of petty thieves, the Christian
picnic-tramp—
They’re acquainted with his stamp,
Outside saint, and inside scamp :
So they never dare to shut their eyes when praying in
the camp.
Right merry went the picnic till a mighty storm arose —
Umbrellas, hats were blown to sea and half the ladies’
clothes ;
The rain came down in torrents as they rushed the scrub
and trees
All out of breath and hatless, with their skirts above
their knees;
Hapless Janes and sweet Maries,
Feeling very ill at ease,
Underneath the ti-tree blossoms bobbing shower-baths in
the breeze.
Vic.
