A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, September 27, 1898.
My Deab Myee—
The opening’ of N.S. W. Rowing Association’s
annual regatta gave society one chance more to air
its race clothes, and let it into the mild excitement
of some close racing. The University rolled up
strong headed by Vice-Chancellor Backhouse and
wife, but not all the blue-and-gold rosettes that
puttered m the breeze could put sufficient heart
into the academic juniors to win them a
single event. Measles got a clutch on two
and University—a new stroke
having to be found for the Sydneys on the
very morning of the raea This club, reckons
it could have won the Junior Tours if the
finishing-post had been at Abbotsford, as an-
nounced by programme, instead of down the river
at One-man Wharf. The Sobraon boys, with
young bodies and the faces of grown-up men,
rowed a laborious stroke, evidently not being used
to putting on the pace. Several people won’t for-
get that outing in a hurry. Unlucky wights in
new pale-grey or pale-green clothes, who stood on
the lower part of the steamer Lady Hampden
were covered from head to foot with wet red paint’
and many ravishing summer toilettes were ruined
in that way.
The Art Society’s annual conversazione filled
the rooms till ices melted like thin air one hot
night last week. Gatherings like these give fancy
free play, and queer specimens of drapery are
always on exhibition. What these draperies do all
the rest of the year is a puzzle, for one never sees
such frocks at any other frivols. Pansy-beds
climbed over two aesthetic heads, and every woman
related to an artist wore her hair on her neck and
pmned whole flower bushes alongside her knob.
v. ■ so^ le y musician ” gowned herself gracefully in
white foulard, a few black frocks wore their usual
air of superiority, and the rest of the assemblage
was a matter of gamboge and Prussian blue, and —
carmine, lots of carmine.
By gaslight the water-colours looked nice and
gentle, but gas is less kind to oils. Therefore
Gouli doesn t say all she could say about two par-
ticular oil paintings that haunt worse than lobsters.
One of these was a dream within a dream. A fair
moustachioed artist, with a rabbity mouth, dreams
of ome and mother in his studio, and up at the
top of the picture is a little side-show that depicts
exactly what he dreamed. I can’t remember it
all but feel sure there was an angel amongst the
other commodities. The name of a well-known
brush-wielder is attached to this conception, but
that may be a mistake. The other picture is also
a sort of very bad dream. A woman is working a
sewing machine in front of a bed of lilies, and the
head of Christ appears in the background, and a
starving baby fills up the rest of the scheme called
Consider the Lilies.” Which is all very well
y° u ca n’t help considering the sewing machine’
time*** and head o* Christ at the same
That Lancelot, ” though only a novice, should
have grown up m complete ignorance of woman-
kind and their ways, savors a little of stageland
unreality to spectators of “La Poupee.” Yet
this stageland monk has his counterpart right
here m Sydney in the person of a girl at Marrick-
ville, who has reached the age of 20 years without
once seeing a man of any age, rank, or class.
Ihe girl, who is probably only one of several, be-
longs to the one and only real order of cloistered
nuns having been placed there in babyhood. One
wonders what her impression of the male animal
would be if she were to be suddenly dumped
down in front of a football “scrimmage,” or in
think l) add ° Ck at 'Randwick. What do you
rp , l<s P ort ß‘g Life,” the new melodrama at the
lheatre Royal, is just a little too late. Race-
week would have been a better setting than seven
da 7® ] a . t ® r > when sporting life at Randwick has
lobbed the public of so many illusions and so much
coin. Horses carry the weight of this play along
l’ ar l fc hjig-, real racers, that show a
noble dishke to showing off, and little sham quad-
rupeds that move round the race-course by clock-
an ork, 1 hen there are the two feather-weights
who give women a hotly-coveted glimpse into some
ot the mysterious doings of the “ring.” Speak-
ing from a certainty that hasn’t anything at all tc
back it up, I should say that the boxing contest,
" ■ IIS 1 l S , background of naive young men
with shock hair, and its two agile towel-flappers, was
a very life-like matter, with the exception of the
tat mans flop According to the stage, everybody
faints, dies, falls down from loss of wind in ex-
actly the same attitude, stretched out like’ a pair
of tongs with the feet standing stiffly up, and the
head just enough on the side for the audience to
see the face. I have never seen anyone drop dead of
a broken heart, but have witnessed faints (and feints)
and semi-faints, and demi-semi faints, and the
subjects have always collapsed in attitudes that
defy all stage conventions; that is to say, they
have drooped and huddled up on the floor or sofa.
Miss Watson in the role of a wicked enchan-
tress, trimmed her first frock with speaking scar-
let and crowned her dark head with an effectively
brilliant scar.et toque. If only that head wouldn’t
writhe so. A little incident that may have been
unconscious realism happened over her frilly red
parasoh She dropped it, and her husband, played
by Mr Arthur Styan, merely poked it towards
her with his stick, but didn’t pick it up—a truly
realistic touch. Miss Watson’s gliding, snake-like
movements are properties more suitable to wicked
parts like Olive de Carteret than to the persecuted
heroine with the dull-brown heroine-wardrobe on
ner back.
Everybody’s favorite, Mrs. Bland Holt, seems
to get a little mixed in her dressing. Her en-
trance, in a dashing ulster and trim hard-hitter
hat, suggests a smart sportswoman rather than a
slavey in love with a, policeman. In the first scene
the audience was quite horrified to see such a smart
lady make free with the consthabulary, and
couldn’t realise her humble grade until the play
had moved on a long way. Of course slaveys do
“ dress,” but their tastes don’t run to trim ulsters
and neat hats—not in Australia, anyway. Miss
Ross suffers a little from being the daughter of a
trainer and having to dress like a country maid.
I liked her best in her white-and-yellow race-frock,
with its polonaise over yellow skirts, but I per-
severe in my disapproval of all that beautiful
stateliness in ankle-length muslins. Miss Fitz-
maunce Gill has a tear-sodden part that dies out
ot the piece suddenly, and doesn’t get any frocks
worth mentioning. Bland himself scores heavily
as an Ikey who forbears to foreclose. Everyone is
so used to Bland Holt in the character that rights
everyone else’s wrongs that it was a shock to see
him cast as a hook-nosed shent-per-shent. But,
luckily for his praiseworthy reputation as an
neroic deed-doer, this “monish” man has a soft
heart, and saves everybody wholesale. For the
rest, a murderer and a big Irish policeman don’t
explain their positions in the play very skilfully.
A quick -ch an ge artist could scarcely help being
a ladies man, but Biondi works hard for his repu-
tation. The other night, Lady Rupert Clarke and
a toney complement filled the bottom box at the
~- lv ‘ Biondi wasn’t on the stage a minute before
he spotted them, and from then till the end he
acted into that box so hard that the other side of
the theatre scarcely saw his face again all the
evening.
Local music makers are rather silent this month.
wi 1 ! 1 poison’s eoi nes a copy of the Melba
Waltz, by G. de Cairos Rego, a dreamy and
melodious series of themes that cover eight pages
and keep their virtue to the last chord. Sydney
Hoben sends a new edition of his Ave Maria,
already too well known to need comment. Annie
bummerbelle deserts waltz music for a march, the
Valkyrie (Paling and Co.), overgrown with corn-
flowers from Sid Long’s pencil. A march isn’t as
useful a musical .commodity as a waltz or a song,
but this would suit little pianists who want to add
some easy dash to their after-dinner pieces.
Poussard, who is asserted by some to have been
75 when he died, has left all his money
(L 2000) (w will to his youngest son, little Louis,
r ls ? be adopted by M. Lachaume, one
?i oussard § oldest friends. Another story is
that Poussard left the will, but omitted to sign it
and that his wife, from whom he had been
estranged, will inherit the greater share of the
little fortune. The former version is probably the
correct one.
Race week brought the bushies down to town in
scores. I overheard one burly bushie telling his
wife to “Look out! or you’ll run into that blanky
fence! This was in King-street, where she was
standing still to gaze after a wonderful passing
The lingo of the bush clings hard, but there is
something even more characteristic that gives
away a bushie at first glance. The eyes of this
strange race are quite different from city and
suburban orbs. They are always dreamy and
wide open, staring with long slow stares at one
thing at a time. Town eyes take everything in at
a glance, which is just as much a matter of prac-
tice as roller-skating and man-trapping, and other
city gambols. But the bush fixes its gaze on one
object, and has to keep it there until the object
soaks into its consciousness, then slowly and care-
fully carries its gaze away to the study of some-
thing else. Whenever I see these big clear eyes
from the bush, I smell gum-trees and hear the
crows and kookaburras, and I don’t need to look
lower down at the mouths ajar to know where
they come from.
The feeble sex’s strategy. On Circular Quay
one day a man noticed the lurchygait of a comely-
lookmg woman—she was carrying a heavy-looking
basket, and he put her stagger down to its possible
weight. As she drew near, a dude anticipated
him, but fell m, for the fair one declined his aid
with much beery pathos and tottered away with a
determined hold on that harmless but judicious
basket.
Queer thing, human nature. On the German
mail-boats nine meals a day are regular institu-
tions, passengers are allowed to sleep on deck
going through the Red Sea horrors, and everyone’s
clothes are washed on board. But all these privi-
leges are discounted by the fact that an article is
judged by its price, and that the English mail-
boats charge higher rates of passage. So it’s
never yet been quite swagger to go ’Ome per
German mail, though the line’s business is growing
by leaps and bounds.
It is said that nowhere are order and neatness
more insisted upon than in Convent schools, and
for that reason many parents send their girls to
convents for six months of their school lives The
other day a stylish miss of sixteen was taking a
singing lesson from a young and even more stylish
master when a severe-faced nun called the girl out
of the room Presently the culprit returned all
blushes with an unsightly stocking on each
shoulder. Ihe stockings had been thrown on the
floor, and the nun had hit upon an artful way of
preventing that happening again. In a certain
boys school m Sydney also is discipline curiously
enforced. Iwo boys caught lighting the other
day, were stood on the platform for an hour
durmg which time they were made to kiss each
other every five minutes. They owned to feeling
pretty sick afterwards, especially when some of
the boys who had seen “ Ma Mie Rosette ” asked,
“How do you like kissing, Jones ? Is it as nice as
roley-poley pudding ? ”
Sarah Grand backs the New Woman against the
old one with furious certainty in a late magazine,
and quotes a woman who hiked in bloomers, and
hurried home to put her baby to bed and get her
husband s tea. She seems to think that this won-
derful creature entirely clinches the argument in
favor of the New Woman. But the Old Woman is
good for more than that. A bush trooper, up Quean-
beyan way, called at a farm-house, the other day,
and found the house-mother seated on a chair
with one foot in a bucket. “ Good morning,
sargmt, excuse me rising. Ye see I’m just
mendm ’is coat—and nursin’ the baby—and
readin the paper—and makin’ a bit of butter, ”
ar fd she peacefully scraped the cream off her foot
with her hand between each explanation.
For once, one of the latter order of beings has
been found not far away when “ dewty ” called. A
Robert ” was sent to a local hospital with an
attack of measles. On arrival was ordered to bed.
He objected to an “ able-bodied man like himself
being put to bed with a choild’s complaint,” but
had to go. Separated from the measles tents by a
net wire fence were the scarlet fever tents. Here a
child was just recovering from the fever. Its father
rolled up one evening, and demanded his child.
Was refused. Waited about, coaxed the child
to him, lifted it over the fence and made off.
Nurse ran and informed ‘ ‘ Robert, ” who sprang
U P> got into his clothes and soon brought back the
man and child. He was then ordered ignomini-
ously back to bed. “ What! go back to bed when
Oim capable av performing me dewty. No, Oi
won’t! ” And he didn’t.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“lo that lecture in the Town Hall on Glad-
stone came Ma Madden, in her yellow-satin cape,
with prim little daughter Leslie, in a blend of
blue and black. Janet Lady Clarke wore sad-
color, as usual, and a jet tiara, but a glimpse of
arm-and-neck flesh-tints blushing through black
lace gave promise of brighter things to come. The
Acting-Governor, in the chair, talked lashins of
flowery Irish twaddle in that rich, fruity brogue
of his. M.L.C. Nick Fitzgerald in his turn
sounded equally Paddyish m voice and vein. Rev.
Marshall, -Scotch as marmalade, was succeeded by
Rabbi Abraham, elaborating his big words and
letting his little ones take care of themselves, pro-
nouncing to^ 1 ter, ’ and otherwise cockneyish. The
only pure English accent on the premises was the
property of C. L. Andrews, the Australian-born
lecturer, who officiated in aid of Prevention of
Cruelty to Children. Bridegroom Vallange, a
shade plumper than heretofore, was back in aide-
de-camp trappings again, unlike attache Neville,
who still revels in his fast-fleeting freedom from
gold-braid and buttons.
“ The Acting Gov.’s letters are just as buttery
and diffuse as his talk. One wouldn’t mind bet-
ting he helped his Better-Half to write her pub-
lished appeal for the Children’s Hospital. There
were sentences in it as Irish as potatoes, not alto-
gether because of ‘bulls, ’ but of blarney. Remem-
ber that rhetorical outburst something to this
effect : “ What mother but has felt paralysed with
dread by the danger of her little infant? ”
One Ivan A. Rosenblum, of Melbourne, is
seeking subscribers to his novel, ‘ Be true to Thy-
self, ’ dedicated to ‘ the Administrator of the
Govt.’ His Ex.’s acceptance of the honor is a
splendid testimony to the book’s moral character.
When first ‘approached’ on the subject he wrote
to the author in a doubtful spirit—‘ One who con-
sents to have a book dedicated to him becomes in
some degree sponsor for its matter, and as I do not
know what may be the purport, tone, or object of
yours I would like to be informed as to these
tilings before giving the consent which you so
kindly ask. It is, as you will well understand,
very necessary for a Judge and Governor (salute !)
to be extremely cautious as to what he may consent
to associate himself with.’
John Madden, in his great dual capacity, felt
bound to Be True to Himseii, hence his request for
full particulars. The information proved to be
satisfactory. A week later he wrote, ‘the precis
of your book involves, if I may say so, a robust
and wholesome text which will ensure that the
literary vehicle of it will not be worked out in a
fashion unbecoming our loyalty to our fellow colo-
nists, or offending the prudent view of social
propriety, which a large portion of our community
still rigorously insists on, notwithstanding the
varied assortment of shocks to it which the modern
novel so persistently gives. It will afford me
much pleasure to have the fa vor of youi dedica-
tion. Very truly yours, John Madden.’ God
have the Queen and Mrs. Grundy !
“Awhile ago, Hon. Mrs. Freeman Thomas
set the V ic. police in motion, and worried all
hands on board the Manapouri, concerning a dia-
mond necklet, which the d. eventually turned up,
in ambush, among the soapsuds in the lady’s
cabin.
“ Anon, Mrs. Premier Turner, after a Guvment
Ouse ball, carefully hid her family baubles, and
couldn t think where they had got to (with the
usual result of taking the detectives off duty to
hunt for them), until the lost property gave itself
away by sj larkling in an unconsidered, dark corner.
, “Quite recently there was a similar case on
board a coasting steamer, where a lady raised the
old hysterical cry of diamond robbery. More
Vic. detectives were called in, and another un-
pleasant period of searching enquiry and suspicion
was terminated by the captain taking the job in
hand, whereupon the valuables were found at the
bottom of a box belonging to the lady. If the
police continue to be called in by emotional
feminines every time these latter lose their dear
little heads we shall presently have them sent
for to unravel the mystery of somebody’s silk
stockings, which unaccountably got behind the
chest of drawers when they ought to have been
under the bed.
It takes a woman to carefully describe the
dreadful caddishness of another woman under the
impression that she is dilating on a splendid
virtue. An account of the character of the mur-
dered Empress of Austria is published by one
who has enjoyed T.he inestimable privilege of
forming part of her immediate circle.” The
writer describes how on one occasion the Empress,
m the heyday of her beauty, made a young noble-
man fall madly in love with her and then punished
him. She danced with him at a ball; she let him
take her out in the moonlight on to a scented ter-
race, where she stood before him with huge
emeralds “glittering in her hair an 1 bosom,”
' VI tli her INarcissus-like fairness ” and her large
brilliant eyes “looking so abnormally large in her
delicate face.” Having thus tempted the young
nobleman till the inevitable happened, and he put
‘‘out his arms to clasp her to his heart,” she had
him exiled by order of the Kaiser, “to whom she
related the incident.” And this is actually told
as an example of the late Empress’ splendid
character !
“Re that big law-suit, the masterful trustee, it
seems, had all along dealt out their own money
liberally enough to his wards when he approved of
their proceedings. But he granted or withheld
supplies just as he thought fit, which naturally
palled on the rightful owners as years went on.
Not a bad sort of old dad, perhaps, with vast con-
fidence in himself as a father bound to judge best
for his children. But when a pretty young step-
mother came into the concern ‘ the boys ’ kicked
harder than ever.
“Recent disclosures have set fortune-hunters
smacking their lips at the thought of that dear
little heiress, an engaging creature still in her
teens, the one ewe-lamb of the litigant flock. The
poor innocent is at peace with pa and all the
world, her young stepmother included. The
latter, by the bye, is a smooth-haired charmer
addicted before marriage to Sunday-school classes
and seamen’s concerts.

“Ada Reeve, changing her frocks fast and fre-
quently at her farewell matinee, looked as well in
none of them as in the discarded brown velvet
bloomers of times agone. Fact is, our old favor-
ites among her costumes have lost their freshness,
and the new ones fall below the standard of her
pristine sartorial triumphs.
“Of late, instead of smart, snappy Parisian
street-suits and hats, better than fashion-books to
beholders, her fancy has strayed to hackneyed
pinafore-frocks after the style of those stage inno-
cents, ‘ Baby What’s-her-names,’with touzled curls
at one end and lots of adult tan-clad foot and leg
at t’other. Just as well she’s going ’Ome, maybe,
if only to give her wardrobe a London pick-me-up.
Ada’s parting spoil was plentiful—a cab-load, m
fact, when removal-time came. A garlanded rush-
chair, a big portrait of herself on an easel fes-
tooned with daffodils, and lots of smaller tributes,
including the blossom from the palpitating button-
hole of ex-Little Minister Cecil Ward. Later on,
when Ada tossed farewell bouquets to her favor-
ites while singing ‘Only a Penny,’ a return
rosebud found its way to his quondam rever-
ence. The recipient politely relinquished his
prize to a lady and never got another, though
not for want of trying. Lesly Thomson,
convalescent presumably, took part with the
beneficiaire in a little thing of his own called
‘Parted.’ Ada, as a nicely-behaved ball-room
belle in yellow evening frock, scarcely any ankle
showing, and no opportunity for naughty innuendo,
seemed rather like spice going a-begging, or caviare
sandwich wasted on Sunday-school guzzlers. The
two young things fell out, you know, and they
climbed on chairs to inspect one another over a
screen, and rubbed noses surprisedly across the
top, and so on. They were given to witty, cynical
remarks too, about matrimony and sich, even in
their most tender moments. In fact their words
were much older than their looks.

During one week recently there died in
Kiama (N.S.W.) district four women, whose ages
totalled 367 years. One was 97, another 95, a
third 92, and the fourth 83.
In 42 years of Responsible Government in
N.S. Wales only two barristers, Martin and Reid,
have attained the Premiership, while in Victoria
seven lawyers have reached that dignity.

With prayer he won his way.
He took the world between his hands
As it were potter’s clay.
Speaker Abbott is said to be the best
amateur orchid-cultivator in N.S.W. Had he gone
to live in London, as was likely some time ago, this
hobby would have endeared him to Joe Chamber-
lain.

portrait in oils of the mueh-talked-of Paris Nesbit,
Q.C., presented by the latter and accepted as a
specimen of Longstaff’s quick work. Which suffi-
ciently indicates its character.

The S.A. politician who dates back
furthest in his connection with the Parliament is
Leg. Councillor Martin, first elected for Barossa
in ’65. President Richard Baker dates from ’7O.
He was the first native to get into Parliament, the
first native to become a Minister, and the first
native to be elected President. The late Johnny
bray held the record as the first native-born
Knight, Premier and Speaker.
Upon a certain wealthy Australian’s recent
death in Australia, his wife instructed an execu-
tors’ co. to obtain letters of administration, but
she died herself before their completion, and on
going through the papers the co. discovered that
the real wife, who had been deserted for 40 years,
was in an English workhouse. But the man’s
money appeared to be fatal, for the discovered one
also died before receiving the estate.
Dear Bulletin. Your correspondent
(17/9/’9B) is astray in asserting that Phillipson,
Peter Waite’s .lieutenant, “even more than Thos.
Elder, saw the possibilities of the camel in dry
country.” In the great drought of ’64 Samuel
Stuckey, of Elder and Stuckey, was the one.to
“see the possibilities,” &c. He went to Knrra-
ehee, whence he imported to Pt. Augusta the
first lot of 100 camels, also 10 asses, and 10 Af-
ghans. —Oomberatana.
Died in Adelaide, James Scott, ex-merchant
and philanthropist, one of the founders of that
most desirable South Australian institution, the
Prisoners’ Aid Society—a body which did the
substantial part of Booth’s Prison-Gate work long
before the latter was inaugurated. Also, Mr.
Scott was for 60 years a local preacher ; but, apart
from the period, that is no distinction in S.A.,
where amateur parsons and amateur magistrates
comprise the bulk of the population.
“ S.” : The Bulletin has an affection for
the ancients. Another has just passed away in
the person of Mrs. Davidson, perhaps the oldest
woman in Australia, a few days back. She was
.102? at the time of her death, at Portland, last
week. The astonishing thing about Mrs, David-
son is the fact that she did not, just previous to
her death, walk seven miles to church. Such
extraordinary intelligence in one so old should, it
seems to me, be duly recorded in The Bulletin.

Three Australasian Premiers Seddon,
Forrest and Kingston—are 6ft. high and broad in
proportion. It was said by a Scotchman that
Scotland would be as big as England if the hills
were rolled out flat and Reid would be the tallest
Prime Minister of the crowd if he were similarly
treated.
Rumored that Premier Reid is now study-
ing French harder than ever ; as The Bulletin
has already remarked, every Agent-General should
speak French. Also, G. lI.R. quite believes that
his oratory is of a brand to captivate the House
of Commons.
Dr. W. Crooke, the brave veteran of 87
years, who is practising like a youngster of 50 in
Darlinghurst, Sydney, started life at Hobart in
the forties. He was one of the committee which
gave a send-off to Gov. Denison when he left
V.D.L. for Botany Bay. After that, the doctor
went to Melbourne and lived in Fitzroy 30 years.
Mrs. Stephen Jarvis, who died in Adelaide
lately, was the first white woman to live in the
heart of Australia. She went to Mt. Margaret
with her husband in ’62, and was at Stuart’s Creek
when Stuart was camped there on his way back
from his famous trip to the Northern Territory.
More than that, she took four children with her
in a spring-cart—the youngest of them seven
months old.

Years ago, a good-na-
tured, jolly theatrical mana-
ger, a real rough customer,
“ lent ” some of his artists
for a night to “entertain”
at a swell social club which
was giving a party. In re-
cognition, the club invited
him to dinner. On being
shown through the club, he
off handedly remarked to his
guide —a rich, fat, bluff
“ sport ’’—that they’d better
put him up for election as a
member. “ Ah,” said the
sport, “ you wouldn’t have a
dog’s show.” Brutal but
that’s the way thesport talks.
Well, presently the club was
giving a late smoke-night,
and thought to get some of
the manager’s artists to look
in and help things along
after the theatre show was
over ; so they wrote him on
the subject; of course they
were willing to pay. Manager delayed his
answer till midnight. Then he sent ’em this reply :
“ You haven’t a dog’s show.” Selah !
John Lambert, a member of the original
Cobb and co. firm, whose coaches did so much to
civilise the Australian backblocks, died lately in
his (U.S.) native town. The sole remaining mem-
ber of that first firm—J. M. Peck—is a resident of
Melbourne. The proprietary passed from the first
firm to Watson and Hewitt, and thence to Robert-
son, Wagner, Walter Hall, Rutherford, and
others. The first co. was wholly American.
Jenkinson—the smart young Q. journalist
who lately snatched the Wide Bay seat from the
Govt,—paid rather a tall price for his political
apotheosis. To liquidate election expenses he
parted with a parcel of mining scrip which has
since become worth <£lo,ooo. The irony will
come in should Jenk., who was returned at the
bye-eleetion two months ago by a bare majority,
be beaten at the approaching general election.
When the tugboat Thistle visited s.s.
Tomki to land Premier Reid at Port Macquarie,
on his recent visit, he was surprised to find no one
had come to welcome him, and he remarked : “ I
suppose they’re too busy electioneering to come
out.” He was badly “knocked” when someone
pointed out that the sole occupant of the boat,
other than the crew, was a small flathead which
some sly humorist had placed prominently on
deck.
Opposition leader Downer (S.A.), who is
not a teetotaller, recently accused Premier King-
ston of whining. “There are two kinds of
‘ wining,’ ” retorted Kingston, “ and I am
addicted to neither.” “You are advertising your
only virtue,” said Opposition chief lieutenant
Solomon. Whereupon Kingston : “ And you are
jealous because you have none to advertise. If I
could think of one of your virtues I would men-
tion it.”

Rev. Mr. Boyce has troubled himself to
calculate how much English bishop Sydney has
had in 46 years. Since 1852 the Bishops of
Sydney—Broughton, Barker, Barry and Smith
have had in all 14 years’ leave of absence.
The death of Hori Grey leaves Sir Henry
Barkly the senior living ex-Governor of an Aus-
tralian province. Grey reigned inS.A. from 1841
to 1845, and Barkly in Victoria 1856 to 1863.
Grey died at 86 years of age and Barkly is 82.

The Wallace case affords many illustra-
tions of the fatuity of the Tory theory in favor
of “ business men ” as administrators in public or
private life. The average “ business man ’’ is as
big a fool as the poet—out of his own particular
line.
Strange fact that Brown, White, Gray,
Green, and Scarlett are mostly English names,
whilst the Blacks, as a rule, are Scotch Presby-
terians with a gloomy belief in Hell. There’s
something in the color of a name. Gold, Goold,
or Gould is distinctly Jewish.
The Age assumed that because Judge
Hartley Williams is selling his furniture he means
to retire and go to England. But the announce-
ment seems to be unfounded. And, if he did
retire, Attorney-General Isaacs would not take
the Judgeship. He will not take a Judgeship
for years to come, at all events. He likes the
political game too well.
Premier Turner, whose close-fistedness
with public money is a byword, is inclined to be
generous to his own department. Govt. Printing
Office people—who worked a lot of overtime during
the Fed. Convention Melb. sittings, and were paid
time-and-a-quarter for such overtime—last week
received a bonus of =£1 each, foremen getting a
month’s wages extra, and the Govt. Printer no
less than £ 125.
Lyttelton Times (M.L.) suffers the loss of
that clever Bulletin verse-man, Liddell Kelly,
who succeeds Harry Taperell as editor of the
Wellington Times. Taperell’s speedy resignation
didn’t altogether surprise his Sydney friends.
Wellington, after all, is not a very large metro-
polis, and “Tap.” was apparently denied the free
hand in the reformation of the Times so necessary
to save its soul.
Richard Jordan, the “ champion draughts-
player of the world,” has come to Australia with
the intention of stopping here for as long as he can
turn an honest penny by the exhibition of his
small and dreary science. Many years ago James
Wyllie the “Herd Laddie,’’ worked this country
on the championship ticket, and his terms were 12
honest pennies per game. Old Wyllie, like his
young successor, Jordan, was a gloomily-silent
person who took a “ shnllin’” in ad vance from
any stranger who cared to compete with him, and
didn’t return the money if the game ended in a
draw or a defeat for himsAf. Pretty frequently
the game did end in a draw. There’s a terrible
lot of draw about draughts.. When Jordan
wrested the championship from Wyllie he won
two games out of 20 ; the old ’un took one game,
and the remaining 17 were draws. Later on Jor-
dan beat another “ champion ” named Ferric by
four games to three out of 40. Draughts, among
practical players, is simply a test of endurance,
and the man who gets least tired is the winner,
sooner or later.

The president of S.A. Wesleyan confer-
ence the other day told the assembled brethren a
story of the supernatural. On the previous
Saturday, he said, he had sat down to write to a
minister of his denomination, of whose indisposi-
tion he had just heard. He had got half-through
the note, when “something arrested his hand,
and he was unable to write. An impression
flashed through his mind that his friend was dead.
. . . When the telegram arrived shortly after-
wards announcing the sudden death, he com-
pared the time, and found that his friend”—the
Rev. J. Y. Simpson—“died at the very time he
received this strange impression.” Mr. Simpson
died at the Burra ; this occurred at Adelaide.
The places are 101 miles apart.
Dr. Fernando Gagliardi, the Melbourne
Public Librarian who recently died, was, though
a mild, genial little man, no end of a fighter in
his early days. He fought about 30 duels in his
time, and bore on his body evidences thereof.
He was the friend of Garibaldi and Mazzini, and.
once fell into the hands of the police at Pisa .as a
“suspect.” He had at the time, in his trousers
pocket, a list of subscriptions for the promotion.of
“ theuause,”• and felt, that all .was up. On his
way to the police-station, he begged the police-
men’s pardon for a moment, ran into a shop to
say “ good-bye ” to a friend behind the counter,
and thrust into the latter’s hand the tell-tale sub-
scription-list. This saved him, and he escaped
rom what threatened to be a severe trouble.

By the death of John Terence McMahon,
of G.P.O., Sydney, there passes away one
other witness of the shooting of the Duke of
Edinburgh at Clontarf in 1868. McMahon's story
of the attempt at assassination differed little from
the recorded version. Panic seems to have ruled
the Prince’s party, judgment in most cases being
conspicuous by its absence.
There seems good reason to believe that
Rougemont, the alleged cannibal chief of 30 years'
standing in North-western Australia, and the
most ridiculous Munchausen of the decade, was
a perfectly peaceful Frenchman who lived for
many years and until quite recently in Sydney
and other unromantic spots, and earned a blood-
less living by photography. It is also alleged that
he is, and has long been, badly wanted, not for
cannibalism, but for the commonplace offence of
deserting his wife. Even in his placid photo-
graphic days Rougemont (or the man who is
supposed to have been Rougemont) was full of
wondrous tales, but he romanced mostly about the
South Sea Islands in those times.

N.S.W. politics broke out in a new place
last week when Meagher, M.L.A. (with a whip)
and Norton, ex-M.L.A. (who usually carries a
pistol) got mixed up in a frantic dog-fight in Pitt-
street, Sydney, the end being that Norton took
out his gun and pursued Meagher a great distance.
The pace was tremendous while it lasted;
Meagher’s coat-tail was absolutely horizontal as
he went by, and the brief Norton showed a
surprisingly agile sprint. This makes about the
115th person who has tackled the short and stormy
editor in one way or another, and John’s luck
still stands by him. On the following day Norton,
who is an alderman, complained in the city
council of the awful amount of dust which flies
about in Pitt-street; but he said nothing about
taking steps to lay the bullets in the air.

PILSENER LAGER BIER
Aboriginalities.
Apropos the prodding of the prostrate
Arabs after the battle of Omdurman, on account
of “Fuzzy-wuzzy’s” aptitude for “shamming
dead.” The Australian blacks, especially those
of the nor’-west, are pretty good at the same trick,
Which, in their case, is more deceptive because of
their known ability to die when they feel that
way. It has its value, too ; thus doubtful hus-
bands occasionally test the depth of their spouses’
devotion by shamming death. It is cheaper than
hiring private detectives—and probably just as
ineffective as a rule.
• * * •
“L.F.H.”: Re bush morality. Knew a
selector—a single man -who at age of 55 induced
a married woman to forsake her husband and live
with him. She brought along her 12-year
daughter, and all went well for about five years,
when the old man suddenly transferred his affec-
tions to the daughter, visited the local parson,
and got hitched up. Mother is now relegated to
the kitchen, and has the honor of waiting on her
daughter and son-in-law. I myself have heard
the daughter “rounding on” her mother for not
having dinner ready at the time ordered.

Dear B.— Re shoes and harness for work-
ing bullocks, your recent correspondent is all
right (though any blacksmith can’t make them).
Lots of teams over on the Richmond have shoes
jn stony, hilly country. As to expense, however,
in shoeing, harnessing, and even feeding, the bul-
lock isn’t in it with the horse.— Mackell.
» * * •
“ Tom Collins ” : Re bullock-yoke. Whilst
honoring the humanity which prompted recent
pars, of “Raspberry” and “ Camiro,” I must
take exception to their remarks on the Australian
pattern of yoke. A life-long familiarity with
bullock-driving—including eight years’ exclusive
pursuit of that exalted occupation—has convinced
me that bullock-tackle speaks its last word in a
well-shaped yoke of Murrumbidgee or Macquarie
oak, with judiciously-set bows of 7-8 iron. The
padded collar (a large horse-collar turned upside-
down and accurately fitted to the bullock’s shoul-
ders) has been tried times without number, and
always appears a success till replaced by a yoke ;
then you will see your pair of bullocks travel like
new creatures. A pulling horse steps from the
lower shoulder, a pulling bullock from the upper
shoulder, hence the pace of the latter is fettered
by a collar but in no way impeded by a bow,
which lightly embraces the neck, merely serving
to keep the yoke down. Pulling at the extreme
strength-limit respectively, with collar and yoke,
is certainly more distressing to horses than to
bullocks. Bullocks, by the way, can start a load
that they cannot travel with, whilst horses can
travel with a load that they cannot start. Also,
the shoeing of bullocks is an extremely difficult
operation. The divided hoof alone would make it
so; but, in addition to this, the hoof is much
thinner and much harder than that of the horse.
Some bullocks suffer much at the hands of brutal
or dirty-flash drivers, and even more from un-
avoidable privation. On one occasion I dropped
20 first-class bullocks out of 28 during a single trip
of 180 miles and back. It is such homely little
tragedies as this that make life worth living
A NATURAL ASSUMPTION.
JOHNNY-COME-LATELY : “ You want me to pay you before I leave the yard. Do you think me to be
such an ass as to take it into my head to come back without the horse? ”
CANDID DEALER: “No, it ain’t that, sir; but, you see, the 'orse may take it into his 'ead to come
back without the ass!”
out-back But let humanitarians be assured
that the design of the Australian yoke is respon-
sible for no part whatever of that misery which is
the lot of most working bullocks.

He came upon
us at night-fall.
A tall, red man,
very silent, too.
Nature and cir-
cumstance had
something to do
with it. Tucker
was on the table,
and Bill handed
over his knife and
plate to the visi-
tor. The latter
nodded and
pegged a claim.
He ate largely and
drank much hot
tea. Suddenly he
cocked his head
on one side and
seemed to listen.
“ Cow bellowin’ ,”
he said. Then he
wiped his knife in
his mouth. Pre-
sently he pushed
out his fingers at
the fire and yawn-
ed under his mou-
stache. He didn’t
smoke. Bill did.
So did I. About
an hour later we
crawled into bunk.
As Bill pulled his
sack to his nose
he looked at the
visitor and said
very deliberately,
“’Taint no cow;
it’s a bull.” Next
morning we woke
and there was no
stranger. A bit of
torn newspaper lay
on the table, and
written on the mar-
gin was, “ Can’t
stop no longer in
this shop, too much
blanky argument .”

In Mareeba
(N.Q.) “boiled
mutton” is “ kid” —literally and actually.
* • • *
A pioneer squatter, who owned a station
on the N.S.W. side of the Upper Murray, rode
one day over to Vic., and when he returned at
night the river (which comes down quick there)
was a banker. It was July, and, though frost and
snow were over the flats, the old man stripped,
strapped his duds to his saddle, turned his norse
in, caught his tail, and was towed across. But
no sooner did the moke feel his feet than he broke
away, and made for home. The old boy trudged
after him, and when he reached the homestead
naked was tree’d by the dogs. The first to answer
his yells was the housemaid, who, of course, fled
forthwith, and the old man had to wait until one
of the boys came along with a stockwhip and
drove off the besiegers. Didn’t even have a cold
next day, but was up early and shot two of the
dogs.
* * • *
“E.G.T.” : The enthusiastic sportsman
who rides several miles to slay a goanna has been
immortalised, but I have seen no mention of the
equally prevalent youth who, with a very blunt
axe, patiently hacks at a very tough tree for half
a day for the sole glory of despatching a solitary
native bear. Then ? after jubilantly watching his
yelping curs demolish the unfortunate victim, he
will cheerfully set to work to chop and haul the
tree off the road. It always falls across the road—
or into somebody’3 cultivation. “ That tired
feeling” never attacks young bushie except
when requested to fetch wood or water for the
house.

“Wallaroo”: Re the woman - worker.
Know a large family, mostly girls, who travel
round taking clearing and similar jobs. The old
man superintends—looks after the fires and pots—
while his womenlcind do the hard graft. Has lost
several boys, and rather regrets that some of the
girls were not “ taken ” instead. “ Can’t get
Half the work out of a woman :” he says. Never-
theless, his girls make the local yokels dream of a
time when they will be able to stay at home and
pot-jostle while the missus works the selection.
But, so far, the old man has refused to part with
any of his slavesses.

‘ ‘ Darkie ” : “ Deuce of bobs ” given in
Bulletin, 27/8/’9B, as Western slang term for
florin. Western Queensland slang term is “ swi-
deener.” A few more W.Q. slang words. A
penny is a “Cobar,” 3d. a “traybit,” 6d. a
“zack,”ls. a “hog” or a “deener,” and 2s. a
“ swideener ; ” ill is a “funt,” .£5 is a “spinna-
ker,” and i>2s “ unlimited capital;” tobacco is
“snout,” opium “twang,” a Chinaman a “can-
ary,” and a blackfellow is a “ swatser.” In a
shearing-shed: The boss is the “finger,” the
shearers the “brutes,” the rouseabouts “leather-
necks,” “spoonbills,” “ loppies ” or “Jacks,” and
the wool-pressers “ tickjambers.” To “ ring ” the
shed or shear most sheep is to “ swing the gate ; ”
to “ carry the drum ” is to be last man in the
shed; to have a whisky is to “ oil up ” or
“jorum;” to get drunk is to get “inked;” to
shake for drinks is a “ pongello; ” to throw in Is.
all round and shake (winner to “ shout ”) is a
tambaroora ; ” to force the publican to shout is
to “strike oil;” to leave the pub. broke is to
“ take water.” When anyone has died he has
“ passed the buck; ” when he’s buried he has
“pitched his camp.”

“ T. M‘L.” : Here’s my stockman’s say-so
about the “ ’roo ’’-tail question. “No ; he
doesn’t spring from it in jumping, because the
only way you can catch him when you haven’t got
a dog is this. When you run him down, as you
gallop past you’ll see his tail sticking straight out
and up, feet off the ground, and you lean over and
grab it and throw him, and the shock stuns him
enough for you to get off and hit him on the head
with the stockwhip.” I went out with the man
shortly after, saio the thing done, and am stronger
than ever in the belief that Nature provides no
useless appendages.

“N.F.H.”: Out Longreach way I was
biking recently up a hill, and overtook a mob of
spare horses belonging to carriers ahead, and
driven by a boy who, doubtless, had never seen a
bike before. As soon as the horses saw me they
stampeded, and the boy pulled up and stared in
blank astonishment till after I had passed, when
he shouted, “Make him gallop, mister ! ”

“ P.M‘D.” : The bower-bird is a fraud—
can’t “ imitate ” worth a cent. His supposed
mimicries are his natural cries. Have heard him
cry like a cat out in the Never-Never, wherejjhere
are no cats, but never heard him imitate the dingo
or the crow, which he is constantly hearing. Even
the crow shines by comparison with the b.b.,
which, when he wants to bully a butcher-bird,
ruffles up the feathers on his neck and makes a
noise like a hail-storm. But it’s all bluff ; a wag-
tail can “ do him bad ” if it comes to fight.
* * * *
A farmer at the recent Agricultural Bureau
Congress in Adelaide adversely criticised the
average judging at shows, and, in proof of the
correctness of his strictures, stated that, a prize
being offered for five hens of a certain breed, of
which he only had four, he plucked a rooster and
otherwise “ adjusted him ” to pass for a lady, and,
to his gratified astonishment, took first prize. His
brethren crassly seemed to think that the yarn
told as much against the narrator as the judges.
* * * •
“Jeff” : Re bulldog ants. One day when
as usual the overseer of an adjoining station came
to mash the school-teacher in the dinner-hour
some of us boys set to work cutting bull-ants in
two and throwing away the tail (stinging) ends.
After we had got about 50 front (or pinching)
halves in a matchbox, one of us quietly crawled
with it under the school-floor until he came to a
knot-hole just under where the teacher sat
“spooning.” Taking the lid off the box ha
jammed the mouth of it up the hole, and in half
a minute two-score half-ants were climbing up the
unsuspecting teacher’s legs. Then the circus
started. Unearthly yells and squeals and capsiz-
ing of desks, slates, &o. Then out rushed the
overseer looking like one who had ’scaped with
his life, and, by the time ho had galloped out of
sight, all was silent in the school again. Some
whispered, “ She’s dead ”; but presently she
opened the door and sent one of us down to the
creek for some water. For months afterwards
nothing was heard of the overseer, and all the old
women cats in the neighborhood maintained that
the poor girl was only trying to shield him by
saying it was some creatures stinging her. If any-
one wanted conclusive evidence, he was referred to
“ what the children said.”
* * * *
“Werrimee”: There was for long on
Currawilla Station, Q., an old gin who crawled
about on her hands and knees. Her lord and
master had cut off her feet because of her pro-
pensity to “walk about.” Knew a case on the
Richmond in which the lady had been hamstrung
by her old man for running away, and had to
shuffle about in a sitting position, shoving her
useless legs in front of her.
* * * *
“ Cecil S.” : In the coast districts between
Sydney and Port Stephens, a “bushman” is a
timber-getter. Tried to impress upon a collier
at Catherine Hill Bay that this was only a local
appellation—and failed. Quoth I, “Don’t you
think the stockman on a N.Q. cattle-station is a
bushman ? ” Said he, “ No, if he don’t get
timber.”

“ P.H.” to The Bulletin :
The Cowra treasure-seekers’ proposed expedition re-
calls an episode of 1881. Dugald M’Vigor, squatter, of
Cooper’s Creek, Q., took a mob of fats to Sydney. On
the way down his blackboy, Jacky, a Diamantina native,
told Dugald such a circumstantial story of lumps of
yellow-, heavy stone lying about among white stones and
sometimes stuck to the white stones that Mac. became
intensely interested. The locality of the metal (evidently
gold) indicated by Jacky was on the dry range separating
lower Diamantina from lower Cooper waters, where the
minaritehy grows. The first quartz met with after
Jacky’s tale was told was at once identified by that wor-
thy as “ brother belongin’ ” to the white stones aforesaid.
Daily questioning failed to shake Jacky’s testimony, and
long ere the markets were reached Dugald indulged in
dreams of coming affluence. The cattle sold, Dugald
repaired to Orange, N.S.W., where he had friends, and
unselfishly imparted the glad tidings. Jacky was intro-
duced, and, after withstanding successfully all sorts of
questioning, had a royal time. Gold-fever spread, and
soon a party of 12 Orange district residents, accompanied
by M'Vigor and Jacky, was on its way to the promised
Eldorado. Six spring -carts carried the outfit. 800
miles of weary trudging brought the party to Keeroon -
gooloo station, on the Cooper, where a halt was
made, and where the writer encountered them. Station-
manager Coleman was taken into confidence, and it was
decided to reconnoitre. Accordingly, Coleman, M‘Vigor
and another went forward, under Jacky’s guidance, to-
wards the land of promise, now about 60 miles distant.
But, on the second night out, Jacky “cleared” from
camp, and the expedition ended. A few days were spent
in fruitless endeavors to trace Jacky, and the advance-
party returned to the main camp with the icy news.
Further search for Jacky failing, the gold quest was
abandoned, and the party sadly dispersed, some returning
home direct; others made for Mt. Browne. Jacky—a
young myall, who had never been away from his tribe
before—was unanimously voted a perjurer, and generally
a prototype of the later-day Westralian newsman. His
levanting was taken as proof (it was the only proof) of his
mendacity. Probably a just conclusion, but much remains
unexplained.
* • * *
“ Verax”: A farmer up this way (Q.)has for
barometer a barrel with open top, in which is placed
a common green frog and about 6in. of water. In
the centre of the cask stands a stick notched at
regular intervals, and about a foot out of the
water. In dry weather froggy remains in the
water, but when a change is coming he begins to
climb the mast. If but little rain is to fall he
only just leaves the water, and the heavier the
prospective fall the higher he goes, every notch
counting so many points. The proprietor of that
frog can foretell changes in the weather with
Wragge-like accuracy.
* * * *
“Tin-Lip”: Common in Fiji to see a
native woman with pet pig running behind, always
small and stunted in growth. Piggy’s mistress is
generally a childless married woman or a ditto
old maid ; latter a very rare thing in Fiji.

Hori Grey.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Horn Grey— the “Great Pro-Consul,” Maori-
land’s “Grand Old Man,” as those who loved him
well, and they were many, delighted to call him—
is dead. The ancient Knight, whose eyes, having
seen the beginning, were very weary as they
looked upon the ending of the century, has gone
to his well-earned rest. Very aged, weak and
worn ; lagging superfluous perhaps on the world’s
stage; yet what a won lerful personality and mar-
vellous career were his ! Here is no attempt to
record that career, or to essay, even in miniature,
a biography of the old man—merely to sketch, in
more or less digressive manner, the old Knight as
he appeared to one who had many opportunities of
studying him in public.
* * • *
It is not as explorer, Governor, Premier, or
politician that the dead man will chiefly be remem-
bered by Australasians in times to come. He will
be spoken of rather as truest gentleman, kindly,
courteous, humble, too, in his way—a proud
humility. His name will be recalled as that of
one who strove, often with incoherence and incon-
sistency in his methods, for what he believed to
be the good of his fellows, and for what he held,
mistakenly or the reverse, to be the right, the
noble and the true. His most remarkable attri-
bute was the fascination he exercised upon all who
came within the radius of his influence. Hostile
politicians were so cognisant of this power that
they did not resist—they fled him. Behind his
back, during more recent years, the cynics of
Maoriland mocked him. Pace to face with his
marvellous enchantment they saluted him another
CLesar, and applauded with the best. As an
active political force in the land of the Maori and
the Moa, the aged statesman —always, by the way,
a despot, even though a benevolent one —has for
long years been a spent volcano. He was at once
too broad and too narrow to make a successful
colonial politician. His metier was—King. As
the idol of the people, however, he never suffered
dethronement.
» * * *
The present scribe saw and heard Hori Grey
for the first time at a great public meeting in
Auckland 1M.L.) 12 or 13 years ago. Some dis-
HORI GREY.
Photo., J. Hubert Newman.
appointing experiences of Maoriland’s political
celebrities —reputedly golden gods, actually idols
of clay —had induced the writer to largely dis-
count m advance Grey’s fame. There were some
2000 people present, men and women. What hap-
pened was this : An old man, slight and bent,
wearing an overcoat and a great comforter, came
feebly upon the platform, and a few seconds later
most of the women were sobbing and many of the
men snivelling: nor does the writer pretend he
was free from the contagion of that extraordinary
emotion. A minute or two subsequently the old
man arose from his seat and began languidly and
tremblingly to endeavor to remove his wraps. A
dozen willing helpers rushed forward to assist.
Divested of his outer garments some of the in-
firmity seemed to fall away from him. One noted
that the veteran wore plain but well-cut clothes, a
white waistcoat, a posy in his button-hole. He
smoothed his silvery hair, took a step to the front,
and, as the plaudits again rang out, he straightened
up and looked a quarter of a century younger
than the enervated and aged man he at first
appeared. Then he began to speak ; eloquent no
doubt he was—in patches; but of consecutive and
sustained argument there was no trace. Never-
theless, the indescribable enthusiasm of the audi-
ence continued throughout, and, when that won-
derful old tongue was silent and the Knight of
Kawau had relapsed into his comforter and
apparent semi-senility, the writer found himself
cheering as vociferously as his neighbors. Of
course, almost any man of gracious and command-
ing personality, sonorous voice and flexible, and
with a great message to deliver, may thus affect
an audience. But Grey’s capital for many years
was merely the shadow of a mighty name, a thin
sweet voice and a faded presence.
* * * *
Hori Grey in-actically withdrew from active
Maoriland politics in November, 1890 ; his subse-
quent reappearances on the stage political being
hardly worth mentioning. In August of the same
year the Opposition of the ’House of Representa-
tives, of which the late John Ballance was then
leader, and Seodox, now Premier, one of the rank-
and-file, conceived the idea of employing Grey
and his popularity as instruments wherewith to
oust Harry’ Atkinson, w’hose hobnailed highloivs
seemed firmly planted in the place of power. The
astute old master, who had used so many men,
detected the device, but suffered it to go forward,
repaying the presumption of the devisers with
one of those sly but droll efforts of mischief
which evidenced that the real pathos of his
Eosition had failed to obliterate his sense of
umor—indeed a dry, fine humor was ever
one of Grey’s most delightful characteristics. On
the particular occasion under notice, a political
meeting, the first of a series, was held at Welling-
ton. Grey was, of course, the big oratorical gun ;
but Seddon, George Fisher —not long thrown
out of the Atkinson Ministry and panting for the
goreof his recentchief— Fish (Dunedin), Moss(now
or lately King Moss I. of Raratonga), and others,
were also to orate. It was arranged that Grey
was to lead off with a denunciation of Atkinson
and all his works and ways. Dear old Hori did
nothing of the kind. After a short preliminary
verbal canter, in which he touched on most things
in Heaven, earth and elsewhere, but never on
Atkinson or his Government, he suddenly de-
bouched to the subject of rabbits—and long stayed
there. “In the old days,” he said, “in the old
days long gone by, I have spoken to the people,
spoken to them in times of trouble. I have also
spoken in the days of prosperity; now in my old
age I come to speak once more. In the eye of our
Heavenly Father all are born in a measure equal. ”
That was how he began; thence he, without halt,
proceeded to the game laws of England, and
then attacked the rabbits. “There were droves of
them; some liked them —for their skins. The
rabbit had put 20 new crimes on the Statute-
book in one year. ” He then drew a blood-curdling
word-picture of a poor boy who, having shot a
rabbit, “is dragged off to a dungeon”—“or,
maybe, a girl! ” Leaving the girl immured, he
plunged into the subject of railways. “Let me
try,” he said, “even—even in my dotage” (this
with a delicious chuckle), “to make these matters
plain.” He immediately covered “ these matters, ”
whatever they may have been, with confusion
worse confounded, and when the venerable
speaker next came up (if the expression be ad-
missible) to blow, he was in the midst of the Lon-
don dockers’ strike and speaking, tears in his
voice, with real pathos and power. One sentence
was memorable. Treating of the assistance sent
to the dock-laborers from Australia, he remarked,
“And then came to those dock-laborers help—help
from their kinsmen in Australia! For years I
have cherished one idea : that the New World was
designed by Providence to save the Old World —
to save them, in spite of themselves. Federation !
Talk of Federation ! Was ever Federation such
as this seen—the New World relieving the Old?”
* * * *
The foregoing is recorded to show some of Grey’s
later political and oratorical devices, also the ex-,
tent of his popularity. Though the meeting was \
a red-hot partisan one, and though the Knight was ?
clearly expected to talk on partisan lines, and
signally failed to do so, the great audience cheered
him until hoarse, and no subsequent speaker
attracted in comparison serious attention. It was
not then —perhaps it never was—the power of
what he said, but the power of the man’s indi-
viduality that attracted and delighted.
• * » *
And the secret of that power ? Who can tell ?
Something of it doubtless fbode in name and
gentle blood ; something in venerable appearance,
in great age, in voice sweet and sympathetic, in
manner at once gracious and dignified. In latter
days, even his political foes recognised that he was
a surviving connecting link between the living
present and the dead past, that he was peculiarly
gifted with
The tender grace of a day that is dead,
and that he stood, this once vigorous man of great
achievement, aged and weak, looking wistfully
across a little intervening space to the VaJley of the
Dark Shadows; that he had gained no great
titles, honors, nor wealth, and was in posses-
sion of little that men covet and strive for—unless
it might be the love of the people.
* * * *
But though in his declining years an atmosphere
of pathos seemed to encompass him, it must not
be supposed he was always in plaintive mood.
There was frequently more than a suspicion
of drollery lurking in the corners of the ancient
gentleman’s bright blue eyes, and he had a knack
of repelling undue familiarity and of setting back
a foe quite inimitable. There is a story, perhaps
told before in print, which illustrates his humor
and his stately mode. An old pious politician and
antagonist, about to leave Maoriland, approached
the Knight to tender his farewell. “ I will not
say good-bye, but au revoir, Sir George. We are
both old men : we cannot expect to see each other
again on earth —we shall meet in Heaven.” “I
think not, Mr. Z. ; I—l hope not, good-bye ! ” Sir
George was strongly opposed to the late Harry
Atkinson, and occasionally; during the course of a
debate in the House, would, without the faintest
relevance to the subject under discussion, arise
and pour out a tremendous, but elegant, philippic
upon the “Major’s” devoted head, pointing
his declamatory periods with threatening fore-
finger, ever and anon demanding, in accents-
vibrating with loftiest indignation, “ Let the
honorable gentleman come off those benches—
come off those benches, sir ! ” But when Grey
had finished he would beam with large satisfaction
upon the grinning House, as one who triumphantly
asked—“ What d’ye think of that, eh?” At-
kinson, the hotheaded, bore these chastisements
meekly, recognising the burden of an inevitable
cross, for, whatever the Government, Sir George
was “ agen it.”
The only politician the writer ever heard insult
the veteran was Robert Stout. The incident
occurred 11 years ago. The Knight of Kawau had
spoken with his usual dignity, gentleness, and irre-
levance. Up sprang Stout, and attacked the
aged statesman with virulence, taunting the latter
with his old age, the loneliness of that old age, his
loss of memory, his ‘‘lost career” —even, whether
consciously or not, mimicking the other’s method
of speaking. The House, as the ungenerous tirade
proceeded, became stiller and stiller, polder and
more cold. Stout sat down amid a silence that
could be hacked with a tomahawk. Up bounded the
Hon. T: Fergus —a large Scotchman, with a voice
like an organ in a cathedral aisle. “ I have not,”
he said, in effect: “ I have never professed to have
great admiration for the politics of the venerable
member who has just been attacked with so much
coarseness and malignity; but I have, as which of
us has not, profoundest reverence for such age,
ability, and life-long service. The attack we have
j ust listened to was disgraceful—disgraceful! A
‘ lost career,’ indeed ! Let us compare the lives of
these two.” Which Fergus, the House smiling
acquiescence and admiration, did, terribly to
Stout’s disadvantage. It was a tremendous speech,
a scathing indictment of the sham and younger
Radical. Grey sat through it all, his face impene-
trable as that of the Sphinx.
* * • *
During the last dozen years or so, Grey and one
follower, seldom more, constituted a separate
party in the House. Moss, King Moss I. of the
Cook Group, was at one time the follower; then
Reece, of Christchurch; later Verrall, of the
same city; Grey’s latest henchman was W. L.
Rees, an old politician, a lawyer, and his leader’s
biographer. Young members of generous im-
pulses gravitated to the old Knight, facilely as
ducks to water. Mr. Reece, when returned, gave
his electors solemn pledges he would eschew
the dreaded tempter, the Knight of Kawau.
Maybe the latter heard of this. Anyway, when
the House met, he smiled his sweetest upon the
callow member, who immediately and finally suc-
cumbed to that baleful magic. Christchurch
voters sent their representative a new rat-trap
with an old dead rat in it; but the disciple re-
mained faithful. The whips of both parties hunted
young members from seats anywhere adjacent to
the venerable fascinator. Long ere the present
writer first saw Grey, an old Maoriland politician,
a man of wide education and experience, said to
him (the writer), “I loathe Grey’s politics; but I
was never fool enough to even speak to him in the
House if I could help it, for had he asked me to
vote against my conscience, my conscience would
gone to the devil.” /
j Yet the House of Representatives loved its
Grand Old Man—loved to hear him, whether
pathetic, sarcastic, denunciatory, or, as he occa-
sionally was, humorous. He will be seen in the
House to which he was once so constant no more.
But he will live in the memory of many as he was
wont to appear. See! a big debate is on. The
heavy artillery on either side is silent—the action
seems over. Is the battle finished? No! “Sir
George Grey ! ” The voice is the voice of
Mr. Speaker. An anxious hum circles the floor.
Strangers in the gallery, heretofore bored, dozing,
or asleep, waken, stiffen and crane over the rail.
Ladies nudge each other, smile, whisper, drop
three stitches of their crochet, and then their
crochet, and coo, “Dear old man!” The lights
seem brighter, a trifle more starch appears to
stiffen Mr. Speaker’s Geneva bands. A satisfied
throb circulates floor and galleries. The audience
settles to the play. The star actor who plays the
lead is on!
* * * *
Grey rises, as always, in his frail old man man-
ner. He fixes Mr. Speaker with a glittering eye
and straightens himself; sweeps the House with a
glance and stands erect. “ How well the darling
looks ! ” murmur the ladies. “Mr. Speaker, sir !’’
And that voice, though at first meagre and quaver-
ing, how it warms and deepens, what wondrous if
tremulous timbre it gains with every sentence;
how soon it penetrates every corner of the cham-
ber ! It marshalls the “unborn millions,” and, in
mournful march, shivering—always shivering, how-
ever hot the weather —they slink by, with naked
feet and racking coughs. The speaker’s voice
grows deeper and yet deeper; his manner more
and more tragic. ... A Minister sniggers !
There is a dreadful pause. Round comes the
virile champion of the “ unborn millions ”
all infirmity fallen from him like a gar-
ment. He quivers with righteous indignation;
forth flashes his right arm; his terrible forefinger
picks up and pillories that sniggering Minister,
holds him, lashes him with whips of scorpions,
rives him with lightnings. Stops ! Gazes at it
(not him) with scorn of scorn: thunders with the
prophet of old, “ Thou art the man ! ” and casts
the mangled remains of what was once a Minister
into a far corner—for the dogs on the city wall to
eat. Then? Well, one hardly likes to write it —
he possibly winks in the demurest manner at his
solitary henchman! He goes on. There is no
slavish following of a text, flowers of rhetoric gem
the speech without reference to text or context.
Now he pauses to answer some ejaculator or to
turn the ejaculation to profit; now interrupted by
Mr. Speaker, more often Mr. Speaker interrupt-
ing ; stopping for a moment’s converse with one
who offers a suggestion; discharging a joke or

The United Typewriter and Supplies Co’s representative on the mining fields.
rebuke, subtle and keen; back to his “unborn
millions” and, at last, to his seat. And and,
what has he said ? Said 1 we did not come for
that. ’Twas the manner of saying it we came to
see and hear, for there wa§ and is but one Sir
George Grey.
• • • *
When last the old Knight was seen in the streets
of Wellington—-nearly always in company with a
little child that loved him—almost every man he
met saluted him, and that in a country where each
man considers himself as good as any other man
and a little better. From those streets he has
vanished for evermore. Soldier, explorer, scholar,
linguist, autocratic Governor—at the end, poli-
tician ! One may wonder now, without disparage-
ment of the dead, whether this was altogether the
right rounding of a career so excellently begun, so
stirring and eventful midway, so powerful towards,
if not at, the close. There are those who say.it
was a lost career; but how could it be lost when,
at the end, the old man had such unique hold upon
the love and reverence of his fellow-men ? Truly,
not lost; at worst, only a little marred. And
now he sleeps! May he sleep well—without dole-
ful dreams I
J. EVISON.
The “Break up of the Drought ” picture
in this issue derives a melancholy interest from
the fate of the artist, poor Jack Commins, who
the other day shot himself in a Sydney hotel. Com-
mins, an English-educated Victorian, and a fine
fellow all-round, had recently been travelling for
a big wool-firm, and want of success appears to
have fatally preyed upon a temperament more
sensitive and high-strung than was to have been
expected in a man of Commins’s hearty disposition
and athletic physique.
“ One who knows him ” gives The Bul-
letin this estimate of Glassey, M.P., Q. Labor-
leader, and now Opposition-leader :
Few men are better known—by reputation—to political
students in Australia than Labor-leader Thomas Glassey,
the man who has just undertaken the task of leading the
“ Constitutional Opposition ” in Mongrelia’s Legislature.
Bulletin readers are familiar with the Bulletin’s esti-
mate of Glassey as a politician. His paralysing verbosity,
lack of subtlety and humor, pushfulness, amazing egotism,
inordinate self-confidence, and imperial audacity, have
done much to alienate the sympathy of the more thought-
ful and sensitive workers in the Q. Democratic movement.
But the critic who takes Glassey for a garrulous nonentity
and pig-headed ass doesn’t know his subject. Born into
the most body-destroying of occupations and brain-wither-
ing penury, he was very near manhood before he
denew his alphabet, and all that he knows—no mean
stock of knowledge—he has taught himself between the
Q. LABOR OPPOSITION-LEADER GLASSEY.
brief intervals of long, exacting, and fatiguing work.
Similar early experience leads most men to drink, gaol,
squalid domestio life, irresponsibility—moral, mental,
and physical wrecks before their prime. Glassey has
come through his fierce struggle with broken health, a
fair education, a respectable conscience, and the personal
esteem of all who know him. The new leader of Q. Oppo-
sition is clearly out of his place, and perhaps the worst
that can justly be said against him is that he doesn’t
know it. A wise man shuns situations that lead to odious
comparisons—against himself. Anyhow, Glassey is only a
stop-gap leader, and may come to see the absurdity and
difficulty of his position. He is a very effective platform
propagandist, but a signal failure as a leading parliamen-
tarian. The writer hopes he will not undo his splendid
organisation and agilation work by continuing to occupy
a parliamentary position for which he is by training and
temperament unfitted.

Thefirst hotday broughta bigboomin Lager
Bier business to Messrs. Morgan & Smith, of Syd-
ney. On Sept. 21 the firm sold -lOOcases, representing
22,000 bottles of the famous Franz Josef Pilsener
Lager. A lager boom is just what is needed to

The attention of the woolgrowers of
N.S.W. and Q. is directed to page 21 of this issue,
whereon appears a joint manifesto from the Aus-
tralasian Mortgage and Agency Co. Ltd., Dalgety
and Co. Ltd., Goldsbrough Mort anu Co. Ltd.,
and the New Zealand Loan and Mercantile Agency
Co. Ltd. This manifesto institutes a new develop-
ment in the Sydney woolbrokers’ charges, and
creates a big division in the Woolbrokers’ Associa-
tion. The agitation which has existed for the
past two years in the Sydney wool-market was
begun by the revolutionary scale of charges intro-
duced by the Co-operative Co. ; and the struggle
thus commenced reached its most public expression
in the recent boycott of the Co-operative Co. by
a section of the wool-buyers. Shortly after the
boycott, a compromise on the question of charges
was effected between a section of the Woolbrokers’
Association and the Co-operathe Co. ; and a
manifesto was published setting forth the terms
of this compromise. To these terms the four big
firms whose names were enumerated above take
exception. They form a body of their own, issue
a new scale of charges, and call confidently on
the woolgrowers to support them.

POLITICS, SNUFF AND A BOSS LIAR.


A rich New York lady lately left a dona-
tion to build a church on condition that her body
and bones were crunched up into mortar for the
corner-stone.
Mr. Frank Senior, who has been chemist
to the N.S. Wales Governors for many years past,
has conferred a blessing upon the citizens of his
country by discovering a wonderful cure for head-
ache. The new remedy cures instantaneously, is
very palatable, and perfectly harmless to both
ladies and gentlemen. Moreover, it is perfectly
effectual, no matter what the cause of the head-
ache may be. This cure, the result of years of
experience, can be had in 3 sizes, by post 1/4, 2/6,
3/8., in N.S.W. only. To all the other colonies
1/8, 2/8, 3/8. Beware of imitations. Only address:
Senior, chemist, 246 George-street Sydney.*

after God’s own heart?” Promptly came the
reply from the kniekered son of a lately-elected
Freetrade M.L.A. : “George Houston Reid.”

About Hori Grey.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When Gov. Gawler had run the infant province of
S. Australia into financial quicksands, a very
young man walked into Govt. House, Adelaide
and announced himself as the new Lieutenant-
Governor. I hat was the first intimation given
to Gawler of his deposition ; in those days cere-
mony was not characteristic of the Colonial Office.
The very young man was Capt. George Grey, and
his worldly wisdom had been derived from a
decade in the army and a couple of years of
hazardous exploration in Westralia. In reminis-
cence of his exploring days, Grey carried the scars
of three spear-wounds to the grave.
* * « *
Grey was always the man for a tight place. He
pulled S.A. from the quicksands ; he quelled the
Maoris in the Forties and again in the Sixties;
he broke the power of the Kaffirs. A man of this
masterful stamp had to fall out with the clerk-
lings of the red-tape offices, and Grey at last with-
drew from an atmosphere made mephitio by con-
tinuous bickering.
» * • •
When Governor at the Cape, Grey threw out
the first suggestion for a South African Federa-
tion, now the dearest of Britain’s desires in Africa.
He was rewarded with the worst of official snubs
—a recall to England. On the steamer gangway
he heard the news of his reappointment—the Cape
had become a “tight place” again. Grey, the
man of immediate decision, was off by next boat
to his seat of government. And, in the days of
Hori Grey, government did not stop at bazaar-in-
auguration.
* • * *
Grey had a touch of the eighteenth century in
his courtliness, even to the objects of his bitterest
animosity. For instance, he would never allow
himself to precede a friend through doorway or
passage. Premier John Hall, Hori’s worst politi-
cal enemy, visited England. His return was
judged by a good-natured M.H.R. to be an oppor-
tune moment for reconciliation between the
leaders. “ Sir George,” he remarked, “ here is
the Premier, just back from England.” Hall ad-
vanced with outstretched hand, but Grey only
bowed gracefully, turned on his heel, and walked
away.
* • • •
With all his desire on this occasion for recon-
ciliation, Hall was a man of far less courtly de-
meanor. He persisted one night in reading a
paper whilst Hori was addressing Parliament.
Grey claimed the Premier’s attention, but Hall’s
eyes were riveted on the printed sheet. Grey
stood in utter silence for 10 minutes ; half the
members present raised points of order. The
Speaker ruled the Tribune perfectly within his
rights in awaiting the attention of the rival leader,
and Grey continued to stand before the House,
silent, immovable, and dignified. Three-quarters
of an hour elapsed ere Hall threw aside his paper
and yawned. Grey proceeded, “As I was on the
point of remarking when I claimed the attention
of the honorable Premier It was one of the
great scenes in M.L. Parliamentary history.
♦ * » *
Studied as were his speeches in public, the old
man’s private conversation had absolutely no re-
gard for the niceties of punctuation. He would
meet a friend in the street, doff his hat, and with
an infinite savor of politeness, would say : “ Ah,
how do you do Sir Robert I listened with great
attention to your remarks in the House last night
and there is no necessity for me to say that I dis-
agree entirely with every one of them good after-
noon Sir Robert.”
* * * *
It was the ambition of Parliamentary habitues
to imitate Grey’s singular speech. The thin
tones, thrown against the roof of the mouth, were
only within the art of the best mimics, but
Amelius Smith, one of the Ministerial secretaries,
caught the manner to the life. One day, he was
repeating a characteristic phrase, amid the
laughter of three or four other officials, when
Hori entered the room. Tell-tale silence pre-
vailed whilst a document was sought out at his
request. Hori had opened the door to depart
when he turned and mischievously remarked,
“As you were saying when I interrupted, Mr.
Smith ? ”
* » * *
Like most men of his type, Grey was somewhat
shaky in his theology. In Wellington he consented
to debate a matter of Christian dogma with a
Jewish Rabbi. The Rabbi opened with a regular
fusilade of theological posers. Grey’s return fire
was somewhat evasive. “ You are a student and
a doctor of theology,” he started, gravely ; “ J am
a mere layman, and you must necessarily know
more of these things than I do. Put these ques-
tions to yourself that you have propounded. If
in your own heart you recognise that the answer
is ‘ yes,’ then, of course, it must be ‘yes.’ If, on
the other hand, the answer that occurs to you is
‘ no, ’ then lam willing to admit that ‘no ’ is the
correct answer.”
* ♦ * «
. At a time when he was an objeofc of heated pub-
lic disfavor, Governor Grey was reported to have
been drowned in fording a Maoriland river. He
heard the news from an exultant settler, who did
not know him, and added that the accident was
the best thing that could have happened. “Is
that so?” commented Hori. “ Well, it might not
be such a great thing for me, you see. I’m the
Governor.”
* * * *
Grey made many literary and art contributions
to public institutions. He bought up two copies
of every book of verse published in Maoriland—
good, bad, or worse. One copy he kept; the
other he always sent to the Auckland library. It
will be interesting to know how many valueless
volumes will be taken from their shrine and
destroyed now that Hori is no more. At any
rate, while the old man lived in the province
every M.L. versifier was certain of the sale of
three copies of his book—the two that Grey
bought, and the other that he bought himself.

Answers to Correspondents .
No notice will be taken of communications insufficiently
stamped. Name and address must appear on MS.
No liability is undertaken in any case re contribu-
tions voluntarily submitted, whether sent by post or
handed in,'and in no case will MS. be returned un-
less stamps (of any province) accompany.

suspicion as to his honesty Constant Reader : 1900 is
not a leap-year ; next leap-year is 1904. Every complete
century where the first two figures divide evenly by 4 is
a leap-year, such as 1600 and 2000, but not 1700, 1890 and
1900. The exact year consists of 365 days, some odd
hours, minutes and half-seconds, and if you call it 365
days Xmas has a tendency to shift into autumn and then
round to winter. Leap-year is a rough attempt to keep
it straight. And the omission of three leap-years in 400
years is because we don’t really want a leap-year every
four years—only about 991 leap-years every four cen-
turies. We will now have a drink N. M‘P. : Only
