Mark Twain Again.
Mr Mark Twain has said some very clever
things that have been widely repeated, and his
description of sea-sickness might be classed among
them. “At first,” he says, “you are very much
afraid you are going to die, but after a little as
vou get it worse, you are very much afraid you
are not going to die, and heartily wish you were.’
Few people could have said so much in so few
words A tale that may be of as much interest
to the average reader, though not put in so terse a
form is told by Mr. Wm. Attwood, of Rose-
street Glebe, Newcastle, who recounts his experi-
ence ’as follows: “I suffered for many years
from dyspepsia and constipation of the severest
description, and I could get no relief until I
chanced to come across Bile Beans for Bilious-
ness. I was troubled greatly with a feeling of
fulness after eating, no matter how little I
might eat. Then between my shoulders I had
a terrific pain. I had also an almost continual
pain across the bowels; so bad at times was
this that I could not bend to pull on my boots.
Want of breath was a serious trouble at all
times. Some people thought I had asthma, ihen
I was a martyr to constipation. At work or off work
I always had the same languid feeling, with the
pains either in my back or across the bottom of my
stomach. I saw several doctors ; one said I had
‘ water brush,’ and others put it all down to dys-
pepsia and indigestion, and spoke about asthma.
They gave me plenty of medicine, but I never felt
any real practical benefit from anything till I tried
the Bile Beans. I read of them in the paper ; and
a neighbor gave me two of the Beans to try. I
felt some improvement from these, and then T put
myself under a course. I used them for a number
of weeks, and now I feel as lively as ever I did,
and can get about my work with comfort. The
pains and aches I never feel now, and my brain is
clear, and my eyesight practically as good as ever
it was. No tongue can tell,” went on Mr. Attwood,
“ how grateful I feel to the makers of these Beans.”
Mark Twain might be able to tell a wittier story,
but he could not tell a truer one.
The Crow and the Pitcher.
A MORAL.
A certain crow, who had been haranguing a
ward caucus all the evening, had contracted a
large, importunate thirst.
As he was looking about to find some means of
relieving himself of this same thirst he discovered
a pitcher which contained water.
But the pitcher was very large, and the quan-
tity of water it contained was small, and reposed
at the very bottom of the pitcher.
For anatomical reasons the crow could not dip
his bill into the water, and drink from the pitcher
as it was.
Nor was there any use in trying to pour out the
water, for the crow could not lift the pitcher.
Besides, he had no cup.
But he was a wise crow, and also persistent, and
he immediately resolved himself into a committee
of the whole on ways and means and hatched out
a solution of the problem.
He picked up a pebble from many lying around
dropped it into the pitcher.
He kept on dropping pebble after pebble in the
pitcher until the.water approached near enough to
the top for him to drink, and he slaked his thirst
with ease.
What a splendid moral there is in the action of
that wise crow. Dear reader, see the moral in
our light. We are the proprietors of the famous
Bile Beans for Biliousness. So many people try
one doSe/one box, of Bile Beans and expect to be
cured of an ailment that has taken years to fasten
itself in the system. Bile Beans, if given a fair
show, will cure nearly every ailment that is the
result of a sluggish liver’s action and a weak
stomach. The cost is only 9Jd. per box, and
surely you can afford to make one, two, or three
tries if necessary to prove the moral of the crow
and the pitcher, namely, that perseverance in the
right direction tneans victory. Bile Beans perse-
vered in giving health —that great blessing that
should and could be possessed by us all if we per-
severed, and did not expect that the first few
beans would enable us to drink of the pitcher of
health. Health was not lost in a day, therefore it
cannot be regained in a casual manner.
Bushrangers Again.
The recent occurrence at Captain’s Flat served
to remind the public that the day is not yet passed
when highway-robbery can be practised with a
certain amount of impunity. A man may, even
in this enlightened period, be “ held up ” and re-
lieved of his purse, or, what is a much more
common occurrence, robbed of his health. Ail-
ments such as biliousness, indigestion, influenza,
headache, and constipation are continually taking
inoffensive citizens unawares, and Bile Beans are
the acknowledged specific for all such complaints.
This valuable medicine is too well-known in Aus-
tralia to necessitate a lengthy discourse on its
merits here. Suffice it, then, to remind the reader
that Bile Beans do cure biliousness and kindred
ailments, and, with a judicious use of this remedy,
bushranging of the description mentioned should
soon become a thing of the past.
A Fatal Accident.
One reads nearly every day of accidents result-
ing fatally through the negligence of some person
or other, yet few seem to realise that an attack of
indigestion left unchecked may prove, if not
exactly fatal, at least serious in its results. Bile
Beans for Biliousness cure indigestion, and their
use at the proper time will often prevent the possi-
bility of danger from this source. Attacks of in-
digestion in a more or less mild form are too com-
mon to dwell upon at any length. As its name
implies, this disorder is an insufficient action of
the various digestive organs, which prevents the
food being properly dissolved. In cases of this
kind the effect is twofold : first, the waste part of
the food is kept in the stomach and becomes sour
instead of being carried off ; and, secondly, the
bloodmaking or useful part of the food is kept
from performing its offices. The causes of indi-
gestion are many : insufficient liver action, over-
eating, eating too fast, and partaking of rich and
indigestible foods. Constant worry or mental
strain or lack of regularity may also cause an
attack. . The first thing necessary to cure an in-
disposition of this sort is a thorough opening of
the bowels and a complete clearing out of the
whole digestive organism : then the liver should
be stimulated to action, and regularity observed
in everything. Bile Beans for Biliousness will do
the first two of these ; the patient must attend to
the third. Chronic indigestion, of course, needs
longer treatment than a temporary attack.
The Society Debutante.
When a young girl first enters society her im-
mediate sensations are those of attainment,
triumph, and elation. In the first flush of her
social career her imagination opens for her a long
vista of pleasures, whose brightness is unsullied by
the slightest shadow of care. Soon, though, the
novelty commences to wear off, and she finds that,
far from being a life of unalloyed pleasure, society,
like everything else, has its drawbacks. The
health must be kept up, or one becomes a back
number. For work of this kind there is no
remedy on the market equal to Bile Beans. Keep-
ing the entire system in a thorough state of clean-
liness, free from all waste and impure matter,
toning up the nervous organism, purifying the
blood, and keeping the female organs in regular
and thorough working order, they achieve what
no other medicine can do, i.e., a complete mastery
over all derangements of the body arising from
over-indulgence of any sort. Late hours, rich
food, lack of exercise, &c., all bring on, more or
less, severe maladies, and then a course of Bile
Beans for Biliousness is what you need to put you
right again. Many women suffer untold miseries
through complaints peculiar to their sex, and in
the great majority of cases these disorders are due
to the presence of waste matter in the system.
Here again Bile Beans show their great worth, as
they cure all ailments of this sorb in a speedy and
thorough manner.
A Clerk ½in Trouble.
“ Leave the office this minute 1 How dare you
stay till this hour of the night! If I catch you
here after 10 o’clock again I’ll break every bone
in your body ! Don’t you know that late and
irregular hours bring on indigestion and all that
sort of thing? Get out!” The old gentleman
may have been a bit harsh, but he was quite
correct in his statements. Irregularity is the
cause of a great many disorders of the digestive
organs, such as constipation, indigestion, head-
aches, etc., and for any and all of these ailments
there is no cure so good as Bile Beans. This
remedy dates its existence from the discovery by
an American medical man of a certain vegetable
substance which has the effect in the digestive
organs of Nature’s own animal bile. Bile Beans
are revolutionising all the old methods of treat-
ment for digestive ailments.
They Were There.
In a small country town, not long ago, the
principal church found itself in urgent need
of a new supply of hymn - books, and, on
making enquiries through the post, the vestry
were informed by a certain firm of pub-
lishers that “hymn-books can be obtained at
a greatly reduced rate if we are allowed to in-
sert such advertisements as we think fit.” Eager
to make a sharp bargain, the vestry consented,
and ordered the hymn-books. The books arrived
on Christmas-eve, and were to be used for the first
time next day. On glancing rapidly through
them, the members of the vestry were overjoyed
to note that there were no advertisement pages
therein, and concluded that the publishers had
sent the wrong books. But publishers are not
built that way, as the vestry found, to its amaze-
ment, the next day, when a “ large and imposing
congregation ” rose, with a joyous Christmas feel-
ing in their hearts, and sang—
Hark ! the herald angels sing
Bile Beans are just the thing :
Peace on earth and mercy mild,
Two for man and one for child.
A Sensation at the Royal.
While the performance of “ Sporting Life ” was
in progress at the Royal Theatre, the other even-
ing a young lady sitting between two elderly
gentlemen in the dress-circle suddenly discovered
that Bile Beans for Biliousness was advertised in
the programme. She discovered that they were
an undoubted specific for indigestion, headache,
biliousness, pale and sallow complexions, debility,
and all ailments caused by defective digestion and
liver action. She also discovered that they were
very useful in hot weather for that tired feeling,
fevers, and general weakness. As an after dinner
pill Bile Beans score their greatest triumphs.
They are new to Australia, but old in America.
Their great popularity in the other parts of the
world come from the fact that true, genuine worth
is combined with cheapness, two essential points
for the success of any preparation nowadays. A
cure for ninepence half-penny certainly is not be-
yond anyone’s pocket.
Spring Cleaning.
The summer season in Australia has started
rather early and vigorously this year, and the pres-
ent hot weather, coming suddenly as it does; has an
exceedingly enervating effect on the most of us.
It is as well to remember that at this season of .all
others the human system needs to be toned-up
and put in thorough condition to withstand the
trying severity of a N.S.W. summer.. Bile Beans
for Biliousness are the very best medicine that can
be taken for this purpose, as they cleanse and
regulate the liver, an organ to which may be traced
nearly all the minor ailments, so many people suf-
fer from. If the liver is not in thorough working
order, the blood is impure, the circulation slug-
gish, the digestion impaired, the sleep disturbed,
and the complexion sallow. The consequence is
that there is a lack of energy, and the sufferer be-
comes a victim of “ that tired feeling.” To be out
of sorts this hot weather is most trying, as many
people know. Be warned, then, in time ; take
Bile Beans for Biliousness NOW, and they will
put you in a condition that will withstand the
most severe heat of the coming season. A beauti-
ful piece of musio, entitled the “ Bile Bean
March,” will be sent post free on application.

Four Barrels of Gold.
Once a man of the laboring class who had often
wished to be rich was offered all the money he
wished for if he would accept, without a murmur,
the troubles and anxieties that wealth usually
brings. Gladly the man accepted, and the gold
was delivered to him in four great barrels. He
lived the life of a prince for a few months, and
then the late hours and high living generally
began to tell, and his liver got out of order. To
make a long story short he got worse and worse,
until at last he had spent all his money in a fruit-
less search tor health. This, of course, was before
Bile Beans for Biliousness were discovered. Mr. M.
A. Roberts, of St. Vincent-street, Port Adelaide,
has a different story to tell. He says “ Some
seven or eight years ago I had my first attack of
biliousness. From that time for the next year or
two I had intermittent attacks of biliousness until
about four or five years ago, when my appetite
almost completely failed me. I removed from
Adelaide, where I had been working, to the
Port, but change was no benefit. I saw doctors,
and used many advertised ‘ remedies,’ giving
them fair trials, in the hope that they
would afford relief, but they were of no avail
whatever. During last November I was forced
to take a holiday, and for a time I felt a little
better for it, but not for long. The sickness re-
turned, and I felt utterly unfit for work. One
day a customer dropped in, and on being told
what was wrong, he immediately recommended
me to try Bile Beans for Biliousness. I was ready
to try anything, and at once obtained a box. I
can honestly say that not one week had elapsed
before I was able to enjoy my meals, a thing, as I
have said before, which had been impossible for
years. I have not had an attack of biliousness
since I started with the remedy, and I shall be
only too pleased to tell anyone who cares to know
how I suffered and how I was cured by Bile
Beans.”
This remedy will keep you in excellent health,
a state that is really worth more than four
hundred barrels of gold.

HIS APPRECIATION OF BILE BEANS
FOR BILIOUSNESS.
“ I have never felt better for years,” remarked
Mr. John Perry, a. well-known miner employed at
the Hetton Colliery, when waited upon at his
home in Albert-street, Wickham, recently by a
Newcastle Herald reporter, in reference to his
alleged recovery by the use of Bile Beans for
Biliousness. “Goodness knows,” said he, “I
have suffered enough in my time, and I am not so
ungrateful as to refuse to state publicly that which
might be of benefit to others who may be suffering
as I have suffered.”
Mr. Perry is a native of the Newcastle district,
having been bom at Lambton in 1867. For years
he resided at the Glebe, Merewether, where his
parents are still residing. Latterly he had heard
of many people who had been permanently re-
lieved by using the beans, and he was advised to
try them. He mentioned to the reporter the
names of friends of his who had been cured, but
for the present it will suffice to relate Mr. Perry’s
own case. “I have suffered,” he said, “more
than anyone imagines, though generally able to
continue at work. For years I had a bad liver,
and the doctors seemed unable to afford me any
permanent relief. Then I became a martyr to
indigestion, which at times caused me great pain,
first in the chest and then at the back under the
shoulder. While at work I often felt, intense
agony. It became almost impossible for me to
stoop to drive a drill.

Progress in Science.
Scientists tells us that in this nineteenth cen-
tury of ours about the greatest strides made in
the discovery world have been in medicine. We
have to record a discovery by an American medi-
cal man of a certain vegetable substance that has
the effect on the digestive organs of Nature's
own animal bile. This discovery is revolutionising
all the present methods of treatment for digestive
ailments. The old theory of pepsin, bismuth,
starving, purging, etc., etc., is to be done away
with. This great vegetable substance is com-
pounded with other ingredients to facilitate the
working, and to-day this remedy can be pur-
chased the world over and in Australia under the
name of Bile Beans for Biliousness. This great
remedy possesses the peculiar properties of acting
on the bowels without purging or otherwise
weakening the patient. This is because the sub-
stance that relaxes the bowels has about the same
action as the natural bile. Bile Beans are purely
vegetable, and a course of them will probably suc-
cessfully oust the most chronic cases of Piles, In-
digestion, Headache, Biliouness, Female Ail-
ments, Debility, Constipation, and a host of other
ailments that owe their origin to defective bile
flow,, assimilation, and poor digestion. These
Beans are placed on the market in a form that
anyone can take them without medical super-
vision ; and as the price is so very low, there are
few homes that cannot afford to always have a box
on the shelf for emergency. Australian Depot,

POLITICAL
POINTS
But for the reiterated persuasions of
Parkes, B. R. Wise, it is said, would have at an
early stage formally and publicly renounced poli-
tics for the rest of his life.
There are at present four parties in Q.
Leg. Assembly: the gang in possession, the
Labor-party, Drake’s faction of seven, and Sims’
crowd. Sims’ party consists of Sim and himself.
Premier Reid loudly boasted, on the s.s.
Tomki, that he had “got a wire from Young
saying, if I go up” (to interfere in the Macleay
election), “it’s a certainty.” Another dead bird
in the soup.
Whispered that when P. O. Fysh goes
to Eng. as Tasmanian Agent-General, Attorney-
General Urquhart (an ex-bank manager) will be
Treasurer, and M. J. Clarke or C. D. Hoggins
Attorney-General. Urquhart knows more about
ledgers than law-books.
At the close of ’97 Queensland’s alien
population totalled thusly Polynesians, 8344 ;
Japs, 2675; Chinese, 7795; Javanese, 476; other
Asiatics, 913. Total alien population, 20,203.
These are the official figures, but the data are very
imperfect. The Jap. is increasing by leaps and
bounds.
“Eko”: Would it not be a good idea to
give our Federal Capital a name that will keep
green some memories of the original possessors of
our land? I suggest three : Dimboola—the ab-
original name for Hades : Kumenavadrynke—an
invitation to partake of hospitality ; Bludee—a
term of endearment.
The recent Central Queensland “ wet-
sheep ” case furnishes a valuable object-lesson to
strikers. The men—who had refused to shear
sheep which they considered too wet —acted
throughout as one, and carefully avoided playing
into the enemy’s hands by the slightest disorder—
with the result that the charges were promptly
dismissed.
Interesting to note that it was Holder
(who joined Kingston as the Freetrade-Conserva-
tive ballast), to whom, as Treasurer, the work of
pushing the State Insurance Bill through S.A.
Assembly fell. Holder, who has broadened
gradually, submitted the scheme, not as an admir-
able piece of public policy, but just as an admir-
able piece of business
N.S.W. State Labor-Bureau’s annual re-
port shows fewer enrolments—and, therefore, “ a
reduction in the number of unemployed,” under
the glorious system of Freetrade, of course. But
more likely it shows an indisposition to waste
time in enrolling at a bureau whose highest
achievement in the way of work-finding is to send
men scrub-cutting, sand-shifting, and performing
less or more unnecessary work in districts where
needy Ministers are in want of votes.

The “roads-and-culverts” members in N.S.
Wales are a mere circumstance compared to
their northern neighbors. The railways demanded
by Q. M’s.L.A. total 21; cost of constructing
same, upwards of 25 millions. Even Joey Bell,
staunch member for dismal Dal by, threatens to
“ go over ” next session if his little branch line is
not forthwith constructed.
From the report of a recent speech by
Westralian Premier John Forrest
They wanted their people to be full of faith and hope
and energy, and there would be no need for fear with the
judicious expenditure of borrowed capital they would
make the country self-supporting. He said to them,
'* Don’t be afraid.”
In other words, by plenty of judicious drinking
the country is to get sober.
N.S.W. Labor-member Griffith is reported
to have received some damage on the nose and
elsewhere while trying to suppress Barton at
Macleay in the interests of ex-Pumpkin Minister
Sniith. Griffith’s anxiety to risk everything, even
his nose, on behalf of the Ministry of lawyers
which consistently refused his fellow - worker,
Suffield, the inquiry to which he was legally en-
titled, is pathetic.
From Q. Hansard :—“ It has been stated
that Hugh Nelson was a great financier. A
gentleman who could step from this side of the
House on to the Treasury Benches, remain there
eight years, go to the Jubilee celebrations, get all*
the letters in the alphabet tacked on to his name,
and then cotne back again and drop at once into a
billet of =£looo per annum, is certainly a financier,
if ever there was one.’’
Reid, Carruthers, and Want have been
specially exercised of late endeavoring to make
some insertion in the Federal Bill which will
commit the Commonwealth to Freetrade. In
1890, Carruthers solemnly pledged himself to re-
sist any general Australian policy which would
protect against England, and Reid and Want
have lately got on the same line, and are anxious
to make their position effective.
There is much sectarian bitterness at the
bottom of the political temperance movement in
Australia. The teetotal bosses are equally pro-
minent in the two L.O.L.’s (Orange Lodge and
Local Option). The Orangeman believes that the
Irish followers of the Pope write their names larger
on hotel signs, and that shutting the pubs, would
diminish the revenues of the Holy See. He is
thus agin the drink in pubs., but has a jar of his
own.
Premier Byrnes : “ The Government has
no intention of departing from the settled policy
of this country—that is, the construction of rail-
ways by the State. ... I do not say that there are
not some cases ivhere railways might be constructed
by private persons to the advantage possibly of the
whole community .” The last sentence will no
doubt be properly appreciated by the big syndi-
cate, now all but ready in Q. to put in its judicious
claim.
The N.S.W. Ministry, through Minister
Hogue, talks of introducing a Bill to make State
primary education free, but characteristically
defers the topic till next session. There is plenty
of time in this session to carry the Bill, if the
Government were seriously bent on the business.
The object in view is to take the wind out of the
sails of the Opposition, which body, if it obtains
power, will, it is well understood, go for such a
Bill without delay.
Understood that Q. Premier Byrnes, who,
at date of writing, The Bulletin regrets to hear
is very ill with bronchial pneumonia following
on measles, desires to hasten a dissolution. Should
he return with a faithful majority, he will pro-
bably drop one or two of his objectionable col-
leagues. Minister Dalrymple, who is mainly
ebullient polemics, will no doubt be the first to
fall. And his resurrection will never be recorded
in mundane annals.
“GONE FURTHER OUT.”—A sketch near Mitchell, Q.
Parry-Okeden, of Q. police, lifts up his
voice in protest against members of the “ Foorce ”
employing outside influence to block or obtain
gain promotions, &c. System is
“ demoralising,’ says Parry-O. No doubt it is—
but it must be remembered the evil filters down
from the superior ranks. What is the good of
telling Tom, Dick or Harry in the Civil Service
that he mustn’t make use of outside “ influence ”
to advance himself when he knows that his official
superiors are all busy at the game on every pos-
sible opportunity ? The juggling going on in the
Upper Ranks of Q. Civil Service at present is
a fine object-lesson to all the underlings and an
excellent antidote to Parry-Okeden’s homily.
The £21,000 courthouse at Mt. Victoria,
in Hartley electorate, is pointed to by N.S.W.
Mines Minister Cook’s admirers as a clinching
proof of his abilities as a statesman. “He got
us that!’’ they say. “No other man could have
got us more than a £lO,OOO courthouse for such a
small place! ”

N.S. Wales lottery law or Art Unions Act
is close on 50 years old, and morally as rickety and
anomalous as a measure can well be. Leg. Coun-
cillor Trickett attempted to modernise it a few
nights ago, but was badly beaten in a small House.
You can continue, therefore, to run a lottery under
certain conditions, where the prizes are works of
art, but cannot sell tickets if the works be described
or describable as “ of use or ornament.”
Barrister Drake, who for five years has
acted as nurse and tutor to Q. Parliamentary
Labor-party, has now publicly quitted his erst-
while allies. A few days ago he announced
his election as leaderof the “ Independent-party.”
At present his Parliamentary army consists of
two field-marshals, three full privates, and one
recruit. He hopes to enlist a fair number of
deserters from the Govt, and Labor parties ere
Parliament is dissolved.
Some liquor-reformers favor “time,”others
cash-compensation, for publicans abolished by law.
In the case of the slave-owners, there was both
cash and time. The owners received 46 per cent,
of the market-value of the slaves in hard cash, and
then the slaves were bound for a further period of
six years. Thus in Honduras, in 1834, a slave
was worth .£l2O. When he was emancipated the
owner got £53 cash down, and the service of the
slave was continued to him for six years longer.
At then current rate of wages, the latter service
was worth between £6O and £7O.
This is how they work matters in Banana-
land. An electoral claim was duly signed in a
certain Western electorate by a local Justice, but,
unfortunately, the magic letters J.P. after the
name were omitted. When the electoral list came
before the Bench for revision, the J.P. who had
committed the lapse was in evidence, and promptly
threw out the claim he himself had signed, wit-
nessed, and invalidated. (According to Electoral
Act, a mere informality does not invalidate a
claim.) Official utterance thereupon: “Govt,
carefully refrains from interfering with the local
Benches.”
Ex-Postmaster-General Cook’s boast of
some months ago that the N.S.W. Post and Tele-
graph Department had become a paying concern
is proved by the official State Statistics to be mere
bluff, as usual. At the time Cook spoke, the last
complete year was that up to June 1897, and for
that year the loss was £70,368. But a profit of
over £25,000 is shown by omitting payments made
by other departments on behalf of the P.O. and
leaving out all the interest on money borroived for
construction purposes. What a profit the railways
would show if all the interest on borrowed money
was similarly omitted in their case !
Proposed in Tas. Parliament recently to
introduce the M.L. clause providing that a voter’s
name stays on the electoral roll until he neglects
to vote. Whereupon Dan. Burke, M.P. for
Cressy, upended himself to yell, “ We want none
av yer New Zailand fads—Tasmania wants to
legislate for herself in her own way ; we don’t
want any new things in this colony!” Just so,
remarks a local scribe :
The triumphs of this Maoriland
Won’t lead Tas. by the aose ;
We want no prosperous flowery-land—
Tas. only wants old clothes !
Again the Q electoral laws ! Brisbane
resident, who had lived in the one house many
years, was recently served with a notice telling
him he had changed his residence. (Fact was, a
new house had gone up on the next allotment,
and, consequently, altered street numbers).
Next week, after he had explained mat-
ters, a further official note informed him he was
dead. Having essayed to disprove this to the
best of his ability, he returned home, and two
days after got another notice informing him he
was not living in his own house, and asking him
to say where he was living.
The Westralian Assembly was counted out
the other day. The members were unanimously
absent, watching- two legislators bash and pummel
each otln-r at the back of Parliament House, and
Gabriel's trumpet wouldn’t have raised a quorum
just then.
Mainly owing to the humanitarian persis-
tency of Attorney-General Urqutiart, the Wages
Attachment Bill, abolishing garnishee for
waves or salary, is likely to become law
in Tas. The measure has passed the
Assembly on three occasions, but the
size of the latest majority is likely
to influence the House of Refuse
in its favor. How the garnishee
system—-which was never in-
tended to apply to wages-—is
worked in Tasmania is dis-
closed by a statement in
Parliament that in one
township (Zeehan) 95
garnishee orders on
wages were issued in
one month !

The Bulletin is tired—tired—deadly
tired of the sympathy which the N.S.W. papers
expend on Dreyfus, convicted on hardly a shadow
of evidence, 15,000 miles away ; and their wrath
at the injustice done to him. Suffield, right at
their doors, was convicted as illegally as Dreyfus,
and on even less evidence. The N.S.W. Ministry’s
refusal to give Suffield a re hearing is quite as
shameless as the French Ministry’s savage jump-
ing on Dreyfus to make him fit into his living
grave. And the row which the N.S.W. Press so
unanimously expends on Dreyfus,' who is beyond
its help, would have got Suffield out of his grave
twice over.
Party loyalty is the sum total of the politi-
cal virtues of two-thirds of the present N.S.W.
legislators. At the recent censure-vote it was the
first and last letter of the alphabet of duty.
“ Party loyalty,” says Herbert Spencer, writing of
English politics, “ has come to be a fancied virtue
to which the real virtue of veracity is sacrificed.”
More than veracity is sacrificed in N.S.W. Assem-
bly ; in many eases party loyalty has killed the
power of coherent or intelligent thinking. Mem-
bers hear the blast of their leader, and obey with-
out understanding. The independent member is
often a rampant fraud, but the loyal party-man is
as often a brain-dead automaton.
Premier Reid’s loud allegation before the
Macleay election that Barton, because he held a
paid billet as arbitrator in a case wherein the
N.S.W. Govt, was one of the parties, couldn’t sit
in the Assembly even if elected (this although
Barton had renounced all further payment), was
a strange and fish-like election wile. Supposing
Reid believed bis own statement, the obvious
course for so politic a gentleman was to lie low
and wait. If Smith was elected, well and good.
If Smith wasn’t elected, then the process of un-
seating Barton could begin. But if Reid didn’t
believe that Barton was ineligible for an Assem-
bly seat, then the obvious course was to whoop
about his alleged disability on the chance of per-
suading some electors that it wasn’t worth while
voting for a than who couldn’t take his seat, any-
how. And Reid whooped something tremendous.
Campbell, M.L.C. of the Victorian House
of 48 Rich Landlords, has brought a good-sized
storm about his copious red ear. Campbell de-
clared : (1) that the fallen women of Melbourne
all wanted the, suffrage, whic h argued a consider-
able knowledge of the fallen Tottie on Campbell’s
part; (2) that he sent round a detective to find
out why ; (3) that the detective reported that the
Totties wanted the vote because “ they often had
M’sP. under their thumbs,” and the possession of
a vote would give them double power over these
M’sP.—Heaven only knows how. Campbell urged
the 48 Rich Landlords to reject woman suffrage
because of these alleged facts—and they did. Now
many peop’e write asking the name of the alleged
detective who supplied this remarkable informa-
tion so that h<> may be invited to give further
details. And if there ever was any detective,
which nobody believes, Campbell might certainly
produce him. But he doesn’t. Will some Vic.
M.L.C. move for a Commission to interrogate
Campbell, and his alleged detective, and his
alleged Tottie, and the other parties concerned ?
Henry Weenan, lately sentenced for fraud
upon Vic. Customs, has been taken to the lunatio
asylum. Weenan is a capmaker, and is now as.
mad as the proverbial hatter. A genuine case.
When he was being placed in the police-van, he
tried to Lite the driver, under the impression that
he was Christie, the Customs ’tec. There is, there-
fore, some method in the poor devil’s madness.


TO THE
WOOLGrEOWEES ..
OF
New South Wales & Queensland.
♦ :
Your attention has recently been directed to an agitation in Sydney regarding the
charges for selling wool in this market, and as we have deemed it necessary to adopt a
certain policy, we think it well to acquaint you with the reasons which have compelled us,
in the interests of wool-selling in this market, to differ from the decision of a majority of
the Wool-selling Brokers’ Association.
1. The Co-Operative Company, when it commenced business, gave as a reason for its existence that the only fair and
reasonable charge for selling' wool was a consolidated rate of 3 per cent., without any delivery charge to the
buyer.
2. We now assume that the Co-Operative Company, having discovered that it could not reasonably expect to continue
in business if it charged that rate of commission alone, has, in combination with a certain section of the Syd-
ney Wool-selling Brokers’ Association, agreed to increase its charge by Is. 6d. per bale to you as growers, and
in addition thereto to make a delivery charge of Is. 6d. per bale to the buyer, and by so doing has departed
from the line on which it sought the support of wool-growers.
3. The Co-operative Company, by now agreeing to this new scale of charges, will have great difficulty in proving that
it is acting solely in the interests of Wool growers of New South Wales and Queensland, seeing that it now
directly increases the charges by Is. 6d. per bale to those who have supported it.
4. Our chief reason for dissenting from the amended charges which the new combination has decided to institute is
that they place an unnecessary burden of Is. 6d. per bale on the Grower, whose interests it is our duty to con-
serve. We, therefore, claim the support of all Wool-growers in our opposition to this uncalled-for additional
charge.
5. In order that there may be no misunderstanding as to our scale of charges, we have to state that we have decided
to sell, for small and large growers alike, at a consolidated rate of 3 per cent, only ; charging to the buyer
one-eighth of a penny per 11). on wools realising 3fd. per lb. and over, as heretofore, and reducing the charge
to Is. 6d. per bale below that value.
We app»al with confidence to the Growers of New South Wales and Queensland to support us in
our action, feeling satisfied that the course we are following is that which will most truly conserve
their interests, and increase the importance of Sydney as a market for the sale of wool.
Australasian Mortgage and Agency Company,
Limited.

LOSING OF THE GOOD SHEDS AND
CENTRAL BOOKING OFFICE.
On MONDAY, 3rd October, the Redfern and
Alexandria Goods Sheds will be closed, but the
Goods Sheds at Darling Harbor will be open until
10.0 a.m. for the delivery of perishable traffic.
All other Goods Sheds within the limits of the
County of Cumberland—viz., to Penrith, Picton,
Hawkesbury, and Waterfall inclusive, also the
Police District of Berrima, i.e., Mittagong, Bow-
ral, and Moss Vale—will be closed except for the
receipt and delivery of perishable traffic. Goods
Sheds at other places outside these limits will be
kept open as usual.
The Central Booking Office will be open until
10.0 a.m. for the sale of tickets and delivery of
perishable parcels only.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
The partisans of Ikey Mo may like to read
the; following figures between Tote arid Tommy betting
They will allow that, irrespective of form, and, say. 10
horses start, it is 9 to 1 against any particular one
winninjr. Those are the mathematical odds, but of
course, weight of money makes books’ prices ; and fl-om
a mathematical the real pointof view—supposititious and
entirely arbitrary chances. In aforesaid field of 10 (again
irrespective of form), each animal has a l-10th chance of
winning; 10 times l-10th is 10-lOths, or 1. If Ikey Mo
chooses to Ax it, as he usually does, that each animal has
only, say, a l-9th chance ; 10 times l-9th is 10-9ths, or
1 11, which means 11 per cent, in Ikey’s favor. If he
chooses to reckon each animal has a 1-ll'th chance, which
he usually doesn’t, 10 times 1-llth is 10-llths, or -909.
which means Ikey has about 10 per cent, against him.
Having gripped the foregoing hard, Ikey’s friends will
please to keep hold of it.
Now, see how the last Metropolitan prices (S.M.
Herald s) pan out. 34 started, and the addition of the
odds conies to 1-46 or 40 per cent, to help poor Ikey to
more diamonds ! Also, it was mathematically 33 to 1
the field ; nobody expected, of course, such odds ; but Ikey
only laid on an average 22J to 1 ! Whereby he had but
a miserable 10) points to assist him towards the jewellery.
This is no isolated, fancy case; almost any race shows
similar results. Out of dozens taken haphazard only one
Selling Plate (January 8, 1898) showed fair betting, and
to even-up that bloomer, in the Park Stakes, same day
Ikey only had mathematically and appropriately Bhixtv
per shunt his way. Of course, the prices taken as a basis
are starting prices, and longer odds could be obtained
earlier in the betting, with the extra chance of a dead ’un
thrown in. Also, in ante-post betting prices rule still
longer, and then scratched-horse chances are added to
those of the dead.
~ N ™’J o °k at the tote’s percentage and compare
it with Ikey s ; also look at the tote’s average and do
likewise. The tote percentage never exceeds 10 per
cent and the then average odds are necessarily
mathematically fair every time. And yet there are
partisans of Ikey still at large! The tote is essentially
the people s machine; if big owners don’t like it thev
have an obvious alternative. The public’s accumulated
dollars and i-sovs. enable the big book to lay big wafers to
the big owner ; and at present there does not seem any
sound reason why the public should continue supplying
the money for the business. 3 “

A certain big Australian metallician once early
in his career heavily overlaid in his book a horse for one
of the big Spring handicaps. He had thought that it was
a cionkster and that it would go back in the betting, but
instead it shortened to first favorite. One or two friends
knew that if the favorite should win the bookmaker wa s
a ruined and dishonored man. He disappeared a good
ten minutes before the horses went out, leaving his clerk
to attend to business, and did not show up again until
the race was over and the favorite had been beaten by a
head. Years after, he admitted that he had adjourned
to a secluded part of the course with the intention of
shooting himself if the favorite got home.
» * * •
From a pamphlet recently published in Maori-
land : —“ Since racing clubs have used the totalisator no
single case of money being embezzled to put on the
machine has come before the courts, but before the intro-
duction of the totalisator it was a common thing to hear
of both men and youths embezzling to pay their losses to
bookmakers. A man will not readily commit a robbery
to provide himself with means to wager with, but, having
bet on credit and lost , the temptation to dishonesty is
very great. It should be remembered that the law pro-
hibits minors from putting money oh the machine, and,
as everything is done in broad daylight, the law in this
respect has to be, and is, respected. . . . The totalisator
is the most socialistic innovation of the dav. The auto-
matic machine serves all alike. Under the system of
booking bets, the wealthy sportsman could always get
much longer odds than his poorer fellows ; that is to say
ne could get his amusement at a cheaper rate. In other
words, the man who obtained amusement in having a
little interest in a race, but could only afford to risk, say,
sov ®|’ ei ff n > was always compelled to take a shorter price
than the wealthy sportsman could obtain. But the auto-
matic machine treats peer and peasant alike, which is the
essence of democracy. In the one case, the odds are
arranged by the betting-ring, but in the other they are
declared by the figures on the machine, which express
absolutely the votes of the people. . . . The tote is only
open on the course; ‘settling day’is before, not after,
the event; and a man as a rule can only lose just as much
money as he took out with him. The public are thus not
likely to invest beyond their means. No one, be it re-
membered, is invited or tempted to invest. It is an abso-
lutely voluntary system of obtaining a pecuniary interest
on a race; and, taken as a whole, it is a very healthy
form of excitement. It may be true that here and there
an individual invests beyond his means ; but that is not a
fair test; as well denounce the whole of the clerical call-
ing because one parson turns out to be a black sheep. It
would be interesting to learn how many of those who
profess to be opposed to this so-called iniquitous practice
of the turf have lost money through gambling in gold-
mines. For every one man who has got into trouble over
the totalisator, fully one hundred have come to grief in
gold-mine and share speculations.”

The erratic Fitzroy footballers carried off Yic.
premiership on Saturday by beating Essendon fairly and
fbnarely by 5 goals to 3, or 38 points to 23 altogether.
While Essendon has gone through the season steadily,
Fitzroy has succumbed twice to inferior clubs, and even
•? Ur weeks back nobody would have given sixpence for
its chance of premiership. At the critical time, how-
ever, the Fitzroys played up and beat successively Col-
lingwood, Geelong, and Essendon, which, bv the way, is
the “naicest” Melb. kicking-club and is understood to
have been at some period in the misty past composed of
only youths possessing 16 or more quarterings on their
escutcheons. Of late, however, Essendon has deigned
nwnber among its cracks sundry “Tossers” and
Mickos ” and “ Bunchies ” who dally with the pick and
hod on week days.
* * * *
Vic. football during the late season has been
free from the ugly rumors of metallic influence which
have been so current during past years, yet on one or
two occasions the playing of a few men—not more than
three in one big* team was such as to arouse suspicion in
a close and experienced observer. Apparently so hard,
it is the easiest, thing in the world to “ run stiff” in Aus-
tralian football; a man may play his very best, and yet
be working into the hands of the other side bv the simple
process of doing a little foul play at a critical moment,
the bouth-East Melb., say, are pressing on the North-West’s
goal, and “Gouger” O’Brady, the'eminentN.W. follower,
wtm has been playing the game of the day, just gives the
b.E. s crack goal-kicker a shove behind in a scramble.
There are cries of “ Hard luck, Gouger”; the umpire
awards the unerring shot a free kick just in front of goal,
and the game is altered on the spot.

Stoddart’s team having licked another All Eng-
land Eleven in an autumn test-match, there can be no
more complaining that the “Old Country” wasn’t
properly represented in Australia last year. The men
who came out here will have to provide most of the
flower of English cricket’’for next year’s test matches,,
and, if the Australians at ’Ome don’t come out on top in.
99 it will be about time to drop the Anglo-Australian,
cricket question. If the batting and bowling which
triumphed over Stoddart and co. at this end of the
world is to fail in competition with the same men’s bowi-
hatting at the other end, each side can ascribe
its defeats to the climate or the wicket, or the difference
between three-day matches and matches that start with,
an unlimited time before them. Barring Rhodes of
Yorkshire, no important new light came into All England
cricket this season. Rhodes looks dangerous. He has a
boss bowling average and he can generally make a few

Dear Bulletin.— He the sad demise of “Jim”'
Tooher as the result of an accident whilst racing on the
Sydney Cricket Ground and your remarks thereon!
<24/9/ 98.) You are a bit rough on the Cricket
track, w hich is a lot safer to race on than the Agricul-
tural ditto on. account of the high banking, as witness
the spills which have occurred on the two. The Ag. with
its flat corners has been responsible for most of the
smashes that have happened in Sydney, and is likely to-
be the scene of the largest cycle holocaust on record
some day. I would rather race any time on the Associa-
non track, and so would any racing cyclist who has
ridden in competition on the two. By the wav Mel-
bourne, with alt its cycle racing, cannot find a track to
compare favorably with either of Sydney’s.—Leaguite

The Atacama Case.
The Atacama case, in which The Bulletin was
sued by Mr. M. C. Cowlishaw, a Sydney ship-
owner, ended on 21st Sept., after 17 days’ trial,
before Chief Justice Darley and a special jury of
twelve, in N.B.W. Supreme Court. Mr. Cowli-
shaw was registered owner of the Atacama, a
at V ? ssel * of 1572 tons > which left Newcastle,
To'no W, ’ J m , a very doubtful condition, on Jan. 29,
and foundered, a few days out from port
with a loss of thirteen lives. The Bulletin com-
mented on the facts, and urged the Marine Board
to make thorough enquiry, and Mr. Cowlishaw
claimed .£2OOO damages for alleged libel. The
jury gave a verdict for The Bulletin, and added
that the article complained of did not apply to Mr.
Cowlishaw. The evidence in the action, the
longest and most costly of its kind ever tried in
JN.B. Wales, has been so fully reported in the
daily press that The Bulletin does not
propose to bore its readers with a rehash.
Enough, for the present, to say that the
Atacama case has rung the knell of the effete
and wrongly-constituted N.S.W. Marine Board
and that mainly by reason of the publicity
afforded by this monumental lawsuit to many
grave shipping abuses, the day is now near at
no w en no . sa, lor will go to sea from any
N .o. W. port without having his life safeguarded
at least as carefully as are those of passengers. For
the rest, The Bulletin may here remark that had
Mr. Cowlishaw in the first instance, instead of
issuing a writ for libel, asked it to disclaim having
sought to attribute to him any direct personal
knowledge of the condition in which the Atacama
went to sea, it would have gladly done so, even
while denying his right to legally challenge the
justice of the article complained of.
Sydney D.T. reports Colonel Picquart, of
Dreyfus-case notoriety, as “ allowed to see neither
his friends nor his lawyers.” That “ very proper
distinction ” again.
Cabled that, speaking at Stettin, the Ger-
man Emperor remarked that the future of Ger-
many lies on the water. “Water” must be a
misprint for lager-beer.

Premier Reid, to the electors of Port
Macquarie : “ I, as Premier, have no right to be
in office if Mr. Barton wins this battle.” And
surely Treachery George won’t retain an office
where he has no right to it 1
The Bulletin sincerely thanks the many
hundreds of sympathisers who have congratulated
it on the verdict in the Atacama case, and asks
every individual— you, in fact—to accept this
paragraph in lieu of more formal acknowledgment.
The Bankers’ Institute of Australasia ad-
vertises in this issue a benefit and insurance
scheme, for the assistance of members and their
families, which is well worth the consideration of
all persons connected with the banking profession.


A Samaritan nun who died the other day
in Sydney was, some dozen years ago, prominently
before the public in connection with the girl
Hicks, whose story led to the Mount Rennie holo-
caust. The nuns, be it said to the credit of their
perception, didn’t believe in Miss Hicks.
Old Charles Ryan, the octogenarian
father of “ Plevna ” Ryan and Mrs. Ellis Rowan,
who died the other day at Melbourne, was present
at the capture of “Captain’’ Gepp and his gang
in the Plenty Ranges in ’55. Ryan was following
the late Peter Snodgrass, who was in charge of
the pursuing party, when he fell in with Gepp
and party, who promptly made him prisoner.
Begging for a drink, Gepp produced a bottle of
spirits, and on Ryan supplying a handsomely-
chased hunting knife to open it, the bushranger
shoved the weapon into his own pocket with a
grin. This act afterwards led to his being
hanged, for during the fight which took place—
Ryan being first released by the gang—Gepp was
struck on the breast by a bullet which hit the
knife and glanced aside. “Only for that,’’ said
the ’ranger to Ryan while in gaol awaiting execu-
tion, “ I would have been shot like a man instead
of being hanged like a dog.'’

The Boston Shower.
When one is hot and dry as summer dust, a
shower-bath is the most welcome form of refresh-
ment possible—if obtainable. But even in our
city homes this is not always available, while it
is not too much to say that away back in the
country many thousands of would-be cleanly
people know of no shower save that which is
Heaven-sent, when
The hooded clouds, like friars,
Tell their beads in drops of rain.
The lack of a good water-supply, want of space,
and, not least, the expense of fitting up a room,
are some of the causes preventing the general
possession of what is both a luxury and a necessity
—a good shower-bath.
There are few difficulties, however, which
modern ingenuity cannot overcome, and we give
an illustration of a cheap, compact and satisfac-
tory arrangement, by means of which anyone—be
he an isolated selector or a city man in cramped
quarters—Gan enjoy to the full all the healthy
comfort of a good bath. The old-established mer-
chants of Wynyard Lane, Sydney, and Flinders
Lane, Melbourne, Messrs. M. MOSS & CO., are
the proprietors of the “ Boston Shower.” It can
be fixed by anyone anywhere in a few minutes,
and the pump connected with it, worked with the
greatest ease, gives a continuous downpour, having
a force sufficient to throw water 150 ft. A single
bucket of water is am pie for one or two most satis-
factory showers. The simplicity and small cost
(375. fid.) of the Boston will appeal to all excepting
those who do not need a bath, and of such we
may say, “Every one is as God made him —and
sometimes a good deal worse.”

INCLUSION OF BENEFIT AND ASSUR-
ANCE FUNDS.
rj*HE Committee of management propose
that the following funds be Incorpor-
ated and established in connection with the
Institute :
(1) A Mortuary Benefit Fund.— -To
assure a sum payable at a member’s death to
whomsoever he may appoint as his beneficiary.
(2) A Widows’ and Orphans’ Fund.—
For the assistance of the necessitous widows and
orphans of members.
(3) A Loss of Employment Indemnity
Fund. —To make certain fixed weekly allowances
of money to support members out of employment.
(4) A Permanent Relief Fund, styled the
Comrades’ Help Fund, for the relief chiefly by
loan of necessitous banking men who are aged, or
incapacitated from earning a subsistence, or
unable to find employment.
(5) A Fund for Scholarships to be com-
peted for by children of members.

Lord Berkeley often bragged that no single
highwayman could stick him up. One did, and
reminded him of the boast. Berkeley : “ Why,
you blackguard, there’s your companion skulking
behind ! ” The fellow turned and Berkeley shot
him through the head.
If lady-doctors have their uses, they have
also their disadvantages. A Melb. citizen who
has for some time had a popular lady-medico as
family attendant, recently rang up said lady’s
principal male rival at dead of night—“ Addition
to family ! ” “ Contrary to professional etiquette
for me to attend. Why don’t you go to your regu-
lar doctoress ? ” “I did,” was the reply, “and—
she’s doing the same thing 1 ”

Tasmanian railway general-manager Back
lives next door to a recently-established China-
man’s garden. The other day the railway man
cheerily exchanged greetings over the fence with
his new neighbour. “ Good-day ; name you ? ’
asked Hong Lip. “ Oh, I’m Mr. Back,” was the
reply. “Where you work?” asked Hong.
“ Railway—manage um railway,” said Back.
“ Oh,” said the heathen with the, hoe, “supposee
you gottem welly good billet, eh ? How much?
Two tlee pong one week ? ” Back nodded.
“ T.P.” : Australasian working-men with
land enough to supply vegetables for a large
family will go without rather than grow them
themselves. Cobbett wrote that out of a 60-rod
garden he could get enough vegetables for his
family and support a cow besides. Cobbett
looked upon the use of candles as fast living, and
on tea as a dangerous luxury, but some of his
books would take the cake as helpers of men to
live.

The Ships that Won’t Go Down.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We hear a great commotion
Ji<‘ the ship that comes to grief,
That founders in mid-ocean,
Or is driven on a reef ;
Because it’s cheap and brittle
A score of sinners drown.
But. we hear mighty little
Of the ships that won’t go down.
Here’s honor to the builders—•
Tiie builders of the past;
Here’s honor to the builders
That budded ships to last ;
Here’s honor to the captain,
And honor to the crew ;
Here’s double-column head lines
To the ships that battle through.
They make a great sensation
Bout famous men that fail,
That sink a world of chances
In the city morgue or gaol,
Who drink, or blow their brains out,
Because of “ Fortune’s frown.”
But we hear far too little
Of the men who won’t go down.
Tiie world is full of trouble,
And the world is full of wrong,
But the heart of a man is noble,
And tiie heart of a man is strong !
Tl. ev say tiie sea sings dirges,
But I would say to you
That the wild wave song’s a psean
For the men that battle through.
HENRY LAWSON.

“ K.”: The best rat-trap I ever saw was a
kerosene tin, with lid cut off, quarter full of
treacle. Bought it for spraying fruit trees ; left
it m shed two nights, and caught seven large rats
m it, in a place where rats were scarce.
“ H.Gr.” : In view of the frequent
slaughter of plate-layers by trains, wouldn’t it be
a good plan to use a portable electric alarm oper-
ated upon by the wheel-flanges of the train, as
are the signals on the electric railways at the
Liverpool docks ? The flange pressure closes the
circuit and sounds the alarm, so that with a couple
of these a plate-laying gang could work in safety.

Told of Gen. Sir Redvers Buller that,
when Captain Carey (who was with the Prince
Imperial when he was killed by the Zulus) brought
the story to Buller, the latter heard him quietly
to the end and then calmly observed: “And,
then, Captain Carey, why are you alive ? ” Carey
might just as well have asked the question of
Buller!
After a long rest, the advertisement-sup
piement re-appears as an integral portion of
N.S.W. country press. A half-dozen years ago a
Melbourne daily-paper proprietary scooped in be
tween .£4OOO and .£5OOO profit in a few seasons of
this class of supplement. The country papers
found, however, that the advt.-supplement, though
offered to them for a shilling a hundred, or less,
killed the value of their own advt. space, and, one
by one, they dropped it. The effects of the lesson
seem to have worn off.
When grants of land used to be made to
immigrants in Tas. some amusing attempts at
“grab ” occurred. One man who, on arrival, had
received allotments for “ self, wife, and family,”
made application two months later for a grant for
his infant son, who, for obvious reasons, could
hardly be called a product of the soil. The official
who had to deal with the application—a serious
red-tapist, who had never been known to perpe-
trate a joke—endorsed upon it: “In my opinion
stow-aways do not come within the operation of
the Act. _ I recommend that the application be
not considered.”

The Little Solicitor.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The little township slumbered, and was very still.
The gums and wattle-shrubs and wild bush things
on the hill-slopes round about, murmured a little
sometimes when a westerly wind blew’ dust through
them, and now and again the sheep in the Warra-
gee paddocks bleated, or a laughing-jackass made
uncouth noises in his awkward bights; but, for the
most part, the mantle of The Seven Sleepers
wrapped Warragee in its somnolent folds.
Little John Garnett, who had a brass-plate on a
door in High-street with,
JOHN CROSS GARNETT,
SOLICITOR,
worked in black letters on its shiny surface, made
himself a now epigram about madness. It was
not a very brilliant one, but he had been four
months in Warragee, and, in the bitterness of his
soul, he perceived that the white stone of his door-
way kept its cleanliness, week in, week out ; the
bales of paper in the corner of his office were as
high as ever, and his ink-bill could have been
amply covered by a threepenny bit. All things
were the same as when he had come in the
ricketty coach with his hopes, and his brown port-
manteau, and the new purse his father had given
him as a parting present. The elastic-band was a
little looser than on that first day, but the loose-
ness was probably the most noticeable alteration
in anything pertaining to him.
“ Someone will drop in to-day.” He had never,
in all his one-and-twenty years, worn a remark so
threadbare as that one. But it was four months
now, and nothing even reminiscent of a client had
poked its nose inside his ever-open office door.
“What is wrong with the people?” he asked
himself, savagely.
The answer to which, as his friend Micky
O’Daly, of the Warragee Arms, pointed out to
him, was “Nothin’ is wrong wid the lot av
them. That’s what’s wrong.” Micky O’Daly
was his only comfort. Micky would tell tales to
him, as they smoked their pipes together at nights
on the inn -verandah, of strange and pleasing
things that had happened round Warragee in the
good old days. How Mrs. Kilpatrick, who had
owned more country than a man could not cover in
a week’s riding, had left a will, of which no man
living could make either end or beginning ; it was
only clear from it that she had left much money
to Ellen ; and Ellen had been the only witness of
the will, and Ellen’s husband had protested
because the law had protested ; and the law-suit
that followed, though its ending had been sure
and sharp enough, had promised to have as little
ending as the wonderful will itself. Told, too,
how Mrs. Schmidt, at the German farm, had
charged Alfie Snowden, Mary Ann Baily, and
Teresa Jones with a base, malicious and libellous
statement that she had sold to the district
black-pudding that would not bear investiga-
tion. That had been a hot case, Micky O’Daly
said. There was money in it, too, for there had
been perjury “ and all kinds ‘av things that helps
along the lawyer’s little bhill.” But best of all
was the time when Alfie Snowden, who had a
finger in every mouldy pie, little Bill Greaves, and
the two Hammawayboys were found to have been
altering the brands of many cattle, and thereby
swelling the herds of their respective fathers with
unlawful kine. All the country had gone mad
over that case, said Micky. “ Day nor night
could a man have shlape for thalking av thim
bhoys, and av fwhat was to be the ind av thim.
An’ the mothers cryin’ fit to bust, and the
fhathers shootin’ out their gold like dirt, and the
lawyer fillin’ in his pockets by shovelfuls. That
was the times for yez ! ”
And the Little Solicitor sighed, and wished the
dead past would not so utterly bury its dead.
It seemed to him that Warragee had put on a
suit of virtue that would wear as long as the
bright brass plate on his office-door.
The place had seemed to promise so well, too.
His father, who had made all arrangements about
his coming here, had painted it in glowing terms
to him, and at home the mother and the 11
brothers and sisters were waiting anxiously for
him to make their fortune. He was the eldest of
the unsophisticated lot, and he bore the burden of
many hopes upon his sturdy shoulders, and the
burden weighed on him. His cheeks began to
thin away a little under his eyes. Depression
deepened with him. He walked up and down the
High-street pavement, with gloomy, downcast
eyes, and began to loathe the sight of that white-
stoned doorstep and that shining plate.
One night tender-hearted Micky found his
lodger sitting at the end of the “Arms ” verandah
with his head in his hands, crying.
He was only 21, and there was a letter in his
pocket from his mother, asking him to send down
£5 as soon as possible to help to pay a doctor’s
bill.
“ She doesn’t know,” said the solicitor, “thatl
haven’t made five pence since I’ve been here. I
don’t known what I’m going to do.”
“ Lhook ut here,” said Mickey, greatly troubled
within himself, and not knowing at all what he
was going to say ; “ Lhook ut here, what yez must
do is this—we must make some cases for yez.” He
pondered. “ We must set some folk a-quarrelin’.
Nothin’ better (puff, puff). . . . Why, there’s the
doctors now. What d’ yez say to makin’ up a
row between them ? Nothin’ aisier. (Puff, puff.)
. . . There’s th’ poor old docthor hangin’ on
to his practus like grim death, and here’s the new
wan schemin’ all he knows to get the practus
thransferred to hisself intirely. Now, what yez
ought to do is easy enough.”
He proceeded to expound his scheme in the
fervor of his enthusiasm. The old doctor had been
27 years in Warragee, and no one had ever said
him nay till a few months back, when a sleek and
well-groomed individual had made his appearance
suddenly, and proposed to make a living out of
Warragee’s sick. His top-hat was of the shiniest—
whereas the old doctor’s was browned with age and
dust—and the letters after his name weremanyand
imposing. As well, he was young, and “ had a way
with him,” and the Sprattsof Jimmiboroo had had
him for their measles, and the priest had called him
in for his broken leg, and old James Mafferty, the
richest man in the district, would look at nobody
else since his last bout of rheumatism, and the
sore corner in the touchy old doctor’s heart was
rapidly extending itself. Micky deemed that it
wanted but a few skilful touches to turn this to a
burning fester. Those few skilful touches he pro-
posed to help little Harnett to furnish.
The Little Solicitor smiled sadly, and said it
was all no good. In fact, what was the good of
anything! “ Life is a grievous burden to bear,”
he said —he had Philip Marston’s poems in his
portmanteau.
But Micky banged his pipe thoughtfully against
the wooden railing.
“ When they’ve come to be two fightin’ cocks,
there’ll be a law-case ready-made for yez.” His
eye glowed.
A few days after, he came up the white-stoned
steps, and flung himself into the other chair in
the office.
“ I been to him,” he said, “ an’ sez I, ‘ I hurted
me thumb-jint bad, doctor; ut hurts intirely.’
(By the luck av Brovidince I screwed it in the
dray-wheel.) ‘ It aches me bad,’ sez I. ‘Let
me have the look av it,’ sez he. ‘ I’ll disbandige
ut,’ sez I ; ‘ ’tis bandiged very quare,’ sez I. An’,
sez he, ‘ did yez bandige ut visself,’ he sez. ‘ No,’
sez I; ‘ but the wan who done ut is out av town
this afternoon. They say,’l sez, ‘that the other
doctor boy has a dale av work to do, an’ he’s
runnin’ here, an’ runnin’ there all over the coun-
thry,’ sez I. ‘ Oh,’ he sez, ‘ then this is his doin’,
it it?’ sniffin’scornful round and rootin’ off the
rags and grease as if they had been most un-
elanely. ‘ Well,’ he sez, ‘ I always knew the man
was an idjit, but this beats all,’ he sez. An’ he
hild hold av my hand and looked at it tight-ways.
‘Shure,’ he sez, ‘he’s not fit to physic a cat
with the toothache. Queer thing about that
diplomy av his. Ut ought to be stuffed
down his ignorant throat,’ he sez. Thin he mum-
bled somethin’ about quackeries. ‘ What's
that, Doctor?” sez I, quick-like, not catching it.
But he sez something different to me about i-int-
ments. ‘ Quackeries-i-intmints shall I be get-
ting ? ’ sez I. But he nivver noticed. Still, the
rist of ut was there.”
He paused to draw a deep, rich breath. “ An’
there’s your case! ” he said.
“ Where ? ” said the Little Solicitor.
“ Where? ” (scornfully). “ Askin’ yez to pick it
up 1 All you’ve got to do, me boy, is to keep
alongside av the young doctor awhiles, and talk
frindly to him ; and between whiles, aisily, afther
a dacent time av conversation has illapsed, mintion
fwhat the ould doctor said av him. ‘ I always
knew he was an idjit,’ he sez; an’, ‘ Quare thing
about that diplomy of his. Ut ought to be shtujfed
down his ignorant throat.’ There’s yer libel-action
made for yez.”
A small smile began to stretch itself around the
Little Solicitor’s gloomy mouth.
“But, I say, Micky, it’s hardly the game for me
to be up to, you know. ”
“ An’ fwhy not ? ” said Micky.
“ It’s a bit dirty / ”
“ Dirrty ? Dirrty ? ” with fine scorn. “What’s
dirrtier than money? But yez’ll put ut in the
pocket av your best coat all the same / ”
It was then, for the first time, that it occurred
to the solicitor that Micky was really in earnest.
Micky’s hair was quite grey, and he had seven
children to call him dad ; but all his years and all
his responsibilities had not sered the ever-green-
ness of his youthful heart. A “lhark” was a
“lhark ” for ever, and to help a fellow-creature in
distress was irresistible to him. His wife, indeed,
had been obliged long ago to restrict his pocket
allowance.
“Now, take yer time,” he urged. “Think ut
over. Th’ ould docthor must have a sight of
munny in a shtoekin’ somewhere. Think ut
over, boy.”
And he tossed and grunted in his sleep all that
night, and worked himself into such a fever over
his plotting that he had to get up and walk on the
verandah to cool himself.
But that same night something happened to up-
set all his Machiavellian plans. It was a very
common thing, too ; but Micky had never taken
it into account before.
» ■ * *
** I must go and tell the bhoy,” he said next
morning.
He was standing in the bar-parlor, with a very
long face, considering how he would break the
news, when someone came rushing in.
It was John Cross Garnett himself.
“Micky—Micky!” he cried, “we’ll let them
be. I’ve got work to do—a lot; with money in it.
It’s come from Andrew Harrows, at Bulla Julabee.
His bank-account is simply enormous. We’ll let
your poor old doctor be, Micky. Oh, God! how
glad lam.”
His voice actually gave a little crack. Micky
looked at him a minute. His eyes were glassy,
“ Betther so,” he said. “Poorould gintleman !
Now, I’m thinkin’ ut isn’t much we would have
gotten out av him, saints presarve his soul. He
was took dead in the night with the heart, they
sez, and I’m just going to be measurin’ him for
the fit av his coffin ! Betther so!”
The Little Solicitor was far too joyous over the
turn of the wheel of his good fortune to notice the
note of disappointment that crept in under
Micky’s brogue. But it was there ; even when he
was shaking little Garnett’s hand, and saying,
“Betther so.”
“ We don’t know fwhat he might not be saved
from,” was the only cdhsolation he offered him-
self.
M. LOUISE MACK.

“ Medicus ” to The Bulletin re the matter
of leaving wounded persons in suspicious cases un-
dealt-with till arrival of police :
Last July I was called seven miles to a gun-shot case,
and found an attempted suicide in a state of collapse—
deathly cold, with pulse anyhow'. He had been left lying
on the earthen floor of a draughty stable for over three
hours, because a would-be knowing neighbor had warned
those present not to touch the poor devil till the authori-
ties arrived. It took several hours to restore normal cir-
culation.
A man with two forefingers on one hand
recently died of drink in a far western town.
That hand was the active cause of his decease.
He was a regular source of income to the local
pubs. When the cheques were not being dissi-
pated fast enough it was his business to slouch in,
and the pub-keeper would remark: “My oath;
’ere’s a bloke with five fingers on one hand.”
Most people felt called upon to ask a man with
five fingers on one hand what he would have, and
he had it so often that he died.
Dear Bulletin.— He flogging. lam sur-
prised to find your objections to the lash are purely
sentimental. Sensibility to pain in the criminal is
very blunt—at times wanting : hence the futility
of the lash as a punishment; further, it is brutal-
ising, because it sanctions brutality in the oppres-
sor and oppressed, and does not ameliorate the
anti-social conditions which the criminal evolves.
—Banool.
[Does “ Banool” really mean to assert that sensibility
to pain in the typical sexual-maniac—the sort of offender
most frequently lashed by law—is usually blunt ? The
Bulletin did not profess in those few sentences to state
all its objections to judicial torture ; it only stated the
one incontrovertible objection which, above all others,
the Democracy should keep in mind— i.e., that flogging
is, always has been, and will be while ever it endures, a
purely class punishment. The lash for the workman—
for the man in moleskin—for the man with a soft hat and
a soft head. Never for the man in frock-coat and bell-
topper. Always for the man who says “ Blankv 1 ” never
for him who says “Haw I”—Ed. B.]

Love for a Day.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Wb have been many things in our time, Jack and
I, and when we come together, in our casual,
Bohemian way, we manage to get fun out of life,
impossible to you discreet pharisees and respect-
able publicans. We are never discreet, and rarely
respectable—as you understand Respectability.
We had 10s. between us that day in Sydney,
and 10 hours in which to spend it, so we hired a
boat from an old friend and rowed away across the
harbor. The sea played like a gay child at the
knees of the hills, the sky was clear save for one
or two dainty clouds the south winds chased; and
the smart squat steamers bustled across, leaving
lines of foam and waves that rocked us as they
swelled past.
We landed at the foot of a garden, and the
house belonging to it being vacant, we climbed the
moss-grown steps to the lawn overshadowed with
lemon-trees, golden with fruit. There were beds
of violets and heliotrope, jonquils, narcissus, and
occasional roses, with lazy bees whirring towards
their centres. Leaning back, we looked across the
gay wilderness to the blue water framed in the
quivering leaves of gums and pittosporums. Over
the way were red-roofed houses, lawns and shrub-
beries ; around, the twitter of birds, and the little
murmur in the leaves which made us happy.
We talked of everything and nothing, and told
our love-affairs. You were ecstatic over an
American girl, divinely tall, with blue eyes (mine
are brown); I showed you my diamond ring in re-
turn for the American girl, both relishing the
little pangs of jealousy which gave tang and keen-
ness to the recital. Then we discussed the possi-
bility of marrying each other, as we have done so
often, and will so often do again. Pretended we
were rich, and made plans for trips to the ends of
the world, where Romance lives, in a someday
that will never come for you and me, Jack—
perhaps that is the charm of being together, that
there never will be a someday for us; the charm
of something sweet to sigh for.
What does it mean, for two young, indolent
people, when the afternoon sun glints down
through the trees and a scarlet cannia leans up to
reach it, and violets twinkle below in ecstacies of
suppressed gentle merriment ? When the yellow
jasmine casts down reckless showers of blossom
on gleaming, white marble steps, and a gay honey-
sucker dances in and out ? When in the shade,
near great ferns, velvet pansies lie, with glittering
golden eyes, and in the gums overhead there are
sudden vagrant scarlet leaves ? It meant to us to
talk of poetry and love ; and sometimes be silent
with an occasional smile into each other’s eyes.
Ai ?, w ? returned, the water was quiet, ’the
sparkles had gone ; it was falling asleep, tired of
the day. Over the city lay the veil of smoke
like the gauzy trappings of a foreign dancer, the
lights like gay sequins twinkling as she moved,
ihe sea had grown old since the morning ; it lay
below the city, quiet and full of mystery,” bearing
stately ships and fretting steamers over dark
depths jealously hidden. Far out in quiet reaches,
lights peered down inquisitively to illimitable
depths.
Up a steep street from the wharf, through the
hurrying crowd, past the big post-office looming
darkly against the starry sky, and away up
George-street to a little restaurant we knew of
old. I was glad I was well-dressed—what a
difference it makes ! You know, women when
they build castles in the air, always plan the
dresses they will wear in them—such furs, silks,
jewels, such dainty shoes and gloves. At another
table sat Cyril Yarley and his fiancee. She
was shabbily dressed. I knew Cyril, -and I
knew her worn gloves irritated him ; the limp
lace at her neck affected his appetite, and
the abjectly bad cut of her jacket made the whole
world seem squalid and profitless. Jack ! you
men are so often such snobs, even the best of you.
Cyril would not notice me—well, because. And
yet he hated that I, a little actress girl, should see
how shabby Miss Thwaites was.
We had our coffee in a cafti in the Strand, down
steep steps. We had the place to ourselves, for it
was early, and we lingered over the best coffee in
Sydney, suggesting that, in that Oriental-looking
room, the proprietors should clothe his waiting-
maids in Turkish costumes. I had been a wait-
ing-girl there myself once, before I went on the
stage, and after I had been a governess.
Then off we went to the Tivoli—the dear old
Tivoli 1 always homely and familiar; its perpetual
tree-ferns overflowing with fat, ogling girls in
tights; its .stage crowded with coquettists, or
stolid beauties in evening dress, all intensely self-
conscious—even the damsel who gazed round with
affected indifference, and proud of the fact that
her d&colleU yellow gown was the only garment
worthy of the name, the others being garbed in
some indescribable mush of pink or blue crepon,
lace, ribbon, and silk gloves.
Then Peggy Pryde came on, and Peggy took us
by storm ; we laughed till we cried, and Peggy
told us that the lodgers were a mouldy lot, and
that she had trouble with a shy lover, whom she
urged, “If you want to come in—Come in! If
you don’t—stay out. ”
. Peggy never makes a mistake ; it’s fact all the
time; she’s an actress from top to toe, by nature,
intuition, and art. Those who don’t understand
this say “ Vulgar ! ” and as they are, as a rule, the
worshippers of Wilson Barrett, it does not matter
at all; for between Wilson and Peggy lies the
fact that one is an artist, the other a hard-working
man. You know what I mean, Jack, for every
fibre of us responded to Peggy’s grip.
We laughed at and applauded other things;
turned to gaze at glorious creatures with golden
hair and purple velvet dresses, attended by heavy
beings in chesterfields and soft hats, thinking far
more of the heavy supper they would presently
consume than of the stage before them. At
family parties who loudly applauded everything
from the oldest of chestnuts to Peggy’s newest
utterance. We spared a little sympathy for the
unfortunate tenor whose part it was to sing dreary
love-songs to a fast-emptying house. Then away
with empty pockets, Peggy’s praises on my lips,
you to your lodging-house, heaven knows where,
I to the company of an Qld aunt, with good-night
to you at the gate.
A. LEO WATHE.
